Four Critiques of the Indicator Answer
Commentator raises four objections to the indicator answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical objections raised against the sufficiency of the mental indicator in definition.

Core Idea
Four objections show why the indicator answer is insufficient.

The commentator presents four specific criticisms of the earlier answer that claimed a mental indicator is needed. These objections target the necessity, sufficiency, and arrangement of the indicator in relation to differentia and property. Each objection challenges a different aspect of the previous argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the four objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify each objection marked by 'As for first', 'as for second', etc.
Pay attention to the sequence markers.
2
Understand each objection's key point
For each objection, ask: what assumption of the previous answer is being challenged? Write down the main idea of each critique.
The first objection questions the need for equality in truth; the second questions mentioning the defined; the third denies necessity of joining; the fourth denies necessity of arrangement.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these objections matter. They undermine the claim that a mental indicator is always required in definitions.
These critiques are part of a larger debate on definition theory in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد أجيب أيضاً بأنه إلخ) فيه بحث أما أولاً فلم لا يكفي المساواة في الصدق في الانتقال وأما ثانياً فذكر المحدود لم لا يكفي قرينة، فلا حاجة إلى قرينة أخرى، وأما ثالثاً فلأنه لا نسلم لزوم ضمها مع الفصل أو الخاصة حتى يتحقق التركيب وأما رابعاً فلان انضمامها معه لا يقتضي أن يكون بينهما ترتيب لم لا يكفي مجرد الانضمام من غير ملاحظة ترتيب .
His statement: 'And it was also answered that...' etc. — there is investigation in it. As for first, why is equality in truth not sufficient for transition? As for second, why is the mention of the defined not sufficient as an indicator, so there is no need for another indicator? As for third, because we do not concede the necessity of joining it with the differentia or property so that composition occurs. As for fourth, because its joining with it does not require that there be arrangement between them; why is mere joining without observing arrangement not sufficient?
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a critique by the commentator of the previous answer. He raises four objections: first, why is the mere equality in truth (musawa fi al-sidq) of the differentia and property with the defined not enough to enable transition? Second, why is mentioning the defined itself not sufficient as an indicator? Third, we do not accept that joining the indicator with the differentia or property is necessary for composition. Fourth, even if they are joined, arrangement between them is not required; mere juxtaposition without arrangement suffices.

Key Terms
مساواة في الصدق
قرينة
الفصل
الخاصة
تركيب
ترتيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that any additional element in definition requires arrangement.
Correction: The text shows mere juxtaposition may suffice; arrangement is not necessary.

Checkpoint
How many objections does the commentator raise against the answer about the indicator?
A) Two
B) Four
C) Three
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The indicator answer faces four logical challenges concerning sufficiency, necessity, composition, and arrangement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand a difficult truth, do you instinctively demand extra evidence or structure beyond what is already present? The commentator challenges the need for arrangement—where in your life have you added unnecessary complexity to what is already clear?
?
The second objection asks why the defined itself is not a sufficient indicator. Reflect on a situation where you already knew the truth but looked for external confirmation. What would it take for you to trust what is already within you?

Division of Nazar into Sahih and Fasid
Sanusi divides nazar into sound and corrupt based on leading to the sought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic classification of theoretical reasoning (nazar) into sound (sahih) and corrupt (fasid), and how the arrangement (tartib) is a necessary consequence of the two movements.

Core Idea
Nazar is sound if it leads to the sought; otherwise it is corrupt.

This lesson introduces the foundational division of nazar, on which all subsequent analysis of reasoning depends. The author distinguishes between sound reasoning that successfully attains its goal and corrupt reasoning that fails. He then draws an important logical point: the arrangement involved in reasoning is a necessary consequence of the two movements, not something added.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the division of nazar into two types. Then read the translation to confirm your understanding.
Notice that the corrupt is defined simply as the opposite of sound.
2
Identify the two types
Identify the definitions of sound and corrupt nazar. Sound nazar leads to the sought; corrupt does not.
3
Understand the necessary consequence
The passage states that arrangement is a necessary consequence of the two movements. Reflect on what this means for the definition of nazar.
This is a key logical move: the arrangement is not the essence but a correlate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : أنه يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد يقابله ]
( أنه ( أي النظر ينقسم إلى صحيح وهو الذي يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد
مطلوب نظري قليل وإذا كان كذلك، فالترتيب يكون لازماً للحركتين في التحقيق، فتعريف النظر به تعریف باللازم، فإن جوزناً التعريف باللازم الغير المحمول فذاك، وإلا حملنا الكلام على التسامح بأن المراد بالترتيب ما به الترتيب كما في تعريف الحكمة ساستمكمال النفس، أو على الاصطلاح على ذلك .
قوله : ( وتحديق العقل إلخ) حمل الشارح المعقولات على المبادي التي تقع الحركة فيها على خلاف ما نقله سابقاً، وهو الحق إذ الوجدان شاهد صدق على أنه لا يلزم لنا بعد التوجه إلى المطلوب، استحضار المبادي وتحديق النظر في مناسبتها وترتيبها فتأمل قوله : حتى يظهر لك أن هذه التعريفات كلها تعريفات باللوازم وحقيقة النظر، وحقيقة النظر هي الحركتان وأن لا نزاع
في
الحقيقة بين الفريقين
،
قوله : ( وهو الذي يؤدي إلخ) بيان للحاصل وإشارة إلى أن قوله يؤدي صفة كاشفة لا أن في
العبارة تقدير المبتدأ والموصول .
قوله : ( وتحديق العقل نحو المعقولات فتأمل مراد الشارح بالمعقولات هو المبادي، وأما مراد المصنف بها فهو المطالب لأن الكلام هناك مسوق على انتفاء الاستعانة بالمعلومات السابقة بخلافه ههنا، فالمتوجه إليه والمحدق نحوه متغايران فيما ذكر الشارح هاهنا، بخلافهما فيما نقله عن الأبهري وقد يقال : التمهيد السابق يدل على أن التحديق أيضا نحو المطلوب، وهذا يشعر بأنه نحو المبادي، وهذا هو الظاهر لكن الفرق بين التوجه إلى المطلوب وتحديق العقل نحوه، لا يخلو عن خفاء، اللهم إلا أن يحمل أحدهما على التوجه في الجملة، والآخر على التوجه التام هذا وكأن الأمر بالتأمل إشارة إلى ما دل عليه كلام المصنف من أن التفسير بالتحديق عن الغفلات لمن لا يرى النظر لاكتساب المجهولات من المعلومات ليس بمقطوع به لجواز أن يكون تعريفا باللازم، لكن الكلام في محمولية هذه اللوازم حتى يصح التعريف بها عند من لا يجوز التعريف
بالمباين.
،
قوله : (إلى) صحيح يؤدي إلى المطلوب أي يؤدي نوعه فلا يرد على تعريف الصحيح والفاسد بانتفاء الطرد والعكس، قولنا زيد حمار وكل حمار جسم، ومجرد حمل الأداء على
[The second objective: that it leads to the sought, and the corrupt counterpart.] (That it,) i.e., theoretical reasoning (al-naẓar), is divided into sound (ṣaḥīḥ) which leads to the sought, and corrupt (fāsid) which does not lead. The sought theoretical is rare; and if this is the case, then the arrangement (al-tartīb) is necessary (lāzim) for the two movements in reality, so defining nazar by it is a definition by a necessary consequence (al-lāzim). If we permit definition by a necessary consequence that is not predicated (ghayr al-maḥmūl), then that is one thing; otherwise, we interpret the statement generously meaning that by ‘arrangement’ is meant that by which there is arrangement, as in the definition of wisdom as ‘the perfection of the soul,’ or as a convention. His statement: “(And focusing the intellect, etc.)” The commentator interpreted ‘intelligibles’ (al-maʿqūlāt) as the principles that undergo motion, contrary to what he transmitted previously, and that is correct, for intuitive experience bears true witness that after directing towards the sought, we are not required to recall the principles and focus on their suitability and arrangement—so ponder his statement until it becomes clear to you that all these definitions are definitions by necessary consequences, and the reality of nazar is the two movements, and there is no dispute in reality between the two groups. His statement: “(And it leads, etc.)” is an explanation of the result and an indication that his word ‘leads’ (yuʾaddī) is a descriptive attribute, not that there is a specific subject and predicate in the expression. His statement: “(And focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, so ponder)” The commentator intended by ‘intelligibles’ the principles, whereas the author intended by them the sought things, because the discussion there was based on the negation of using previous information, unlike here. So the object towards which one directs and focuses differs in what the commentator mentioned here, unlike in what he transmitted from al-Abharī. It may be said: the previous preface indicates that focusing is also towards the sought, and this suggests it is towards the principles—and this is apparent, but the difference between directing toward the sought and focusing the intellect towards it is not without obscurity. Unless one of them is interpreted as general direction and the other as complete direction. This is as if the command to ponder is an indication of what the author’s words imply: that the interpretation of ‘focusing from inattentions’ for someone who does not see nazar as acquiring unknowns from knowns is not certain, because it is possible that it is a definition by a necessary consequence. But the subject is about the predicability (maḥmūliyya) of these necessary consequences so that definition by them is valid for those who do not permit definition by the non-predicated. His statement: “(To) sound leads to the sought, i.e., its kind leads, so it is not objected to the definition of sound and corrupt as lacking co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness, our statement ‘Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body,’ and merely carrying the applicability of leading...
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a classification of nazar (theoretical reasoning) into sound (ṣaḥīḥ) and corrupt (fāsid), and a discussion of the implications of defining nazar by arrangement (tartīb). It begins with the second objective: that nazar leads to the sought or fails to. Nazar is divided into sound, which leads to the sought, and corrupt, which does not. Then it notes that the sought theoretical is rare. Given this, the arrangement is a necessary consequence (lāzim) of the two movements in reality, so defining nazar by arrangement is a definition by a necessary consequence. The author discusses two possibilities: if one permits definition by a non-predicated necessary consequence (al-lāzim ghayr al-maḥmūl), then that is acceptable; otherwise, we must interpret 'arrangement' generously as 'that by which there is arrangement' (like the definition of wisdom), or as a conventional term. The passage then comments on several glosses: the commentator's interpretation of 'intelligibles' as principles (mabādi) rather than sought things; the clarification that 'leads' is a descriptive attribute; the distinction between directing towards the sought and focusing the intellect; and the issue of predicability of necessary consequences for definition. The final part discusses the phrase 'sound leads to the sought' meaning that its kind leads, so the definition is not invalidated by examples that lack co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness (like 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body').

Key Terms
صحيح
فاسد
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What defines sound nazar according to the passage?
A) It is well-arranged.
B) It leads to the sought.
C) It involves two movements.
D) It is rare.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is sound if it leads to the sought; the arrangement is a necessary consequence, not the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about your faith, do you assess whether your thinking actually leads you to truth, or do you settle for reasoning that feels structured but bears no fruit? Will you commit to testing every argument by its result, not its elegance?
?
The passage says the sought theoretical is rare. In your pursuit of knowledge, do you recognize how often your own thinking falls short, and does that humble you? Or do you assume your mental efforts always reach truth?
?
You often define things by their consequences—like equating the act of prayer with its essence. How might confusing a necessary consequence with an essence distort your understanding of worship itself? Will you strive to distinguish the two?

intermediate

Definition by Necessary Consequence: Permissibility and Interpretation
Sanusi discusses the permissibility of definition by non-predicated necessary consequence and offers interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over defining nazar by a non-predicated necessary consequence and the alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
Defining nazar by arrangement requires either permitting definition by non-predicated lazim or reinterpreting 'arrangement'.

Having established that the arrangement is a necessary consequence of nazar, the author now faces a methodological question: can we define something by a necessary consequence that is not predicated of it? He presents two options: either permit such definitions, or interpret 'arrangement' in a generous sense. This lesson unpacks that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
The passage presents two possibilities: permit definition by non-predicated lazim, or interpret 'arrangement' generously.
The first option is straightforward; the second requires reinterpretation.
2
Examine the generous interpretation
The generous interpretation includes two sub-options: taking 'arrangement' as 'that by which there is arrangement' (like wisdom defined as perfection of the soul) or as a conventional term.
The analogy with wisdom is instructive.
3
Consider the commentator's conclusion
The commentator concludes that all definitions of nazar are by necessary consequences, and the true reality is the two movements, so there is no real dispute.
This suggests the debate is about terminology, not substance.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of nazar (theoretical reasoning) into sound (ṣaḥīḥ) and corrupt (fāsid), and a discussion of the implications of defining nazar by arrangement (tartīb). It begins with the second objective: that nazar leads to the sought or fails to. Nazar is divided into sound, which leads to the sought, and corrupt, which does not. Checkpoint
What does the generous interpretation of 'arrangement' entail?
A) It means the arrangement is a necessary consequence.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with differing definitions of core Islamic concepts, do you insist on your own precise wording or are you willing to employ generous interpretation as this passage suggests? Where in your current understanding might you be too rigid, and what would it cost you to let go?
?
The commentator says that after directing towards the sought, you are not required to recall principles. Do you trust that Allah can guide your mind directly to the truth, or do you rely on your own ability to arrange arguments step by step? Which reliance truly governs your heart?
?
This passage concludes that there is no real dispute between groups once the essence is understood. Think of a theological disagreement you hold strongly. Are you attached to the terminology itself, or have you examined whether the core essence might be the same? Will you seek that unity even if it means reinterpreting your own words?

Interpreting the 'Intelligibles' and the Predicability of Necessary Consequences
Sanusi clarifies the commentator's interpretation of 'intelligibles' and discusses predicability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different interpretations of 'intelligibles' and the issue of predicability of necessary consequences in definitions.

Core Idea
The 'intelligibles' can refer to principles or sought things, affecting the definition's validity.

This lesson delves into the commentator's distinction between 'intelligibles' as principles versus sought things, and the resulting implications for the definition of nazar. It also addresses the crucial question of whether the necessary consequences used in definitions are predicable (mahmul) or not.

Guided Path
1
Note the different referents of 'intelligibles'
The commentator interprets 'intelligibles' as principles (mabadi), while the author intended them as the sought things (matlib).
This distinction is crucial for understanding the direction of thought.
2
Understand the distinction between directing and focusing
The passage elaborates on the difference between directing towards the sought and focusing the intellect. One is general, the other complete.
This clarifies a potential confusion in the definitions.
3
Grasp the issue of predicability
The passage discusses whether necessary consequences used in definitions are predicated (mahmul) or not, which affects the validity of definition for those who require predication.
This is a logical subtlety.
4
See the example of co-extensiveness
The passage ends with an example concerning co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness to illustrate the point about definitions.
The example is incomplete in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : أنه يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد يقابله ]
( أنه ( أي النظر ينقسم إلى صحيح وهو الذي يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد
مطلوب نظري قليل وإذا كان كذلك، فالترتيب يكون لازماً للحركتين في التحقيق، فتعريف النظر به تعریف باللازم، فإن جوزناً التعريف باللازم الغير المحمول فذاك، وإلا حملنا الكلام على التسامح بأن المراد بالترتيب ما به الترتيب كما في تعريف الحكمة ساستمكمال النفس، أو على الاصطلاح على ذلك .
قوله : ( وتحديق العقل إلخ) حمل الشارح المعقولات على المبادي التي تقع الحركة فيها على خلاف ما نقله سابقاً، وهو الحق إذ الوجدان شاهد صدق على أنه لا يلزم لنا بعد التوجه إلى المطلوب، استحضار المبادي وتحديق النظر في مناسبتها وترتيبها فتأمل قوله : حتى يظهر لك أن هذه التعريفات كلها تعريفات باللوازم وحقيقة النظر، وحقيقة النظر هي الحركتان وأن لا نزاع

قوله : ( وتحديق العقل نحو المعقولات فتأمل مراد الشارح بالمعقولات هو المبادي، وأما مراد المصنف بها فهو المطالب لأن الكلام هناك مسوق على انتفاء الاستعانة بالمعلومات السابقة بخلافه ههنا، فالمتوجه إليه والمحدق نحوه متغايران فيما ذكر الشارح هاهنا، بخلافهما فيما نقله عن الأبهري وقد يقال : التمهيد السابق يدل على أن التحديق أيضا نحو المطلوب، وهذا يشعر بأنه نحو المبادي، وهذا هو الظاهر لكن الفرق بين التوجه إلى المطلوب وتحديق العقل نحوه، لا يخلو عن خفاء، اللهم إلا أن يحمل أحدهما على التوجه في الجملة، والآخر على التوجه التام هذا وكأن الأمر بالتأمل إشارة إلى ما دل عليه كلام المصنف من أن التفسير بالتحديق عن الغفلات لمن لا يرى النظر لاكتساب المجهولات من المعلومات ليس بمقطوع به لجواز أن يكون تعريفا باللازم، لكن الكلام في محمولية هذه اللوازم حتى يصح التعريف بها عند من لا يجوز التعريف

قوله : (إلى) صحيح يؤدي إلى المطلوب أي يؤدي نوعه فلا يرد على تعريف الصحيح والفاسد بانتفاء الطرد والعكس، قولنا زيد حمار وكل حمار جسم، ومجرد حمل الأداء على
[The second objective: that it leads to the sought, and the corrupt counterpart.] (That it,) i.e., theoretical reasoning (al-naẓar), is divided into sound (ṣaḥīḥ) which leads to the sought, and corrupt (fāsid) which does not lead. The sought theoretical is rare; and if this is the case, then the arrangement (al-tartīb) is necessary (lāzim) for the two movements in reality, so defining nazar by it is a definition by a necessary consequence (al-lāzim). If we permit definition by a necessary consequence that is not predicated (ghayr al-maḥmūl), then that is one thing; otherwise, we interpret the statement generously meaning that by ‘arrangement’ is meant that by which there is arrangement, as in the definition of wisdom as ‘the perfection of the soul,’ or as a convention. His statement: “(And focusing the intellect, etc.)” The commentator interpreted ‘intelligibles’ (al-maʿqūlāt) as the principles that undergo motion, contrary to what he transmitted previously, and that is correct, for intuitive experience bears true witness that after directing towards the sought, we are not required to recall the principles and focus on their suitability and arrangement—so ponder his statement until it becomes clear to you that all these definitions are definitions by necessary consequences, and the reality of nazar is the two movements, and there is no dispute in reality between the two groups. His statement: “(And it leads, etc.)” is an explanation of the result and an indication that his word ‘leads’ (yuʾaddī) is a descriptive attribute, not that there is a specific subject and predicate in the expression. His statement: “(And focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, so ponder)” The commentator intended by ‘intelligibles’ the principles, whereas the author intended by them the sought things, because the discussion there was based on the negation of using previous information, unlike here. So the object towards which one directs and focuses differs in what the commentator mentioned here, unlike in what he transmitted from al-Abharī. It may be said: the previous preface indicates that focusing is also towards the sought, and this suggests it is towards the principles—and this is apparent, but the difference between directing toward the sought and focusing the intellect towards it is not without obscurity. Unless one of them is interpreted as general direction and the other as complete direction. This is as if the command to ponder is an indication of what the author’s words imply: that the interpretation of ‘focusing from inattentions’ for someone who does not see nazar as acquiring unknowns from knowns is not certain, because it is possible that it is a definition by a necessary consequence. But the subject is about the predicability (maḥmūliyya) of these necessary consequences so that definition by them is valid for those who do not permit definition by the non-predicated. His statement: “(To) sound leads to the sought, i.e., its kind leads, so it is not objected to the definition of sound and corrupt as lacking co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness, our statement ‘Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body,’ and merely carrying the applicability of leading...
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a classification of nazar (theoretical reasoning) into sound (ṣaḥīḥ) and corrupt (fāsid), and a discussion of the implications of defining nazar by arrangement (tartīb). It begins with the second objective: that nazar leads to the sought or fails to. Nazar is divided into sound, which leads to the sought, and corrupt, which does not. Then it notes that the sought theoretical is rare. Given this, the arrangement is a necessary consequence (lāzim) of the two movements in reality, so defining nazar by arrangement is a definition by a necessary consequence. The author discusses two possibilities: if one permits definition by a non-predicated necessary consequence (al-lāzim ghayr al-maḥmūl), then that is acceptable; otherwise, we must interpret 'arrangement' generously as 'that by which there is arrangement' (like the definition of wisdom), or as a conventional term. The passage then comments on several glosses: the commentator's interpretation of 'intelligibles' as principles (mabādi) rather than sought things; the clarification that 'leads' is a descriptive attribute; the distinction between directing towards the sought and focusing the intellect; and the issue of predicability of necessary consequences for definition. The final part discusses the phrase 'sound leads to the sought' meaning that its kind leads, so the definition is not invalidated by examples that lack co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness (like 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body').

Why 'Bi-manzila' Is Used for Matter and Form
Explains why 'bi-manzila' is used and refutes an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'bi-manzila' (like) is used to describe the relation of sciences and configuration to matter and form, and how the commentators clarify objections regarding accidents.

Core Idea
Matter and form are analogies for reasoning components, not literal parts.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a subtle phrase: 'and they are like matter'. He explains that the word 'like' (bi-manzila) is crucial to avoid a literal reading. He then addresses why this analogy is appropriate and defends it against an objection about accidents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for 'bi-manzila'
Recognize that the sciences and configuration are not literal pillars or matter/form of thought, so the word 'like' indicates analogy.
The author added 'bi-manzila' to prevent misunderstanding.
2
Learn the three reasons given
Identify the two explicit reasons in the text and the third from the commentator: (1) they are not pillars of the arrangement, (2) matter and form are specific to bodies, (3) material and formal cause apply to substances and accidents.
3
Examine the explanation of 'bi-sihha-ha'
Understand that the preposition 'ba' indicates causality, not relation, so the validity of reasoning is due to the validity of its matter and form.
4
Consider the objection and response
Note the objection that thought (an accident) cannot have matter or form, and the response that in technical usage, material and formal causes apply to accidents as well.
This shows the flexibility of the terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهي بمنزلة المادة إلخ ) زاد لفظ بمنزلة لعدم كونهما ركنين للترتيب، ولأن المادة والصورة مختصة بالأجسام، والوجه الأخير ذكره الشارح في حاشيته الكبرى والصغرى، تخصيص مستنداً بأن العلة المادية والصورية شاملة للجواهر والأعراض، منشؤه والاعتراض بمنع عدم الفرق بين المادة والصورة والعلة المادية والصورية فلا تكن من الخابطين. قوله : (بسبب صحتها يعني أن الباء للسببية لا للملابسة، حتى يكون المعنى صحته باعتبار صحتها فيكون وصفه بها باعتبار حال متعلقه أي صحيح مادتها وصورتها على ما في شرح المقاصد . وحاشيته الصغرى توجيهاً لذلك القول أن الفكر عرض لا مادة له ولا صورة، ففيه بحث لأن المفهوم من إطلاقاتهم في مباحث العلة والمعلول عموم العلة الصورية والمادية بحسب الاصطلاح للجواهر والأعراض كما سيأتي إن شاء الله .
His statement: '(And they are like matter etc.)' — he added the word 'like' (bi-manzila) because they are not pillars of the arrangement, and because matter and form are specific to bodies. The last reason is mentioned by the commentator in his major and minor glosses: a specification based on the fact that material and formal cause are inclusive of substances and accidents — its source — and the objection by preventing the lack of difference between matter and form and material and formal cause, so do not be among the confused. His statement: '(Because of its validity' (bi-sihha-ha) means that the ba' (preposition) is for causality, not for relation, so that the meaning is: its validity by considering its validity, so its description by it is by considering the state of its object, i.e., valid in its matter and form, as in Sharh al-Maqasid. And his small gloss as a justification for that statement — that thought (fikr) is an accident with no matter or form — there is discussion in it, because the understanding from their usages in the topics of cause and effect is the generality of formal and material cause in terminology to substances and accidents, as will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage provides clarifications on a specific phrase in the text: 'and they are like matter' (وهي بمنزلة المادة). The commentator explains that the word 'like' (bi-manzila) was added for two reasons: first, because the sciences and the configuration are not literal pillars (rukn) of the arrangement; second, because the terms 'matter' and 'form' are primarily associated with bodies. A third reason, given by the commentator in his glosses, is that material and formal cause in technical usage apply to both substances and accidents, so the analogy is appropriate. The passage then addresses a potential objection: if someone denies the distinction between matter/form and material/formal cause, the commentator warns not to be among the confused. Next, he explains the phrase 'by its validity' (بسبب صحتها), stating that the preposition 'ba' indicates causality, not mere relation. Thus, the validity of reasoning is due to the validity of its matter and form. Finally, he touches on the objection that thought (fikr) is an accident and thus cannot have matter or form, but responds that this objection is questionable because in the terminology of cause and effect, formal and material causes are applicable to accidents as well. This point is deferred to a later discussion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thought (fikr) as an accident cannot have matter or form.
Correction: In technical usage, material and formal causes apply to accidents as well, as seen in discussions of cause and effect.

B) Because matter and form are specific to bodies, and the sciences are not literal parts of thought.
C) Because the arrangement does not need matter or form.

Key Takeaway
The term 'bi-manzila' signals an analogy, not literal identity, and technical terminology extends material and formal causes to accidents.

Why Nazar Cannot Be Clear or Hidden: The Division Belongs to the Proof
The commentator rejects dividing nazar into jali and khafi, and explains that this division applies to the dalil (proof) in two respects: form and matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the division of nazar (reasoning) into clear and hidden is rejected, and how this division correctly applies to the proof (dalil) in terms of form and matter.

Core Idea
Reasoning seeks clarity; it is not itself clear or hidden.

Some scholars proposed that reasoning itself can be clear or hidden. The commentator refutes this by arguing that reasoning is a tool for seeking clarification—it cannot possess the very attribute it seeks. He then clarifies that the proof (dalil) is the real subject of this division, and explains two ways a proof can be clear or hidden: by its syllogistic form and by the nature of its premises. This lesson explores the key argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of dividing nazar
Carefully read the first part of the passage where the commentator rejects dividing nazar into jali and khafi. Notice the core reason: nazar is a preparation for clarification and does not coexist with it.
The argument hinges on the relationship between nazar and bayan (clarification).
2
Understand the comparison with definitions
The commentator contrasts nazar with definitions (ta'rifat). In definitions, the order is fixed and the general term precedes the specific, so there is no variation by form. This helps clarify why the proof is different.
This comparison highlights that variation by form applies to proofs, not definitions.
3
Examine the two respects of the proof
The passage explains that the proof can be clear or hidden in two ways: by form (syllogistic figures vary in clarity) and by matter (premises vary in number and obviousness).
The first figure is clearest; other figures require middle terms. Premises may be immediate or require multiple steps.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Synthesize the main takeaway: the division of reasoning into clear and hidden is rejected because it misattributes a quality of the proof to the act of reasoning itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأن النظر إلخ) يعني أن جلاء النظر وخفاءه إنما هو بالنظر إلى بيانه وكشفه للمنظور فيه وهو لا يجامعه أصلاً لكونه معداً له فلا يتصف بصفاته . قوله : ( وصادقة إما قطعاً أو ظناً أو تسليماً) أي صادقة في نفس الأمر أما حال كونه مقطوعة أو مظنونة أو مسلمة لا أن يكون صدقها بحسب هذه الأمور وإلا لم تتعين الصحة ويدل عليه أيضاً قوله فيما سبق، وإلا وجب الظن بالمطابقة فتأمل. قوله : ( ولا يجامعه) لا بأن يجتمعا في شيء ولا بأن يتصف النظر بالبيان كما صرح به في أبكار الأفكار. قوله : ( بخلاف الاختلاف بحسب الصورة فإن قلت : يجري فيه الاختلاف بحسبها أيضاً بأن يقدم الأعم، أو يؤخر قلت : تأخير الأعم وإن جاز في التعريف لكن الاستعمال على تقديمه قطعاً، فلا اختلاف بحسبها في التعريفات المتداولة فيما بينهم بخلاف الدليل فظهر الفرق .. قوله : ( ومنهم من قسمه ) أي النظر (إلى الجلي والخفي) وهذا بعيد لأن النظر أمر يطلب به البيان ولا يجامعه، فلا يتصف بما هو من صفات البيان فلذلك حققه فقال : ( وتحقيقه أن الدليل قد يعرض له الكيفيتان ) يعني الجلاء والخفاء ) بوجهين أحدهما بحسب الصورة ) وهي الهيئة العارضة للمقدمات ) فإن الأشكال متفاوتة في الجلاء والخفاء ) في استلزام المطلوب فإن الشكل الأول لا يحتاج في ذلك إلى وسط وغيره يحتاج إلى وسط أقل أو أكثر ( وثانيهما بحسب المادة فإن المطلوب قد يتوقف على مقدمات كثيرة وأكثر وذلك بأن لا يكون المطلوب مستنداً ابتداء إلى مقدمات ضرورية، بل ينتهي إليها بوسائط على مراتب متفاوتة فى الكثرة ( وقليلة وأقل ) وذلك بأن يستند إلى الضروريات مثلاً بواسطة واحدة أو يستند إليها ابتداء ( مع تفاوتها) أي تفاوت المقدمات في الجلاء والخفاء، وإن كانت ضرورية ( باعتبار تفاوت في تجريد الطرفين كما مر تقريره، وأنت خبير بأن الاختلاف بحسب المادة يجري في المعرف أيضاً فإن أجزاءه قد تكون ضرورية متفاوتة في الجلاء، وقد تكون نظرية منتهية إلى الضروريات بواسطة أو وسائط بخلاف الاختلاف
His statement: (because nazar, etc.) means that the clarity (jala') and hiddenness (khafa') of nazar is only with respect to its clarification (bayan) and unveiling of the object of inquiry, and it (nazar) does not coexist with it at all because it is a preparation for it, so it is not qualified by its attributes. His statement: (and true either by certainty, opinion, or concession) i.e., true in themselves, but in the state of being certain, opined, or conceded, not that their truth is according to these things; otherwise soundness would not be determined. His statement (previously) also indicates that, otherwise conjecture of correspondence would be necessary. So reflect. His statement: (and it does not coexist with it) neither by the two coexisting in something nor by nazar being qualified by clarification, as stated in Abkar al-Afkar. His statement: (contrary to the variation according to form, for if you say: variation occurs according to it also by presenting the more general or delaying it, I say: delaying the more general, though permissible in definition, usage is to present it definitively, so there is no variation according to it in the definitions current among them, unlike the proof; thus the difference appears. His statement: (and some of them have divided it) i.e., nazar (into the clear and the hidden). This is far-fetched, because nazar is something by which clarification is sought and does not coexist with it, so it is not qualified by what are attributes of clarification. Therefore, he verified it and said: (and its verification is that the proof (dalil) may be characterized by the two qualities) i.e., clarity and hiddenness (in two respects: one according to form, which is the arrangement that accrues to the premises; for the figures (ashkal) differ in clarity and hiddenness in implying the desired conclusion, for the first figure does not need a middle term for that, while others need fewer or more middle terms. (And the second according to matter: for the desired conclusion may depend on many premises, and more—that is, by its not being initially based on necessary premises, but rather ending in them through intermediate stages varying in number—and few and fewer—that is, by being based on necessary premises, for example, through one intermediary or based on them initially. (With their variation) i.e., the variation of the premises in clarity and hiddenness, even if they are necessary (with respect to variation in abstraction of the two extremes, as its explanation has passed. And you are aware that variation according to matter also occurs in the definition (ma'rifa), for its parts may be necessary and vary in clarity, and may be theoretical ending in necessary premises through one or multiple intermediaries, unlike the variation.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The commentator refutes the idea that nazar (reasoning) itself can be divided into clear (jali) and hidden (khafi). He argues that nazar is a process that seeks clarification (bayan) and does not coexist with it—it is a means to an end, not the end itself. Therefore, nazar cannot possess the attributes of clarification. The true subject of this division is the proof (dalil). The proof can be clear or hidden in two ways: (1) by form (sura)—the syllogistic figures differ in how clearly they entail the conclusion; the first figure is clearest, requiring no middle term, while others require fewer or more middle terms. (2) by matter (madda)—the premises may be many or few, and their clarity varies; some premises are necessary and immediately clear, others are theoretical and require intermediate steps. The commentator also notes that variation by form does not apply to definitions (ta'rifat) because in definitions the order is fixed (e.g., genus before differentia), unlike proofs. Thus, the distinction between definitions and proofs is made clear.

تعريف
بيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some scholars thought that reasoning (nazar) itself can be clear or hidden.
Correction: The commentator explains that nazar is a process that seeks clarification and cannot possess the attributes of clarification; the division actually applies to the proof (dalil).

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject dividing nazar into clear (jali) and hidden (khafi)?
A) Because nazar is always clear
B) Because nazar is a mental act that cannot be described
C) Because nazar seeks clarification and does not coexist with it
D) Because the division applies to the matter, not the form

Key Takeaway
The division of reasoning into clear and hidden is incorrect; it is the proof that can be clear or hidden based on its syllogistic form and the nature of its premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about your faith, do you examine both the form and the matter of your arguments, or do you assume that true premises guarantee a sound conclusion? How might you change your reasoning habits after learning this distinction?
?
This lesson teaches that nazar is a preparation for clarity, not clarity itself. In your spiritual life, are you using reasoning as a humble tool to seek truth, or have you made an idol of your own logic? What would it mean to let your reasoning remain a servant, not a master?
?
The passage shows that even necessary premises vary in clarity. Is there a belief you hold as 'obvious' that might actually require deeper examination? Will you commit to testing the foundations of your convictions, not just the conclusions?

Is Inference Clear or Hidden?
Commentary on the metaphorical description of inference as clear or hidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why describing inference as clear or hidden is metaphorical in the view of the majority, and what the truth is according to the commentator.

Core Idea
Clarity and hiddenness belong to the object of knowledge, not to the act of inference.

In this passage, the commentator tackles a subtle issue: can the act of inference itself be described as clear or hidden? Several opinions are presented, each trying to justify a literal or figurative reading. The conclusion draws a sharp distinction between the means of knowledge and its object, clarifying why the description is ultimately metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to how the commentator reports different views before giving the truth.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opinions
The passage presents three interpretations of what 'clear inference' might mean. List each one and note how the commentator evaluates it.
3
Understand the truth (al-haqq)
Focus on the commentator's final verdict: clarity/hiddenness are attributes of the thing known, not of the means of knowing. This reframes the entire discussion.
This is the key doctrinal point.
4
Connect to the broader context
The last line shifts to defining 'sound' inference. Notice how the commentator avoids a tautology by clarifying the intended meaning.
This shows how definitions matter.
5
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does it matter whether description is literal or metaphorical? How does this distinction protect the integrity of kalam reasoning?
Think about the precision required in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فهو لا يعرض للنظر (حقيقة قيل قد ثبت بل اشتهر إطلاق النظر على نفس الأمور المرتبة فلا خفاء في صدقهما عليها حقيقة وأنت خبير بأن ذلك الإطلاق مجازي عند الجمهور، والكلام في اتصاف النظر الحقيقي بهما حقيقة، وقد يقال المراد بجلاء النظر وخفائه كونه مؤدياً أداء واضحاً سريعاً، أو أداء خفياً بطيئاً وإن كانا مستفادين من مادة البيان وصورته، وقيل أيضاً المراد بجلاء النظر كون مقدماته جلية، وهذا الكون المخصوص صفة النظر حقيقة، وكذا الكلام في الخفاء والنقل الصريح من أرباب هذه الصناعة بهذه الإرادة غير لازم لأن قولهم هذا النظر جلي وذا خفي شائع، والأصل في الكلام حقيقته فيحمل مرادهم على هذا والحق أن الجلاء والخفاء بالمعنى المتبادر منهما صفة للشيء باعتبار وتعلق العلم به فلا يوصف به النظر حقيقة لأن النظر ما يحصل به العلم لا ما يتعلق به ذلك، ويمكن أن يحمل قول الشارح على هذا وهذا بعيد فليتأمل . قوله : ( المشتمل على شرائطه إلخ) كانه إشارة إلى أن ليس المراد بالصحيح هاهنا ما مر وهو الذي يؤدي إلى المطلوب لأن القول بأن النظر المؤدي إلى المطلوب يؤدي إليه لغو ولا يتطرق إليه
His saying: 'So it does not apply to inference in reality.' It has been said: it is established, rather widespread, that the term 'inference' is applied to the very arranged things, so there is no hiddenness in their being true of them in reality. And you are aware that that application is metaphorical according to the majority, and the discussion is about the description of real inference by them in reality. And it may be said that what is meant by clarity and hiddenness of inference is its being a performance that is clear and fast, or a performance that is hidden and slow, even though they are derived from the matter of explanation and its form. And it is also said that what is meant by clarity of inference is that its premises are clear, and this specific state is a quality of inference in reality, and similarly for hiddenness. And explicit transmission from the masters of this art regarding this intention is not necessary because their saying 'this is a clear inference' and 'that is a hidden inference' is common, and the principle in speech is its literal meaning, so their intention is carried upon this. And the truth is that clarity and hiddenness in the ordinarily understood sense are an attribute of a thing in consideration of and relation of knowledge to it, so inference cannot be described by them in reality, because inference is that by which knowledge is obtained, not that to which knowledge is attached. And it is possible to understand the words of the commentator as this, though this is far-fetched, so reflect. His saying: 'that includes its conditions etc.' is as if indicating that what is meant by 'sound' here is not what passed, i.e., that which leads to the desired conclusion, because the statement 'inference that leads to the desired conclusion leads to it' is tautological and does not address it.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase from the second objective: 'it does not apply to inference in reality' regarding clarity and hiddenness. The commentator reports that some have argued that the term 'inference' is often applied to the very arranged premises, so describing them as clear or hidden is literal. However, he notes that this usage is metaphorical according to the majority. The real question is whether the act of inference itself can be described by these attributes. Several interpretations are presented: (1) clarity/hiddenness refer to the speed or slowness of the inferential performance, deriving from matter and form; (2) they refer to the clarity of the premises themselves, which would be a real attribute of the premises. The commentator argues that no explicit textual transmission is needed because common speech uses such phrases. The truth (al-haqq) is that clarity and hiddenness in their primary sense are attributes of the object of knowledge, not of the process of inference, because inference is the means to obtain knowledge. Finally, he remarks that the commentator's words may be interpreted in this way, though far-fetched, and calls for reflection. The last line returns to the third objective's definition, clarifying that 'sound' here does not mean the previous definition (leading to the desired conclusion) because that would be tautological.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'this is a clear inference' is literal—the inference itself possesses clarity.
Correction: The commentator explains that clarity and hiddenness in their ordinary sense are attributes of the object of knowledge, not of the inference process. Inference is the means to obtain knowledge, so describing it as clear or hidden is metaphorical.

Checkpoint

B) It is metaphorical because clarity and hiddenness are attributes of the object of knowledge, not of the means.
C) It is literal because the premises are clear.
D) It is unnecessary to consider since it is common speech.

Key Takeaway
Inference is the means to knowledge, so clarity and hiddenness in their ordinary sense cannot be predicated of it literally; they belong to the object of knowledge.

Why a Single Example Is Not Enough
Sharh critiques al-Razi's particular example as insufficient for proving a universal claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi's particular example of reasoning fails to prove that sound reasoning in general yields knowledge, and why a universal major premise is necessary.

Core Idea
A particular claim cannot prove a universal conclusion.

Al-Razi thought he could prove that sound reasoning yields knowledge by offering a single example. But the Sharh shows that this approach misses the mark: the goal is to establish a universal principle, and a particular instance cannot do that. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's argument
Read the passage and identify al-Razi's claim: that reasoning from 'this is contingent' and 'every contingent needs an effecter' yields knowledge that this needs an effecter. Note that he presents this as evidence that useful reasoning exists.
Al-Razi's conclusion is a particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية).
2
Understand the Sharh's criticism
The Sharh says the utility of this claim is little (قل جدواه). Why? Because the purpose is to prove that all sound reasonings yield knowledge, but al-Razi only proved it for one instance.
The Sharh explains that a particular cannot prove the universal; only a universal can do that.
3
See the required syllogism
The Sharh illustrates the needed syllogism: 'This is sound reasoning, and every sound reasoning yields knowledge, so this yields knowledge.' Notice the universal major premise.
Al-Razi's example only provides the minor premise and conclusion, not the universal major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال الرازي قد يفيد العلم فيكون المدعى موجبة جزئية قال في المحصل : الفكر المفيد موجود (وهو) أي هذا المدعى الجزئي ( وإن سهل بيانه ) فإن قولنا : هذا حادث وكل حادث محتاج إلى مؤثر ( يفيدنا العلم بأن هذا محتاج إلى المؤثر فقد وجد نظر مفيد للعلم بلا شبهة ( قل جدواه لأن المقصود الأصلي من إثبات كون الظن الصحيح مفيدا للعلم أن يستدل به على أن الأنظار الصحيحة الصادرة منا مفيدة للعلم بأن يقال مثلاً : هذا نظر صحيح وكل نظر صحيح العلم فهذا يفيد العلم، وإذا كان المدعى الذي أثبتناه جزئياً لم يتيسر لنا ذلك المقصود ( إذ الجزئي لا يثبت ) ولا يعلم حاله ) إلا بالكلي ( الذي يندرج فيه ذلك الجزئي
Al-Razi said: 'It may yield knowledge.' Thus the claim is a particular affirmative. He said in al-Muhassal: 'The useful thought exists.' (And this particular claim, although its clarification is easy, for our statement: 'This is contingent, and every contingent needs an effecter' yields knowledge that this needs an effecter, so a reasoning useful for knowledge has been found without doubt, its utility is little, because the primary purpose of establishing that sound opinion is useful for knowledge is to use it as evidence that sound reasonings emanating from us are useful for knowledge, by saying for example: 'This is sound reasoning, and every sound reasoning yields knowledge, so this yields knowledge.' And if the claim we have established is particular, we cannot achieve that purpose, for the particular cannot be proven nor its state known except by the universal under which that particular falls.)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques a key argument by al-Razi. Al-Razi claimed that sound reasoning can yield knowledge, offering as evidence the concrete example: 'This is contingent, and every contingent needs an effecter, so this needs an effecter.' He concluded that a reasoning useful for knowledge exists, which is a particular affirmative claim (موجبة جزئية). The Sharh then explains why this particular claim has little utility (قل جدواه). The main goal of proving that sound reasoning yields knowledge is to use it as a general premise to demonstrate that all sound reasonings (including those we perform) yield knowledge. For that, a universal major premise is needed, such as 'Every sound reasoning yields knowledge.' But al-Razi's example only proves that some reasoning (one instance) yields knowledge, not that all do. A particular claim cannot serve as the required universal premise; only a universal claim can prove other particulars that fall under it. Thus, al-Razi's argument fails to achieve the intended purpose.

Key Terms
موجبة جزئية
كبرى
صغرى
استدلال
علم
الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Providing one successful example of reasoning is enough to prove that all sound reasoning yields knowledge.
Correction: The Sharh explains that a particular instance (جزئي) cannot establish a universal claim (كلي). To prove that all sound reasonings yield knowledge, a universal major premise is required. The example only shows that some reasoning yields knowledge, not all.

Checkpoint
Why does the Sharh say that al-Razi's particular claim (موجبة جزئية) has little utility?
A) Because it is not supported by the Qur'an.
B) Because it cannot be used as a universal premise to prove the general point.
C) Because al-Razi was a famous theologian but his argument is weak.
D) Because the example is not actually valid.

Key Takeaway
A particular example cannot prove a universal claim; a universal major premise is needed for syllogistic proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you attribute a general ruling or belief to your faith, have you checked whether it is based on a universal proof or just one example? What step will you take today to verify the universality of a claim you hold?

Al-Amidi's Universal Definition of Correct Reasoning
Al-Amidi defines correct reasoning that yields knowledge with three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Amidi defines correct reasoning that yields knowledge as a universal affirmative with necessary conditions, and why these conditions are essential.

Core Idea
Correct reasoning must be certain, sound in form and matter, and free from any opposite of knowledge.

This lesson introduces al-Amidi's precise definition of the kind of reasoning that leads to knowledge. He refines the claim by specifying conditions that must be met, turning it into a universal statement that can serve as a foundation for theological arguments. Let's see how he constructs this definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: it starts with a claim, then adds restrictions, and ends with a conclusion about the nature of the claim.
2
Identify the three conditions
Al-Amidi lists three conditions for correct reasoning to yield knowledge: (1) sound matter and form, (2) being in certainties, (3) not being followed by an opposite of knowledge. Find each in the text.
The opposite includes death, sleep, and heedlessness.
3
Understand why each condition is necessary
Reflect on why al-Amidi adds each condition. For example, excluding probable premises ensures the reasoning is certain; excluding opposites ensures the knowledge persists. How would the definition fail without them?
4
See the universal affirmative structure
Al-Amidi concludes that his definition makes the claim a universal affirmative (موجبة كلية). This means every instance of reasoning meeting the conditions yields knowledge. Understand why this is important for argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الآمدي : كل نظر صحيح بحسب مادته وصورته معاً (في القطعيات) احترز بهذا القيد عن النظر الصحيح الذي في المقدمات الظنية الصادقة، فإنه يفيد ظناً لا علماً ( لا يعقبه ضد للعلم أي مناف له ( كالموت والنوم) والغفلة وفائدة هذا التقييد ظاهرة ( مفيد له ) أي للعلم فقد جعل المدعى موجبة كلية موضوعها مقيد بقيود
And al-Amidi said: 'Every correct reasoning according to both its matter and form together (in certainties) – he guarded by this restriction against correct reasoning that occurs in probable true premises, for it yields opinion, not knowledge – (and) which is not followed by an opposite of knowledge, i.e., a contradictory to it, such as death, sleep, and heedlessness; and the benefit of this restriction is apparent – (such reasoning) yields it, i.e., knowledge.' So he made the claim a universal affirmative whose subject is restricted by restrictions.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents al-Amidi's alternative formulation. Unlike al-Razi, al-Amidi asserts that the claim that correct reasoning yields knowledge is a universal affirmative (موجبة كلية). He qualifies the subject with several conditions: First, the reasoning must be correct in both matter and form. Second, it must be in certainties (القطيعات), excluding probable premises. Third, it must not be followed by an opposite of knowledge, such as death, sleep, or heedlessness, which would prevent knowledge. These restrictions ensure that every reasoning meeting these conditions yields knowledge. Thus al-Amidi's claim is universal, allowing it to serve as a major premise. The passage notes that the benefit of these restrictions is clear: they make the universal claim true.

Key Terms
الموجبة الكلية
القطعيات
الضد
مادة وصورة
النظر الصحيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'opposite of knowledge' includes only contradictory opposites like falsehood, but al-Amidi includes death, sleep, and heedlessness as states that prevent knowledge.
Correction: Al-Amidi explicitly lists death, sleep, and heedlessness as examples of opposites that negate knowledge, showing that any state that prevents the continuity of knowledge is an opposite.

Checkpoint

A) The reasoning must be in certainties
B) The reasoning must be followed by an opposite of knowledge
C) The reasoning must be correct in matter and form
D) The reasoning must not be in probable premises

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi defines correct reasoning that yields knowledge as a universal affirmative subject to three conditions: sound matter and form, certainty of premises, and absence of any opposite that would prevent knowledge.

The Condition 'No Opposite' in Defining Knowledge
Commentators discuss the necessity and interpretation of the condition 'no opposite' for knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition that reasoning must not be followed by an opposite is necessary only for incomplete definitions and how the term 'opposite' can include privations like death.

Core Idea
A definition's completeness determines whether an opposite can succeed it.

In defining reasoning, the condition 'no opposite' seems obvious, but scholars debate its necessity. This passage from a commentary on al-Mawaqif explains that the condition applies only to non-complete definitions, and addresses an objection about death being a privation, not an opposite. You will see how precision in language is crucial in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the two distinct points: the scope of the condition and the objection about death.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the scope of the condition
The first part says the condition is only needed when defining reasoning in a non-complete sense. For the complete definition, there is no possibility of opposites succeeding before the goal is attained.
Reflect on why completeness removes the need.
3
Examine the objection and response
The second part addresses the objection that death is a privation, yet it is called an opposite of knowledge. The response is that 'opposite' is used based on a view that death is existential.
This shows how technical terms can shift meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا يعقبه ضد للعلم قيل : هذا القيد إنما يحتاج إليه في تعريف النظر إذا خص بما سوى التحديد التام وأما فيه فلا لعدم احتمال تعاقب الأضداد عقيب التمام قبل حصول المطلوب فتأمل . قوله : (أي مناف (له فلا يرد أن الموت عدمي فكيف يكون ضداً للعلم؟ والضدان هما الوجوديان وقيل: إطلاق الضد مبني على مذهب من يقول : إن الموت وجودي لا يقال الموت
His statement: '(which is) not followed by an opposite of knowledge' – it was said: This restriction is only needed in the definition of reasoning when it is specified as other than the complete definition; but as for it (the complete definition), it is not needed because there is no possibility of the succession of opposites after completeness before attainment of the desired outcome, so reflect. His statement: 'i.e., a contradictory to it' – so it is not objected that death is a privation, so how can it be an opposite of knowledge? The two opposites are existent things. And it was said: The use of 'opposite' is based on the view of one who says that death is existent – it is not said that death (is non-existent).
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that correct reasoning must not be followed by an opposite of knowledge. Some commentators argue that this condition is only necessary when defining reasoning in a non-complete sense; in a complete definition, it is unnecessary because opposites cannot succeed each other before the goal is attained. The passage then addresses a potential objection: death is a privation (non-existential), yet it is called an opposite of knowledge. But opposites are typically both existential. The response is that calling death an opposite is based on a view that death is existential. Thus the condition remains valid. This passage shows the nuance in the definitions used by kalam scholars.

Key Terms
الضد
التحديد التام
الوجودي
العدمي
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the condition 'no opposite' is always necessary in defining reasoning.
Correction: The condition is only necessary for incomplete definitions; in complete definitions, it is not needed because there is no possibility of opposites succeeding before the goal.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why might the condition 'no opposite' be considered necessary only for a non-complete definition of reasoning?
A) Because in a complete definition, opposites cannot occur before the goal
B) Because complete definitions do not include conditions
C) Because the opposite of knowledge does not exist
D) Because reasoning is always followed by knowledge

Key Takeaway
The condition 'no opposite' is necessary only for incomplete definitions; 'opposite' can include privations like death based on a view of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why the Claim About Nazar Cannot Be Known: Objection and Response
al-Jurjani presents a dilemma against the knowability of 'nazar yields knowledge' and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma that the proposition 'correct speculation yields knowledge' cannot be known either necessarily or speculatively, and the response that it is both true and known.

Core Idea
Knowing that nazar yields knowledge faces a dilemma: necessary (disputed) or speculative (circular).

Al-Jurjani presents a powerful objection against the very claim that correct speculation (nazar) yields knowledge. The objectors argue that this claim cannot be known at all – if it were self-evident, it would not be disputed; if it were speculative, it would lead to circularity. The response clarifies that the objection only targets our ability to know the claim, not its truth. Let’s dive into the passage to see this argument unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objection uses a classic dilemma structure.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objectors say the claim must be either necessary (daruri) or speculative (nazari). Identify where each is discussed in the passage.
The text uses 'أما الأول' and 'وأما الثاني' to mark the two horns.
3
Understand why the necessary horn fails
Necessary truths are not disputed, but this claim is. Also, it is less certain than obvious truths like 'one is half of two', so it is not self-evident.
The objectors give two reasons: disagreement and lack of self-evidence.
4
Understand why the speculative horn fails
If speculative, proving it would require another speculative proof, leading to circularity (proving nazar by nazar), which is a contradiction.
Circularity is a logical contradiction.
5
Study the response
The response argues that the objection only shows the impossibility of knowing the claim, not that it is false. The claim remains true and known to be true, because the purpose depends on knowledge.
The response reframes the debate from truth to knowability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال المنكرون ( لكون النظر الصحيح مفيداً للعلم (هذا) أي كون النظر الصحيح مفيداً له ( إن كان معلوماً كان ضرورياً ) مستغنياً عن الاحتجاج عليه ( أو نظرياً محتاجاً إليه ) وهما باطلان أما الأول يعني كونه ضرورياً ( فلان الضروري لا يختلف فيه (العقلاء أصلاً خصوصاً إذا كان الضروري أولياً ( وهذا) أي كون النظر الصحيح مفيداً للعلم ) مختلف فيه ) بين العقلاء ( ولأنا نجد بينه ) أي بين الحكم بأن النظر الصحيح مفيد للعلم ) وبين قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين تفاوتاً ضرورياً) معلوماً ببديهة العقل ( ونجزم بأنه) أي كون النظر مفيداً للعلم ( دون ذلك ) القول ( في القوة ولا يتصور ذلك ) أي كونه دونه في القوة ) إلا باحتماله للنقيض ولو بأبعد وجه وإنه ) أي احتماله للنقيض ينفي بداهته قطعاً فلا يكون بديهياً ( وأما الثاني) يعني كونه نظرياً فلأنه إثبات للنظر بالنظر إذ يحتاج على تقدير كونه نظرياً إلى نظر يفيد العلم به فيلزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه ( وإنه تناقض ) لاستلزامه كون الشيء معلوماً ليس معلوماً، فإن قيل : هذه الشبهة إنما تدل على امتناع العلم بكون النظر مفيداً لا على انتفاء صدقه، لجواز أن يكون صادقاً في نفسه مع امتناع العلم به، قلنا: المدعى عندنا هو أن هذه القضية صادقة معلومة الصدق لأن المقصود بها يترتب على العلم
Then the objectors said regarding [the claim] that correct speculation is beneficial for knowledge: this [i.e., that correct speculation is beneficial for it] if it is known, it would be necessary [i.e., self-evident] dispensing with proof, or speculative needing it. Both are false. As for the first, meaning it being necessary: because the necessary [truth] is not differed about by the rational people at all, especially if it is a primary necessary [truth]. And this [i.e., that correct speculation is beneficial for knowledge] is differed about among rational people. And also because we find between it [i.e., the judgment that correct speculation is beneficial for knowledge] and our statement 'one is half of two' a necessary difference known by the intuition of the intellect, and we are certain that it [i.e., that speculation is beneficial for knowledge] is less than that statement in strength, and that [its being less in strength] cannot be conceived except by its admitting the opposite, even in the most remote way, and that [its admitting the opposite] negates its self-evidence completely, so it is not self-evident. And as for the second, meaning it being speculative: because it would be a proof of speculation by speculation, since on the assumption of it being speculative, it would need a speculation that yields knowledge of it, leading to proving the thing by itself, and that is a contradiction because it entails the thing being known and not known. Then it is said: This sophism only indicates the impossibility of knowledge that speculation is beneficial, not the negation of its truth, for it is possible that it is true in itself while knowledge of it is impossible. We say: Our claim is that this proposition is true and known to be true, because the purpose [intended] by it is dependent upon knowledge.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the claim that correct speculation (nazar) yields knowledge and then answers it. The objectors, likely the Sophists, argue that the proposition 'correct speculation is beneficial for knowledge' cannot be known. They offer a dilemma: either it is known necessarily (daruri) or speculatively (nazari). If necessary, it should be universally agreed upon, but it is disputed; moreover, it is less certain than obvious truths like 'one is half of two', which shows it is not self-evident. If speculative, proving it would require another speculative proof, leading to circularity (proving nazar by nazar), which is a contradiction. The answer (qulna) responds that the objection only shows the impossibility of knowing the proposition, not that it is false. The author's claim is that the proposition is true and known to be true, because the purpose for which it is intended (i.e., to establish knowledge) depends on its being known.

Key Terms
نظر
علم
ضروري
نظري
بديهة
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that the claim 'nazar yields knowledge' is false.
Correction: The objection only argues that the claim cannot be known, not that it is false. The response maintains that it is both true and known.

Checkpoint
According to the objectors, why is the claim that correct speculation yields knowledge not necessary (daruri)?
A) Because necessary truths are not disputed, but this is.
B) Because it requires proof.
C) Because it leads to circularity.
D) Because it is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma against knowing the foundational claim of kalam is cleverly answered by distinguishing between truth and knowledge of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objectors assumed that a proposition must be either self-evident or proven to be known. In your own life, do you sometimes doubt truths that you actually know but cannot prove? How does that affect your certainty?
?
The author distinguishes between a proposition being true and being known. Are there areas of your aqeedah where you hold beliefs as true but struggle with the feeling of being certain? What would it take for you to move from mere belief to certain knowledge?
?
If you were challenged to prove that your foundational beliefs are valid, would you be able to respond like the author, or would you fall into the dilemma? What step can you take today to strengthen your intellectual foundation?

The Deniers' Objection to the Utility of Reasoning and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding the knowability of reasoning's utility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' two-pronged argument against the epistemic value of correct reasoning and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between truth and knowability.

Core Idea
Reasoning's utility cannot be proven without circularity or disagreement—or can it?

In this dense passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif, al-Ījī (or the commentator) presents a powerful objection against the claim that correct reasoning yields knowledge. The deniers argue that if such a claim were known, it would be either necessary or theoretical—both of which lead to impossibility. The author then provides a subtle refutation. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the deniers' objection is presented first, followed by the refutation.
2
Identify the two-pronged objection
The deniers argue that if knowledge of the benefit of reasoning is possible, it must be either necessary or theoretical. Find why they claim both are impossible.
For necessary: disagreement among rational people; for theoretical: circularity.
3
Understand the refutation
The author responds by asserting that the proposition is both true and known to be true, and addresses a potential counterargument about the difference between truth and knowability.
Note the subtle shift: the deniers claim impossibility of knowledge, not falsity.

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the deniers (al-munkirūn) against the claim that correct reasoning (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) is beneficial for knowledge (al-ʿilm). The deniers argue that if the benefit is known, it must be either necessary (ḍarūrī) or theoretical (naẓarī), both of which are impossible. The first possibility is impossible because necessary matters are not disagreed upon, but this proposition is disagreed upon. Additionally, there is a clear difference in strength between this proposition and a basic self-evident truth like 'one is half of two,' indicating it is not self-evident. The second possibility is impossible because it would lead to circularity: proving proof by proof. The deniers conclude that the benefit cannot be known. The author then addresses a potential objection: that the doubt only shows impossibility of knowledge, not falsity. The response is that the claim is that the proposition is both true and known to be true, because its purpose depends on knowledge. The commentary explains details: that the terms 'necessary' and 'theoretical' exhaust possibilities, that the word 'in' is for supposition or alternation, and that the claim of truth entails claim of knowability. It also notes a counter-objection regarding the benefit of opinion (al-ẓann) which is agreed upon, but the response is that the deniers claim opinion specifically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The deniers' objection proves that the proposition 'reasoning yields knowledge' is false.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the deniers' objection only shows that knowledge of the proposition is impossible, not that the proposition itself is false. The author refutes by asserting that the proposition is both true and known.

Checkpoint
What is the deniers' second argument against the utility of correct reasoning being known?
A) It is impossible because it would be a necessary truth that everyone agrees on.
B) It would require a proof of proof, leading to circularity.
C) It is only probable, not certain.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The deniers' objection fails because it assumes that knowledge must be either necessary or theoretical, but their own premise leads to circularity; the refutation shows that the claim is both true and known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The deniers' objection makes that fear seem rational, but the refutation shows that doubting reasoning itself relies on reasoning. How does this insight compel you to trust the intellect God gave you as a valid tool for seeking truth about Him?
?
You have likely used reasoning to accept the existence of the Creator. But what if someone told you that your reasoning is itself unreliable? Would your faith collapse? This lesson shows that the objection against reasoning is self-defeating. What step will you take today to reinforce your reliance on sound reasoning in matters of faith?
?
The deniers' objection is intellectual, but its root is a distrust of reason. Is there an area of your faith where you hesitate to apply reason because you fear it will lead to doubt? How does the lesson's resolution—that the proposition is both true and known—empower you to engage your intellect fearlessly in understanding your beliefs? The source says: 'What we claim is that this proposition is true and known to be true because the purpose of it depends on knowledge.' How does this apply to your personal conviction?

Al-Razi's Defense: Disagreement Does Not Refute Necessity
Al-Razi rebuts the objection that disagreement negates necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi maintains that the utility of correct reasoning is necessarily known despite disagreements, and how he rebuts the first objection that disagreement would invalidate necessity.

Core Idea
Disagreement does not disprove necessity; the issue is often in conceiving the terms, not the judgment itself.

Imam al-Razi and his followers assert that the utility of correct reasoning (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) is known necessarily, i.e., as a self-evident truth. However, critics object that if it were truly necessary, no one would disagree. This lesson examines al-Razi's rebuttal, which draws on the fact that even some self-evident propositions face denial due to obscurity in conception or difficulty in abstraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the structure: a claim by al-Razi, an objection, and his response. Note the use of terms like ضروري and بديهي.
The passage begins with al-Razi's choice, then quotes the objection after 'قولكم'.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that if the utility of correct reasoning were a necessary truth, it would not be subject to disagreement. Find the Arabic phrase 'لو كان ضروريا لم يختلف فيه'.
3
Analyze al-Razi's response
Al-Razi first rejects the premise that disagreement cannot occur with necessity. Then he provides evidence: some deny even basic self-evidents. The real cause is difficulty in abstracting the terms from accidental attributes.
Look for the words 'خفاء' (obscurity) and 'عسر' (difficulty).
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this defense strengthens the argument for the necessity of correct reasoning. Disagreement alone is not a valid objection if it stems from conceptual confusion.
This is a common move in kalam to defend foundational propositions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Imam al-Razi and his followers who choose the view that the utility of correct reasoning is known necessarily (ḍarūrī). It defends against two objections. First, the objection that if it were necessary, it would not be disagreed upon is rebutted because disagreement does not preclude necessity; some people deny self-evident matters entirely due to obscurity in conception or difficulty in abstracting the terms from accidental attributes. Second, the objection that it is weaker than obvious self-evident truths because it admits the possibility of the opposite is rebutted by explaining that the difference in strength is due either to familiarity (al-ulf wa al-isti'nās) with the common statement or to a difference in the degree of abstraction, neither of which undermines its self-evident nature. The passage references a previous rebuttal of the deniers of self-evident matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement proves a proposition is not self-evident.
Correction: Disagreement can arise from obscurity in conception or difficulty in abstraction, not from the proposition's lack of self-evidence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's defense, why does disagreement about the utility of correct reasoning not negate its being a necessary truth?
A) Because only a few people disagree.
B) Because disagreement arises from difficulty in abstracting the terms, not from the judgment itself.
C) Because the proposition is actually theoretical.
D) Because it is a matter of opinion.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement alone does not invalidate a proposition's necessity; the true cause may be conceptual difficulties in the mind of the denier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear truth—whether from revelation or reason—but find yourself resistant, do you honestly examine your own conceptual clarity and attachments, or do you dismiss the truth because it is uncomfortable? What 'accidents' might you be clinging to that prevent you from seeing the judgment as it is?
?
Think of a fundamental belief you hold about God or existence. If someone disagrees, does your certainty waver? How does al-Razi's argument challenge you to separate the truth from the noise of disagreement? Will you commit today to seeking conceptual clarity rather than relying on consensus?
?
Are there areas in your spiritual life where you have denied a necessary truth—for example, about your own accountability or the nature of divine mercy—because you found it difficult to abstract from your own desires? What would it cost you to strip away those attachments and see the truth plainly? Are you willing to pay that price today?
