المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور
الوجه الثاني أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان) ( وأولاها بأن يكون معلوماً له بحقيقته وأحواله ( هويته) التي يشير إليها بقوله : إنا ( وإنها غير معلومة لا من حيث التصديق بوجودها فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه، بل من حيث ) تصورها بكنهها، ومن حيث التصديق بأحوالها من كونها عرضاً أو جوهراً، مجرداً أو جسمانياً منقسماً أو غير منقسم إلى غير ذلك من صفاتها (إذ قد كثر الخلاف فيها كثرة لا يمكن معها مع تلك الكثرة ( الجزم بشيء من الأقوال المختلفة ) المتنافية ) التي ذكرت فيها في تلك الهوية (كما ستقف عليها ) على تلك الأقوال في مباحث النفس، فلو كان النظر يفيد العلم بتلك الهوية وصفاتها، لما اختار العقلاء الناظرون فيها أقوالاً متناقضة ) وإذا كان أقرب الأشياء إليه كذلك ) أي بحيث لا يفيد التصديق الظني أيضاً كذلك، إذ لا فرق بينهما في أن كلاً منهما يستدعي تصور الطرفين على ما هو مناط الحكم، فإذا وجب التصور بالكنه في التصديق اليقيني لجواز أن يكون في ذاتهما ما يمنع ذلك التصديق، وجب في التصديق الظني أيضاً، لجواز أن يكون في ذاتهما ما يمنع التصديق، وقيل الظني لضعفه يجوز أن يكفي فيه التصور بالوجه الذي هو ضعيف بخلاف التصديق اليقيني .

قوله : ( وأولاها إلخ) أي لكونها حاضرة عنده دائماً، والعلم ليس إلا حضور المدرك عند المدرك وفيه إشارة إلى أن المراد الأقرب إدراكاً لا ذاتاً .

قوله : ( فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه إذ كل أحد يعلم بأنه موجود حتى الصبيان والمجانين، وهذا التصديق ليس بالأحوال المخصوصة حتى يستدعي تصوره بالكنه، فلا يرد أنه إذا كان التصديق اليقيني فرع التصور بالكنه عندهم كيف يقولون بحصول هذا التصديق؟ مع عدم التصور بالكنه .

قوله : ( الثاني أقرب الأشياء إلخ ينبغي أن يقيدوا الأشياء بالغائبة عن الحواس، وعدم الاتساق والقرب من الأوهام كيلا ينقض دليلهم بالهندسيات والحسابيات والممكنات، ثم إنه إنما يتم على تقدير تسليم عدم معلومية النفس أن لو كانت أقربيتها في المدركية، وإذا لا يلزم من أقربيتها اتصالاً أقربيتها إدراكاً ألا يرى أن القوة الحاسة لا تدرك نفسها لم يلزم مدعاهم .

قوله : (لا) من حيث التصديق بوجودها فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه فيه بحث لأن التصديق عندهم يستدعي تصور المحكوم عليه بالكنه كما تبين من دليلهم الأول، وإذا لم تكن النفس معلومة من حيث التصديق فكيف يقولون هي معلومة من حيث التصديق بالوجود بداهة؟ والحمل على بداهة التصديق الظني بوجودها بعيد اللهم إلا أن يبني الكلام على إرادة إلزام الخصوم بأنها غير معلومة عندكم، فلزمكم الاعتراف بما ذكرنا فمرادهم بقوله : فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه أنه بديهي عندكم لا خلاف فيه بينكم.

النظر فيه علماً ( فما ظنك بأبعدها عنه وإفادة النظر فيه العلم، وهذا من قبيل التنبيه بالأدنى على الأعلى لا من القياس الفقهي كما ترى قلنا : لا نسلم أن هوية الإنسان غير معلومة (له ( أصلاً ) وكثرة الخلاف فيها لا تدل إلا على العسر) أي على عسر معرفتها ( وأما الامتناع أي امتناع معرفتها أو عدمها ( فلا ) تدل عليه تلك الكثرة لجواز أن تكون معلومة لصحة بعض تلك الأنظار وفساد باقيها، فلم يثبت بما ذكرتم أن هناك نظراً صحيحاً لا يفيد علماً، بل ثبت أن تميز النظر الصحيح عن غيره مشكل جداً، فيكون ذلك في الإلهيات أشكل ولا نزاع فيه
The Fifth Observatory - Third Objective: Correct Reasoning according to the Majority. The second aspect: the closest things to man, and the most deserving of being known to him in its reality and its states, is his essence (identity) which he refers to by saying: I (and indeed it is unknown, not from the aspect of assent to its existence, for that is self-evident with no dispute, but from the aspect of) conceiving it in its essence, and from the aspect of assent to its states, such as whether it is an accident or a substance, abstract or corporeal, divisible or indivisible, and other attributes (for indeed disagreement about it has become so great that with that great number it is impossible to be certain of any of the different contradictory) opinions that have been mentioned concerning that identity (as you will come across) those opinions in the investigations of the soul. So if reasoning were to yield knowledge of that identity and its attributes, the rational thinkers who reason about it would not have chosen contradictory opinions. And when the closest thing to him is like that, i.e., it does not yield even probable assent likewise, for there is no difference between them in that each of them necessitates conceiving the two extremes in the way that is the basis of judgment. So if conceiving in essence is necessary for certain assent due to the possibility that there is in the extremes themselves something that prevents that assent, then it is necessary for probable assent as well, due to the possibility that there is in the extremes themselves something that prevents assent. And it is said that probable, due to its weakness, it is permissible that conceiving by the face that is weak suffices for it, unlike certain assent. His saying: 'and the most deserving etc.': i.e., because it is always present with him, and knowledge is nothing but the presence of the perceived object with the perceiver. In it there is an indication that what is meant is the closest in perception, not in essence. His saying: 'for it is self-evident with no dispute': since everyone knows that it exists, even children and the insane. And this assent is not about specific states such that it would necessitate conceiving it in essence. So it is not objected that if certain assent is a branch of conceiving in essence according to them, how do they say this assent occurs despite the lack of conceiving in essence? His saying: 'the second: the closest things etc.': They should restrict 'things' to those absent from the senses, and lack of consistency and closeness to illusions, so that their proof is not refuted by geometrical, arithmetical, and possible things. Then, it is only valid on the assumption of submitting that the soul is not known, if its closeness were in perceptivity. And if closeness in connection does not necessitate closeness in perception — do you not see that the sensory faculty does not perceive itself — their claim does not follow. His saying: 'not from the aspect of assent to its existence for it is self-evident with no dispute' contains a discussion, because assent according to them necessitates conceiving the subject of judgment in essence, as was shown from their first proof. So if the soul is not known from the aspect of conception, how do they say it is known from the aspect of assent to existence by self-evidence? And interpreting it as self-evidence of probable assent to its existence is far-fetched. Unless the speech is built on intending to compel the opponents that it is unknown according to you, so you must admit what we mentioned. So their meaning in his saying: 'for it is self-evident with no dispute' is that it is self-evident according to you, with no dispute among you. Reasoning about it yields knowledge. So what do you think about that which is further away from it and reasoning yielding knowledge about it? This is of the type of alerting by the lower to the higher, not of the type of juristic analogy, as you see. We say: We do not accept that the identity of man is unknown to him at all. The abundance of disagreement about it does not indicate except difficulty, i.e., the difficulty of knowing it. As for impossibility, i.e., the impossibility of knowing it or its non-existence, that abundance does not indicate it, because it is possible that it is known due to the correctness of some of those reasonings and the corruption of the rest. So you have not established by what you mentioned that there is a correct reasoning that does not yield knowledge. Rather, it is established that distinguishing correct reasoning from others is very problematic, so that in the divine matters it is more problematic, and there is no dispute about that.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation structure. It presents an argument (the 'second face') that correct reasoning (nazar) cannot yield certain knowledge, using the soul as the closest object of knowledge. The argument claims that since the soul is closest to man yet its essence and attributes are heavily disputed, reasoning cannot produce certainty—otherwise rational thinkers would not contradict each other. It extends this to probable knowledge, arguing that the same requirement for essential conception applies. The commentary then clarifies each point: the phrase 'most deserving' indicates perceptual closeness, the self-evidence of existence is not affected because it does not require essential conception, and the proof must restrict 'things' to metaphysical matters to avoid counterexamples. The commentary also notes a potential circularity in the opponents' claim about self-evidence. Finally, the response ('We say') denies the premise: disagreement does not prove impossibility but only difficulty. It argues that some of the contradictory opinions may be correct, so correct reasoning may still yield knowledge; it only shows that distinguishing correct from incorrect reasoning is hard, especially in theology. This refutation maintains that reasoning can yield knowledge, though identifying correct reasoning is problematic.

التصديق

بديهي
العقلاء
المتناقضة

The Refutation: Disagreement Indicates Difficulty, Not Impossibility
Refutes the argument that the soul's unknown essence proves reasoning fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation: that the abundance of disagreement about the soul does not prove that reasoning cannot yield knowledge, only that it is difficult to identify correct reasoning.

Core Idea
Disagreement shows difficulty, not impossibility, of knowledge.

Having heard the argument that the soul's disputed nature proves reasoning fails, we now turn to the response. The refutation denies the premise that the soul is completely unknown, and argues that the mere existence of contradictory opinions does not negate the possibility of correct reasoning. It only shows that distinguishing correct from incorrect reasoning is challenging, especially in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the passage that begins with 'قلنا : لا نسلم'. Note that it directly rejects the opponent's claim.
The refutation is concise but powerful.
2
Identify the two key points
First: disagreement does not prove impossibility (امتناع), only difficulty (عسر). Second: some of the contradictory opinions may be correct.
3
Understand the implication for theology
The refutation concludes that the real problem is distinguishing correct reasoning, not that reasoning itself is invalid. This is especially hard in divine matters.
This preserves the possibility of valid rational theology.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور
الوجه الثاني أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان) ( وأولاها بأن يكون معلوماً له بحقيقته وأحواله ( هويته) التي يشير إليها بقوله : إنا ( وإنها غير معلومة لا من حيث التصديق بوجودها فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه، بل من حيث ) تصورها بكنهها، ومن حيث التصديق بأحوالها من كونها عرضاً أو جوهراً، مجرداً أو جسمانياً منقسماً أو غير منقسم إلى غير ذلك من صفاتها (إذ قد كثر الخلاف فيها كثرة لا يمكن معها مع تلك الكثرة ( الجزم بشيء من الأقوال المختلفة ) المتنافية ) التي ذكرت فيها في تلك الهوية (كما ستقف عليها ) على تلك الأقوال في مباحث النفس، فلو كان النظر يفيد العلم بتلك الهوية وصفاتها، لما اختار العقلاء الناظرون فيها أقوالاً متناقضة ) وإذا كان أقرب الأشياء إليه كذلك ) أي بحيث لا يفيد التصديق الظني أيضاً كذلك، إذ لا فرق بينهما في أن كلاً منهما يستدعي تصور الطرفين على ما هو مناط الحكم، فإذا وجب التصور بالكنه في التصديق اليقيني لجواز أن يكون في ذاتهما ما يمنع ذلك التصديق، وجب في التصديق الظني أيضاً، لجواز أن يكون في ذاتهما ما يمنع التصديق، وقيل الظني لضعفه يجوز أن يكفي فيه التصور بالوجه الذي هو ضعيف بخلاف التصديق اليقيني .

قوله : ( وأولاها إلخ) أي لكونها حاضرة عنده دائماً، والعلم ليس إلا حضور المدرك عند المدرك وفيه إشارة إلى أن المراد الأقرب إدراكاً لا ذاتاً .

قوله : ( فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه إذ كل أحد يعلم بأنه موجود حتى الصبيان والمجانين، وهذا التصديق ليس بالأحوال المخصوصة حتى يستدعي تصوره بالكنه، فلا يرد أنه إذا كان التصديق اليقيني فرع التصور بالكنه عندهم كيف يقولون بحصول هذا التصديق؟ مع عدم التصور بالكنه .

قوله : ( الثاني أقرب الأشياء إلخ ينبغي أن يقيدوا الأشياء بالغائبة عن الحواس، وعدم الاتساق والقرب من الأوهام كيلا ينقض دليلهم بالهندسيات والحسابيات والممكنات، ثم إنه إنما يتم على تقدير تسليم عدم معلومية النفس أن لو كانت أقربيتها في المدركية، وإذا لا يلزم من أقربيتها اتصالاً أقربيتها إدراكاً ألا يرى أن القوة الحاسة لا تدرك نفسها لم يلزم مدعاهم .

قوله : (لا) من حيث التصديق بوجودها فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه فيه بحث لأن التصديق عندهم يستدعي تصور المحكوم عليه بالكنه كما تبين من دليلهم الأول، وإذا لم تكن النفس معلومة من حيث التصديق فكيف يقولون هي معلومة من حيث التصديق بالوجود بداهة؟ والحمل على بداهة التصديق الظني بوجودها بعيد اللهم إلا أن يبني الكلام على إرادة إلزام الخصوم بأنها غير معلومة عندكم، فلزمكم الاعتراف بما ذكرنا فمرادهم بقوله : فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه أنه بديهي عندكم لا خلاف فيه بينكم.

النظر فيه علماً ( فما ظنك بأبعدها عنه وإفادة النظر فيه العلم، وهذا من قبيل التنبيه بالأدنى على الأعلى لا من القياس الفقهي كما ترى قلنا : لا نسلم أن هوية الإنسان غير معلومة (له ( أصلاً ) وكثرة الخلاف فيها لا تدل إلا على العسر) أي على عسر معرفتها ( وأما الامتناع أي امتناع معرفتها أو عدمها ( فلا ) تدل عليه تلك الكثرة لجواز أن تكون معلومة لصحة بعض تلك الأنظار وفساد باقيها، فلم يثبت بما ذكرتم أن هناك نظراً صحيحاً لا يفيد علماً، بل ثبت أن تميز النظر الصحيح عن غيره مشكل جداً، فيكون ذلك في الإلهيات أشكل ولا نزاع فيه
The Fifth Observatory - Third Objective: Correct Reasoning according to the Majority. The second aspect: the closest things to man, and the most deserving of being known to him in its reality and its states, is his essence (identity) which he refers to by saying: I (and indeed it is unknown, not from the aspect of assent to its existence, for that is self-evident with no dispute, but from the aspect of) conceiving it in its essence, and from the aspect of assent to its states, such as whether it is an accident or a substance, abstract or corporeal, divisible or indivisible, and other attributes (for indeed disagreement about it has become so great that with that great number it is impossible to be certain of any of the different contradictory) opinions that have been mentioned concerning that identity (as you will come across) those opinions in the investigations of the soul. So if reasoning were to yield knowledge of that identity and its attributes, the rational thinkers who reason about it would not have chosen contradictory opinions. And when the closest thing to him is like that, i.e., it does not yield even probable assent likewise, for there is no difference between them in that each of them necessitates conceiving the two extremes in the way that is the basis of judgment. So if conceiving in essence is necessary for certain assent due to the possibility that there is in the extremes themselves something that prevents that assent, then it is necessary for probable assent as well, due to the possibility that there is in the extremes themselves something that prevents assent. And it is said that probable, due to its weakness, it is permissible that conceiving by the face that is weak suffices for it, unlike certain assent. His saying: 'and the most deserving etc.': i.e., because it is always present with him, and knowledge is nothing but the presence of the perceived object with the perceiver. In it there is an indication that what is meant is the closest in perception, not in essence. His saying: 'for it is self-evident with no dispute': since everyone knows that it exists, even children and the insane. And this assent is not about specific states such that it would necessitate conceiving it in essence. So it is not objected that if certain assent is a branch of conceiving in essence according to them, how do they say this assent occurs despite the lack of conceiving in essence? His saying: 'the second: the closest things etc.': They should restrict 'things' to those absent from the senses, and lack of consistency and closeness to illusions, so that their proof is not refuted by geometrical, arithmetical, and possible things. Then, it is only valid on the assumption of submitting that the soul is not known, if its closeness were in perceptivity. And if closeness in connection does not necessitate closeness in perception — do you not see that the sensory faculty does not perceive itself — their claim does not follow. His saying: 'not from the aspect of assent to its existence for it is self-evident with no dispute' contains a discussion, because assent according to them necessitates conceiving the subject of judgment in essence, as was shown from their first proof. So if the soul is not known from the aspect of conception, how do they say it is known from the aspect of assent to existence by self-evidence? And interpreting it as self-evidence of probable assent to its existence is far-fetched. Unless the speech is built on intending to compel the opponents that it is unknown according to you, so you must admit what we mentioned. So their meaning in his saying: 'for it is self-evident with no dispute' is that it is self-evident according to you, with no dispute among you. Reasoning about it yields knowledge. So what do you think about that which is further away from it and reasoning yielding knowledge about it? This is of the type of alerting by the lower to the higher, not of the type of juristic analogy, as you see. We say: We do not accept that the identity of man is unknown to him at all. The abundance of disagreement about it does not indicate except difficulty, i.e., the difficulty of knowing it. As for impossibility, i.e., the impossibility of knowing it or its non-existence, that abundance does not indicate it, because it is possible that it is known due to the correctness of some of those reasonings and the corruption of the rest. So you have not established by what you mentioned that there is a correct reasoning that does not yield knowledge. Rather, it is established that distinguishing correct reasoning from others is very problematic, so that in the divine matters it is more problematic, and there is no dispute about that.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation structure. It presents an argument (the 'second face') that correct reasoning (nazar) cannot yield certain knowledge, using the soul as the closest object of knowledge. The argument claims that since the soul is closest to man yet its essence and attributes are heavily disputed, reasoning cannot produce certainty—otherwise rational thinkers would not contradict each other. It extends this to probable knowledge, arguing that the same requirement for essential conception applies. The commentary then clarifies each point: the phrase 'most deserving' indicates perceptual closeness, the self-evidence of existence is not affected because it does not require essential conception, and the proof must restrict 'things' to metaphysical matters to avoid counterexamples. The commentary also notes a potential circularity in the opponents' claim about self-evidence. Finally, the response ('We say') denies the premise: disagreement does not prove impossibility but only difficulty. It argues that some of the contradictory opinions may be correct, so correct reasoning may still yield knowledge; it only shows that distinguishing correct from incorrect reasoning is hard, especially in theology. This refutation maintains that reasoning can yield knowledge, though identifying correct reasoning is problematic.

Key Terms
هوية الإنسان
العسر
الامتناع
النظر الصحيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement among experts proves that a subject cannot be known.
Correction: Disagreement only indicates difficulty, not impossibility. Some reasonings may be correct even if others are not.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what does the abundance of disagreement about the soul indicate?
A) That the soul is completely unknowable
B) The difficulty of knowing the soul
C) That reasoning about the soul is always wrong
D) That the soul is not the closest object to man

Key Takeaway
Disagreement about the soul only shows that identifying correct reasoning is difficult, not that reasoning cannot yield knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological question where scholars disagree, do you conclude that the truth is unknowable? How does this passage challenge that conclusion, and what will you do differently next time?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief simply because it is disputed? What does this lesson demand you to remember about difficulty versus impossibility?
?
The passage says distinguishing correct reasoning from incorrect is problematic. How seriously do you examine your own reasoning about divine matters? What practical step can you take today to improve that process?

Two Objections to Reason's Sufficiency for Knowing God
Presents and refutes two objections against reason's sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two common objections against the sufficiency of reason for knowing God and how classical theologians answered them.

Core Idea
Reason and revelation work together; each has its role in knowing God.

This passage presents two objections that might be raised against the claim that reason alone suffices to know God, without need for a teacher. The first objection argues that if reason can verify a teacher's truthfulness about God, then it should be sufficient on its own. The second objection warns of an infinite regress if reason is insufficient. The answers clarify that reason cooperates with the teacher and that a divinely supported teacher or prophet can end the chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the two objections and their answers.
Notice the structure: each objection is introduced by 'الوجه' and answered by 'وأجيب'.
2
Identify the two objections
Distinguish the first objection (circularity argument) from the second (infinite regress argument). Observe how each is presented as a potential problem.
The first objection suggests that if reason suffices for the teacher's truthfulness, it suffices for divine matters. The second says if reason is insufficient, the chain continues endlessly.
3
Examine the answers
Study how each objection is answered. The first answer says reason and teacher cooperate; the second says a teacher may have a special divinely supported reason or end with revelation.
The answers avoid both circularity and infinite regress.
4
Understand key concepts
Focus on the cooperation between reason and teacher, and the role of revelation in ending the chain. Also note the concept of 'complete syllogisms' in the commentary.
This shows the balanced approach in Islamic theology.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this discussion matters: it defends the need for revelation while affirming the use of reason. The passage guards against both rationalism and fideism.

صدقه فيما يخبر عن الله تعالى ( بالعقل ففيه كفاية ) في معرفة الأمور الإلهية فلا حاجة إلى المعلم ( وأجيب) عن هذا الوجه ( بأنه قد يشارك العقل قوله ) في العلم بصدقه ( بأن يضع المعلم ( مقدمات يعلم بالعقل ( منها صدقه) فيكون العلم بصدق المعلم مستفاداً منهما معاً ، فلا دور ولا كفاية. الوجه ( الثاني لو لم يكف العقل) في معرفته تعالى ( لاحتاج المعلم ) فيها (إلى معلم آخر ويتسلسل وأجيب ) عنه ( بأنه قد يكفي عقله ) لكونه مؤيداً من عند الله بخاصية تقتضي كمال عقله واستقلاله في معرفته ( دون عقل غيره، أو ينتهي إلى الوحي ) أي إِن سلم احتياجه إلى معلم آخر لم يلزم التسلسل لجواز الانتهاء إلى النبي الذي يعلم الأشياء بالوحي 
قوله : ( ففيه كفاية إلخ) لأن العلم بصدق المخبر فيما أخبر به هو العلم بصدق ما أخبر به فإذا كفى نظر العقل في معرفة صدق المعلم كفي في معرفة صدق ما أخبر به، فلا يرد ما توهم من أن صدق المعلم ليس من المعارف الإلهية التي يدعى عدم استقلال العقل فيها، فلا يلزم من كفاية العقل فيه كفايته فيها .
قوله : ( بأنه قد يشارك إلخ ) جواب باختيار الشق الثالث .
قوله : ( الذي يعلم الأشياء بالوحي فهو يعلم المعارف الإلهية بطريق الضرورة من غير احتياج إلى معلم آخر .
قوله : (كما في الأقيسة (الكاملة وهي التي لا تحتاج في الإنتاج إلى قياس آخر، وهو الشكل الأول والقياس الاستثنائي المتصل .
قوله : ( مكابرة ) كيف وذلك العلم حاصل لنا مع الغفلة عن المعلم والتعليم.
قوله : ( وإن علم صدقه فيما يخبر عن الله تعالى بالعقل ففيه كفاية فيه بحث لجواز أن يعلم صدقه فيه بدليل دال على أن كلامه مطلقاً صادق، وليس صدق المعلم من المعارف الإلهية التي يدعي عدم استقلال العقل فيها لأن المراد بها الأمور الغائبة عن الحواس وصدقه مما يهتدي إليه بمشاهدة قرائن الأحوال .
His truthfulness in what he informs about God Almighty (by reason is sufficient) in knowing divine matters, so there is no need for the teacher. (And it was answered) about this aspect (that it may participate with reason in the knowledge of his truthfulness) that the teacher arranges premises known by reason (including his truthfulness), so the knowledge of the teacher's truthfulness is derived from both together, so there is no circularity and no sufficiency. (The second aspect: If reason were not sufficient) in knowing Him Almighty (then the teacher would need) therein (another teacher and it would lead to an infinite regress. And it was answered) about it (that his reason may be sufficient) by being supported from God with a special property that necessitates the perfection of his reason and its independence in knowing Him (without the reason of another, or it ends at revelation) i.e. if his need for another teacher is conceded, infinite regress does not necessarily follow because it is possible to end at the prophet who knows things by revelation. 
His saying: (for it is sufficient, etc.) because knowledge of the truthfulness of the informer in what he informs is knowledge of the truthfulness of what he informed. So if reason suffices for knowing the teacher's truthfulness, it suffices for knowing the truthfulness of what he informed. So what was imagined - that the teacher's truthfulness is not among the divine matters in which reason is claimed to be insufficient - does not refute it. So the sufficiency of reason in it does not imply its sufficiency in them. 
His saying: (that it may participate, etc.) is an answer by choosing the third branch. 
His saying: (who knows things by revelation) so he knows divine matters by necessary means without need for another teacher. 
His saying: (as in the complete syllogisms) which are those that do not need another syllogism for production, that is the first figure and the connected hypothetical syllogism. 
His saying: (obstinacy) how? for this knowledge is present in us while being heedless of the teacher and teaching. 
His saying: (And if his truthfulness in what he informs about God Almighty by reason is sufficient, there is a discussion on it) because it is possible that his truthfulness in it is known by a proof indicating that his speech is entirely truthful, and the teacher's truthfulness is not among the divine matters in which reason is claimed to be insufficient because what is meant by them is matters absent from the senses, and his truthfulness is something guided to by observing circumstantial evidence.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two objections against the claim that reason alone is sufficient for knowing God, without need for a teacher. The first objection argues that if reason suffices to verify the truthfulness of a teacher regarding God, then it suffices for knowing divine matters themselves, so no teacher is needed. The answer explains that reason can work together with the teacher: the teacher provides premises that reason uses to confirm his truthfulness, so knowledge comes from both, not from reason alone, and there is no circularity or complete sufficiency of reason alone. The second objection argues that if reason were insufficient, then any teacher would require another teacher ad infinitum. The answer states that it is possible that a particular teacher's reason is supported directly by God with a special property, making it independent, or the chain ends at a prophet who receives revelation. The commentary glosses further explain these points: the first gloss clarifies that knowledge of the informer's truthfulness is equivalent to knowledge of what is informed, so reason's sufficiency in one implies sufficiency in the other, but the objection is avoided because the teacher's truthfulness is not among the 'divine matters' claimed to require revelation. The second gloss notes that the answer chooses the third possibility (cooperation). The third gloss explains that the prophet knows by necessity from revelation and is independent. The fourth gloss defines 'complete syllogisms' as those that produce knowledge without needing another syllogism, giving the first figure and conditional syllogism as examples. The fifth gloss argues against the claim that such knowledge requires a teacher, stating that we have this knowledge even while heedless of a teacher. The final gloss discusses that even if one says reason suffices for verifying the teacher's truthfulness, there is a discussion because the teacher's truthfulness might be known through a proof that his speech is always truthful, and truthfulness is not a hidden divine matter but can be inferred from circumstantial evidence.

القياس الكامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some suppose that the answer to the first objection accepts the sufficiency of reason alone, eliminating the need for a teacher.
Correction: The answer actually affirms that reason and teacher cooperate; knowledge comes from both together, not from reason alone.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, how is knowledge of the teacher's truthfulness obtained?
A) By reason alone without any teacher
B) By the teacher's own statement without reason
C) By reason using premises provided by the teacher
D) By revelation without any reasoning

Key Takeaway
Reason is not entirely independent for knowing God; it cooperates with a teacher, and the chain of teachers ultimately ends with revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own reason is insufficient to know God without a teacher, why do you still rely on your own opinions in matters of faith? Have you sincerely sought out a qualified teacher who can guide you? If not, what is holding you back from humbling yourself to learn from those whom Allah has sent?
?
The passage teaches that even prophets receive knowledge through revelation. Have you truly internalized that your intellect cannot reach certainty about divine matters without divine guidance? In what specific area of your life are you still trusting your own reasoning over what has been revealed?
?
The second answer offers two ways to avoid infinite regress: a specially supported teacher or a prophet. Which category do you think applies to your own situation? If you are neither a prophet nor have a divinely perfected mind, then you must rely on revelation through teachers. Are you actively submitting to that chain, or are you trying to bypass it?

The Relied-Upon Argument of Necessity
Author presents the necessity argument and refutes teacher requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relied-upon argument that correct definitive premises arranged validly yield certain knowledge of God by necessity, and why claiming a teacher is required is obstinacy.

Core Idea
Correct reasoning necessarily yields knowledge—no teacher needed for certainty.

This lesson presents the core argument for the sufficiency of reason in knowing God. The author begins by asserting that anyone who knows the correct premises in a valid syllogistic form will necessarily obtain certain knowledge, using the classic example of contingency and cause. He then dismisses the objection that such knowledge requires a teacher as sheer obstinacy, though acknowledging that a teacher facilitates the process.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Notice the direct claim of necessity.
2
Identify the argument structure
Find the premise, the conclusion, and the claim of necessity. The example is a syllogism.
The example is: world is contingent, every contingent has a cause, so world has a cause.
3
Consider the teacher objection
Notice what the author says about the claim that a teacher is needed. He calls it plain obstinacy but acknowledges a teacher eases the matter.

Source Text

في الدنيا ( كالمأخوذ من غير النبي فإنه لا يتم به الإيمان ( ألا ترى إلى قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله مع أن كثيراً كانوا يقولون : بالتوحيد، لكنهم لما لم يأخذوا ذلك منه ما كان يقبل قولهم (لم يرد عليه ذلك ) المعتمد الذي ذكرناه ) وطريق الرد عليه إجماع من قبلهم من هذه الأمة (على) حصول ( النجاة ) بالمعرفة الحاصلة بلا معلم ) والآيات الآمرة بالنظر في معرفة الله سبحانه ( متكررة متكثرة في معرض الهداية إلى سبيل النجاة من غير إيجاب التعلم) فدلت دلالة ظاهرة على أن التعلم غير محتاج إليه في النجاة، فهذه الآيات طريق آخر للرد عليهم 
قوله : ( ألا ترى إلخ) هذا التنوير على تقدير أن يراد من لا إله إلا الله معناه أعني التوحيد أي حتى يأخذوا التوحيد مني، وأما على تقدير أن يكون المراد منه تمام الكلمة بأن يراد لا إله إلا الله إلى آخره أو يجعل لا إله إلا الله علماً لتمام الكلمة فلا تنوير كما لا يخفى .
قوله : ( وطريق الرد عليه إلخ ) هذا إنما يتم إذا كان الخصم معترفاً بالإجماع إلا أن يراد الرد على سبيل التحقيق دون الإلزام.
قوله : ( فدلت دلالة ظاهرة إلخ فيه أن الآيات الآمرة إنما علم من طريق التعليم من النبي، فيكون العقل مفيداً للعلم بمشاركة المعلم فتدبر.
قوله : ( حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله قيل : معناه حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله لا شك في عدم انتهاء المقاتلة بقبول التوحيد فقط بدون تصديقه عليه السلام بكونه عليه السلام رسول الله فاكتفي بالبعض للظهور ، فحينئذ لا دلالة على أن المقاتلة إنما كانت بسبب عدم أخذهم التوحيد منه ، وقيل : أخذه والقول به من حيث إنه متلقي منه عليه السلام يدل على تصديقه في جميع ما أمر به، فلهذا انتهى المقاتلة به .
قوله : ( وطريق الرد عليه إلخ وقد يرد أيضا بأن ذلك المعلم هو النبي عليه السلام وكفى به إماماً ومرشداً إلى قيام الساعة من غير احتياج في كل عصر إلى إمام يجدد طريق الإرشاد، والتعليم وتتوقف النجاة على متابعته والاعتراف بإمامته .
(And the relied-upon) in refuting them (is the claim of necessity, for whoever knows the correct definitive premises (appropriate for knowing God Almighty in a form that necessitates) the result (with necessary entailment) as in the complete syllogisms) obtains knowledge with certainty, like our saying: the world is contingent, and everything contingent has an effecter, so the world has an effecter. And what is said that knowledge of those premises in that form cannot be obtained except through a teacher is plain obstinacy. Yes, if there is a teacher, the matter is easier. (And this) relied-upon argument (only becomes a proof against those who said: reasoning does not yield knowledge without a teacher in knowing God Almighty. (As for those who said: (it yields knowledge, but the premises for proving the Maker and His attributes necessitate knowledge of their conclusions, but (knowledge obtained by reasoning alone does not benefit salvation in the Hereafter, and faith is not completed by it) in the world) like what is taken from other than the Prophet, for faith is not completed by it. (Do you not see his saying, peace be upon him: I was commanded to fight people until they say 'la ilaha illa Allah', although many used to say: by Tawhid, but since they did not take that from him, their saying was not accepted.) (That) the relied-upon argument we mentioned (does not refute it). (And the way to refute it is the consensus of those before them from this nation) on (obtaining) salvation (by knowledge obtained without a teacher). (And the verses commanding reflection in knowing God Almighty) are frequent and numerous in the context of guidance to the path of salvation, without requiring learning, so they indicate clearly that learning is not needed for salvation. So these verses are another way of refuting them. 
His saying: (Do you not see, etc.) This clarification is on the assumption that what is meant by 'la ilaha illa Allah' is its meaning, i.e., Tawhid, so that they take Tawhid from me. But on the assumption that what is meant is the complete phrase, i.e., 'la ilaha illa Allah' to its end, or that 'la ilaha illa Allah' is taken as a marker for the complete phrase, then there is no clarification, as is not hidden. 
His saying: (And the way to refute it, etc.) This is only valid if the opponent acknowledges consensus, unless it is meant as a refutation in a definitive way without being binding. 
His saying: (So it indicates clearly, etc.) In it is that the commanding verses are only known through the teaching of the Prophet, so reason yields knowledge with the participation of the teacher, so ponder. 
His saying: (until they say 'la ilaha illa Allah') It is said: its meaning is until they say 'la ilaha illa Allah, Muhammad Rasul Allah'. There is no doubt that fighting does not end by accepting Tawhid only without attesting that he, peace be upon him, is the Messenger of Allah. So the part is mentioned for the sake of clarity. In that case, there is no indication that fighting was only because they did not take Tawhid from him. And it is said: Taking it and uttering it as received from him, peace be upon him, indicates attesting to him in all he commanded, so fighting ended with it. 
His saying: (And the way to refute it, etc.) It may also be refuted that the teacher is the Prophet, peace be upon him, and he suffices as an imam and guide until the Day of Judgment, without need in every age for an imam to renew the path of guidance and teaching, and salvation depends on following him and acknowledging his imamate.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the author's relied-upon argument (al-mu'tamad) for the sufficiency of reason in knowing God, and then addresses an objection that such knowledge does not save in the Hereafter. The argument is the claim of necessity: anyone who knows correct, definitive premises arranged in a valid syllogistic form (like the complete syllogisms of the first figure or conditional syllogism) necessarily obtains certain knowledge of the conclusion, for example, the world is contingent, every contingent has a cause, so the world has a cause. Claiming that such knowledge requires a teacher is plain obstinacy, though a teacher makes it easier. This argument only refutes those who deny that reasoning yields knowledge without a teacher. As for those who admit that reasoning yields knowledge but argue that it does not suffice for salvation, the author refutes them by pointing to the consensus of the early Muslim community that salvation is obtained by knowledge acquired without a teacher, and by the many Qur'anic verses commanding reflection, which are presented as guidance to salvation without requiring a formal teacher. The commentary glosses discuss the hadith about fighting until people say 'la ilaha illa Allah', explaining that it could mean taking Tawhid from the Prophet himself, or the full testimony. They also note that the consensus argument requires the opponent to accept consensus, and that the commanding verses themselves are learned through the Prophet's teaching, implying reason's cooperation with a teacher. Another possible response is that the Prophet suffices as a guide until the end of time, so no additional teacher is needed in every age for salvation.

Key Terms
المعتمد
الضرورة
المقدمات الصحيحة القطعية
القياس الكامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That correct reasoning cannot produce certain knowledge without a teacher.
Correction: The author argues that valid necessary premises yield certain knowledge by necessity, and a teacher is not required.

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon argument's claim?
A) Knowledge of God requires a teacher.
B) Correct premises in a valid form yield certain knowledge by necessity.
C) Only the Prophet's teaching can give certainty.
D) Reasoning alone is insufficient.

Key Takeaway
Valid reasoning from correct premises produces certain knowledge of God without needing a teacher.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own belief in God, do you lean more on arguments you've reasoned through or on the authority of teachers and scholars? Which gives you greater certainty—and why?
?
Imagine you had no teacher, no books, and no community—only your mind. Would you still be able to arrive at knowledge of God through reasoning alone? If you hesitate, what does that hesitation reveal about your trust in the rational faculty Allah gave you?
?
The author calls the claim that we need a teacher 'plain obstinacy.' Is there any area of your faith where you have been obstinate—refusing to follow a clear argument because it would disrupt a comfortable assumption? Will you ask Allah for the courage to follow the truth wherever it leads?

Salvation Through Reason: Refuting the Objection
Author presents the salvation objection and refutes via consensus and verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that reasoning alone does not save in the Hereafter, and the author's refutation using consensus and Qur'anic verses.

Core Idea
Reasoning alone saves—consensus and Qur'anic commands confirm it.

After establishing that reasoning yields certain knowledge, the author now addresses a different opponent: one who accepts that reasoning gives knowledge but claims it does not suffice for salvation. This lesson presents that objection and the author's response. He argues that the consensus of the early community and numerous Qur'anic verses commanding reflection prove that knowledge gained without a formal teacher leads to salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice the objection: reasoning yields knowledge but not salvation. The hadith is used to argue that faith must come from the Prophet.
2
Identify the first refutation
The author points to the consensus of earlier generations that salvation is attained by knowledge gained without a teacher.
3
Identify the second refutation
The author cites Qur'anic verses that command reflection and guide to salvation without requiring a teacher.

Two Objections of the Mulhida and the First Refutation
The author presents two arguments of the Mulhida and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two common objections raised by the Mulhida against the sufficiency of reason for theological knowledge, and the author's refutation of the first objection.

Core Idea
Disagreement among thinkers does not disprove reason—it only shows some reasonings are flawed.

The Mulhida (materialists/atheists) put forward two arguments claiming that reason alone cannot lead to knowledge of God. The first argues that the widespread disagreement among rationalists proves reason's inadequacy. The second observes that even in simple subjects people need teachers, so how much more in theology. In this passage, the author presents these objections and offers a pointed refutation of the first, while the second is stated without immediate counter (the text breaks off). Let's examine these challenges and the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two arguments introduced with 'لهم وجهان' and the refutation beginning with 'قلنا'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first argument and its refutation
The first argument: disagreement among rationalists shows reason is insufficient. The refutation: disagreement arises because some reasonings are invalid, not because reason itself is flawed. Correct reasoning still yields knowledge.
The author concedes that the disagreement indicates difficulty in distinguishing correct from invalid reasoning.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument: people need teachers even in easy sciences like grammar and prosody, so they certainly need teachers in the difficult science of theology.
The text ends before the refutation is given.
4
Reflect on the missing refutation
Consider what the author's likely response would be based on the principles of kalam. Hint: The need for a teacher does not prove reason's insufficiency; it shows that teachers facilitate learning, but reason can still attain knowledge independently.
5
Connect to the broader lesson
These objections and refutations demonstrate that the Mulhida's challenges do not undermine the validity of correct reasoning. The debate continues in later sections.

لهم ) أي للملاحدة ( وجهان الأول أنه كثر الخلاف بين العقلاء في المعرفة كثرة لا تحصى ) ولو كان العقل ) باستعمال النظر ( كافياً ) فيها ( لما كان ) الأمر ( كذلك ) بل كانت العقلاء الناظرون فيها متفقين على عقيدة واحدة (قلنا ) ذلك ( الخلاف) إنما وقع ( لكون بعض تلك الأنظار الصادرة عنهم فاسدة ) فترتب عليها عقائد باطلة، وذلك لا ينفعكم ولا يضرنا ) فإن المفيد للعلم ) عندنا ( إنما هو النظر الصحيح لا الفاسد نعم دل الاختلاف المذكور على صعوبة التمييز هناك صحيح النظر وفاسده، وهو مسلم ( الثاني نرى الناس محتاجين) إلى معلم ( في العلوم الضعيفة ) التي يكتفى فيها بأدنى نظر ) كالنحو والصرف والعروض ( لا يستغنون فيها عن المعلم فكيف لا يحتاجون إليه ( في العلوم العويصة التي هي أبعد العلوم
(To them) i.e. the Mulhida (are two aspects. The first: that disagreement among rational thinkers concerning knowledge is countless. If reason) by using reasoning (were sufficient) in it (then the matter would not be) such, (but rather the rational thinkers who reflected on it would agree on one doctrine. We say) that (disagreement) only occurred (because some of their reasonings are invalid) so false doctrines resulted, and that does not benefit you nor harm us. For what yields knowledge) according to us (is only correct reasoning, not invalid. Indeed, the mentioned disagreement indicates the difficulty of distinguishing correct reasoning from invalid, and that is conceded. (The second: we see people needing) a teacher (in weak sciences) where the slightest reasoning suffices, like grammar, morphology, and prosody; (they do not dispense with a teacher in them, so how would they not need him) in the complex sciences that are the farthest of sciences
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the Mulhida (atheists or materialists) against the claim that reason alone suffices for knowledge of God, along with their refutations. The first argument is that rational thinkers disagree immensely about knowledge, and if reason were sufficient, they would agree on one doctrine. The refutation is that the disagreement arises because some reasonings are invalid, leading to false beliefs. This does not disprove the efficacy of correct reasoning. The author concedes that the disagreement shows it is difficult to distinguish correct from invalid reasoning, but that does not harm their position. The second argument is that people need teachers even in easy sciences like grammar, morphology, and prosody, so they must need teachers even more in the difficult science of theology. This argument is presented without a refutation in the text provided (the page ends). The implied response is that the need for a teacher in simple sciences does not prove that reason is insufficient; rather, it shows that teachers facilitate learning, but reason can still attain knowledge independently when properly used.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement among rational thinkers proves that reason is incapable of attaining truth in theology.
Correction: Disagreement only shows that some reasonings are invalid; correct reasoning leads to knowledge, as the author states: 'Indeed, the mentioned disagreement indicates the difficulty of distinguishing correct reasoning from invalid, and that is conceded.'

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the disagreement among rational thinkers indicate?
A) That reason is completely insufficient for knowledge.
B) That correct reasoning is easy and everyone agrees.
C) That it is difficult to distinguish correct reasoning from invalid reasoning.
D) That there is no correct reasoning at all.

Key Takeaway
The Mulhida's objections are answered by showing that disagreement stems from faulty reasoning, not reason itself, and the need for teachers does not negate reason's capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's distinction between valid and invalid reasoning change your perspective? Commit to evaluating each reasoning on its own merits rather than dismissing the possibility of truth.
?
The author concedes that disagreement highlights the difficulty of distinguishing correct from invalid reasoning. Does this difficulty discourage you from seeking truth? How will you actively train yourself to better discern sound arguments from flawed ones in your own study?
?
Reflect on a time when you were confident in a conclusion but later realized your reasoning was flawed. How can you apply the lesson's principle that only correct reasoning yields knowledge to avoid similar mistakes in your pursuit of understanding God?

The First Objection: Disagreement Among Rational People
Presents the atheists' objection from widespread disagreement and the response distinguishing correct from corrupt consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from widespread disagreement is answered by distinguishing between correct and corrupt consideration.

Core Idea
Disagreement arises from corrupt reasoning, not from correct consideration.

In this passage, the atheists raise an objection: if rational consideration were sufficient for knowledge, all rational people would agree, but they disagree. The response shows that only corrupt consideration leads to disagreement, while correct consideration yields uniform knowledge. This sets a key methodological distinction in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the atheists' claim that disagreement among rational people disproves the sufficiency of rational consideration.
Notice the logical structure: if X then Y, but not Y, therefore not X.
2
Read the response
Read the reply that disagreement stems from corrupt consideration, not correct consideration.
The response does not deny disagreement but reinterprets its cause.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the text concedes that disagreement shows the difficulty of distinguishing correct from corrupt consideration, yet maintains that correct consideration is sufficient for knowledge.
This concession is important for humility in theological methodology.

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of the atheists against the sufficiency of correct consideration (النظر الصحيح) and the response. The atheists argue: if correct consideration were sufficient for knowledge, then all rational people using it would agree on one creed, but in fact there is abundant disagreement. The response distinguishes between two types of consideration: correct (صحيح) and corrupt (فاسد). Disagreement arises only from corrupt considerations; correct consideration yields uniform knowledge. The passage acknowledges that the existence of disagreement shows how difficult it is to distinguish correct from corrupt consideration, but this difficulty does not negate the sufficiency of correct consideration itself—it is simply conceded (مسلم).

Misconception: Disagreements among theologians prove that reason is unreliable in religious matters.

Disagreement among thinkers is not evidence against the sufficiency of correct reasoning; it only shows the prevalence of corrupt reasoning.

Ash'ari Doctrine: Knowledge from Reasoning by Custom
Explanation of the Ash'ari view that knowledge occurs by custom after correct reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that knowledge from correct reasoning occurs by God's custom (ʿāda), based on the principles of direct divine causation and absolute divine freedom.

Core Idea
In the Ash'ari view, there is no necessary causal link — only God's custom.

This lesson introduces the first of three major schools on how correct reasoning yields knowledge. The Ash'ari school, founded by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, holds that knowledge follows reasoning by God's custom (ʿāda). This is based on two core principles: all possible things are directly from God, and God is absolutely free. We'll explore how this leads to a denial of natural causation and defines customary versus extraordinary events.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the concepts.
Notice that the passage begins by stating this is the first of three schools.
2
Identify the two foundational principles
Find where the author explains why al-Ash'ari holds this view. Look for the two principles: (1) possibles are directly attributed to God, (2) God is powerful and free, meaning no necessity and no causal connections.
These principles are the basis for the entire Ash'ari occasionalist worldview.
3
Understand the concept of custom
Notice that the 'custom' refers to God's regular pattern of creating events in sequence, like burning after fire contact. There is no causal efficacy in the fire; God creates both.
Custom does not imply necessity; it is simply God's habitual way of acting.
4
Distinguish customary from extraordinary events
The passage explains that if an action occurs regularly, it is called customary; if rarely or once, it is extraordinary or rare. This classification applies to all events, including knowledge after reasoning.
This prepares the ground for miracles (kharq al-ʿāda).
5
Apply to knowledge after reasoning
The passage concludes that knowledge after reasoning is a possible, created thing, and God is its only cause, created by His will without necessity, and it is permanent or frequent (i.e., customary).
This is the key point: knowledge follows reasoning by custom, not by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في كيفية إفادة النظر الصحيح للعلم بالمنظور فيه والمذاهب التي يعتد بها ثلاثة مبنية على أصول مختلفة الأول مذهب الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري إنه أي حصول العلم عقيب النظر بالعادة وإنما ذهب إلى ذلك بناء على أن الممكنات مستندة عنده إلى الله سبحانه ابتداء بلا واسطة وعلى أنه تعالى قادر مختار فلا يجب عنه صدور شيء منها ولا يجب عليه أيضاً ولا علاقة بوجه بين الحوادث المتعاقبة إلا بإجراء العادة بخلق بعضها عقيب بعض كالإحراق عقيب مماسة النار والري بعد شرب الماء فليس للماسة والشرب مدخل في وجود الإحراق والري بل الكل واقع بقدرته واختياره تعالى فله أن يوجد المماسة بدون الإحراق وأن يوجد الإحراق بدون المماسة وكذا الحال في سائر الأفعال وإذا تكرر صدور فعل وكان دائماً أو أكثرياً يقال إنه فعله بإجراء العادة وإذا لم يتكرر أو تكرر قليلاً فهو خارق للعادة أو نادر ولا شك أن العلم بعد النظر ممكن حادث محتاج إلى المؤثر ولا مؤثر إلا الله تعالى فهو فعله الصادر عنه بلا وجوب منه ولا عليه وهو دائمي أو أكثري
On how correct reasoning yields knowledge of the object of consideration. The schools that are relied upon are three, built on different principles. The first is the school of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that knowledge occurs after reasoning by custom. He adopted that based on the principle that possibles, according to him, are directly attributed to God, may He be exalted, without mediation, and that He, may He be exalted, is powerful and free, so nothing from them is necessary from Him, nor is it necessary upon Him, and there is no connection at all between successive events except by the operation of custom, by creating some after others, like burning after contact with fire and quenching after drinking water; so contact and drinking have no role in the existence of burning and quenching, rather all occurs by His power and choice, may He be exalted, so He can create contact without burning and burn without contact. The same holds for all actions. And if an action occurs repeatedly and is permanent or frequent, it is said that He did it by custom. And if it does not recur or recurs rarely, it is extraordinary or rare. There is no doubt that knowledge after reasoning is a possible, originated thing in need of an effecter, and there is no effecter but God, may He be exalted; so it is His act issuing from Him without necessity from Him nor upon Him, and it is permanent or frequent.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the first of three schools on how correct reasoning yields knowledge: the Ash'ari school. It states that knowledge follows reasoning by custom ('āda). The Ash'ari view is based on two principles: (1) All possible things are directly from God without intermediate causes, and (2) God is absolutely powerful and free, so no event is necessary from Him nor upon Him, and there is no causal connection between successive events. The only link is God's custom of creating one after another, e.g., burning after fire-contact. Fire-contact does not cause burning; God creates both. He could create contact without burning or burning without contact. When an event occurs regularly, it is called customary; if it occurs rarely or once, it is extraordinary (khāriq li-l-‘āda). Knowledge after reasoning is a possible, created thing, and God is its only effecter, created by His will without necessity.

نادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view of custom denies all regularity in the world.
Correction: The Ash'ari view affirms regular patterns as God's custom while rejecting necessary causal efficacy. Custom means God habitually creates events in a certain order, but He can break this pattern (extraordinary events).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari school as presented, why is knowledge after reasoning called 'by custom'?

B) Because God creates knowledge after reasoning repeatedly by His free will, not because of a necessary connection.
C) Because human reasoning has causal power.
D) Because it is purely accidental.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school teaches that knowledge after reasoning is created by God's custom, not by any necessary connection, reflecting His absolute power and freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you pray for something, do you expect God to act according to His custom, or do you limit Him by your expectations? How does this passage challenge your understanding of God's freedom?
?
If knowledge after reasoning is a gift from God by custom, not a necessary result, how does that affect your humility in seeking knowledge? Are you grateful or proud of your understanding?

Imam al-Razi's View: Rational Necessity of Knowledge from Investigation
Imam al-Razi's view: knowledge necessarily follows investigation rationally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's doctrine that knowledge necessarily follows investigation by rational necessity, not by generation, and how it differs from customary necessity.

Core Idea
Investigation compels knowledge rationally, not by generation.

Imam al-Razi presents a distinct position on how investigation (nazar) leads to knowledge. While some thinkers held that knowledge is generated from investigation by customary necessity, al-Razi argues it follows by rational necessity. This passage states his view, traces its roots, and provides an intuitive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: wujub aqli (rational necessity) versus wujub adi (customary necessity).
2
Distinguish the two necessities
Identify where the passage distinguishes rational necessity from customary necessity. Understand that al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni are interpreted as meaning customary, not rational necessity.
3
Reflect on the proof
The passage gives a brief proof: anyone who knows the world is changing and that every changing thing is originated, and combines these premises, necessarily knows the conclusion. This shows rational compulsion.
The proof is cut off in the source but the idea is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا مذهب آخر اختاره الإمام الرازي وهو أن العلم الحاصل عقيب النظر واجب لازم حصوله عقيبه عقلاً غير متولد منه. قيل: أخذ هذا المذهب من القاضي الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين، حيث قالا باستلزام النظر للعلم على سبيل الوجوب من غير توليد، ورد بأن مرادهما الوجوب العادي دون العقلي. أما وجوبه عقلاً فلانا نعلم ضرورة وبديهة أن من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث واجتمع في ذهنه هاتان المقدمتان على هذه الهيئة.
And here is another doctrine chosen by Imam al-Razi, which is that the knowledge obtained after investigation is necessary, its occurrence after it is rationally necessary, not generated from it. It was said: he took this doctrine from al-Qadi al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn, for they said that investigation entails knowledge by way of necessity without generation. It was objected that they meant customary necessity, not rational necessity. As for its rational necessity, we know by necessity and intuition that one who knows that the world is changing and that every changing thing is originated, and these two premises come together in his mind in this manner.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Imam al-Razi: that knowledge resulting from investigation (nazar) is rationally necessary (wājib ʿaqlī) – i.e., it inevitably follows without being generated (mutawallid) from the investigation. It is noted that this position is attributed to al-Qadi al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who held that investigation necessitates knowledge necessarily, but a reply clarifies that they meant customary necessity (wujūb ʿādī), not rational necessity. The proof of rational necessity is intuitive: anyone who knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is originated, and combines these two premises in his mind, necessarily gains the knowledge that the world is originated. This shows the rational compulsion of the conclusion given the premises.

Misconception: All scholars who say knowledge follows investigation necessarily mean rational necessity.
Correction: Al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni meant customary necessity, not rational necessity, as clarified in this passage.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi asserts that knowledge from investigation is rationally necessary, not merely by custom, as proven by intuitive reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you commit to following the truth wherever reason leads you today?

Imam al-Razi on the Necessity of Knowledge from Reflection
Al-Iji presents Imam al-Razi's view on knowledge following reflection necessarily, with proof and denial of generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's claim that knowledge necessarily follows reflection with rational necessity, and why he denies that it is generated from reflection.

Core Idea
Knowledge after reflection is rationally necessary, not merely customary.

In this passage, al-Iji introduces a distinctive view held by Imam al-Razi on the relationship between reflection and knowledge. Al-Razi argues that when the mind properly assembles premises, the conclusion follows necessarily—not by custom but by rational necessity. He also insists that this knowledge is not generated from reflection, but comes directly from God. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key claims
Read the passage and note that al-Razi makes two core assertions: knowledge after reflection is rationally necessary, and it is not generated from reflection.
The Arabic uses 'wajib 'aqli' and 'ghayr mutawallid'.
2
Examine the proof of rational necessity
Find the syllogism example: 'the world is changing, everything changing is temporal, therefore the world is temporal.' Al-Razi says it is impossible not to know this when the premises are in mind.
This is a first-figure syllogism, but he says it works for all figures.
3
Understand the denial of generation
Al-Razi argues that all possible events are traced directly to God, so knowledge after reflection is by God's power, not by the servant's power. Reflection does not generate knowledge.
This opposes the Ash'ari theory of tawlid (generation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا مذهب آخر اختاره الإمام الرازي وهو أنه) يعني العلم الحاصل عقيب النظر (واجب) لازم حصوله عقيبه عقلاً ( غير متولد منه قيل : أخذ هذا المذهب من القاضي الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين، حيث قالا : باستلزام النظر للعلم على سبيل الوجوب من غير توليد ،ورد بأن مرادهما الوجوب العادي دون العقلي ( أما وجوبه ) عقلا فلانا نعلم ضرورة وبديهة أن من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث واجتمع في ذهنه هاتان المقدمتان على هذه الهيئة امتنع أن لا يعلم أن العالم حادث) وهذا الاستدلال جار في سائر الأشكال والأقيسة، إذا اعتبرت مأخوذة مع ما يحتاج إليها من بياناتها ( وأما إنه غير متولد ) من النظر ( فلاستناد جميع الممكنات) والحوادث (إلى الله تعالى (ابتداء فيكون العلم عقيب النظر واقعاً بقدرته ، لا بقدرة العبد قوله : ( امتنع أن لا يعلم إلخ ضرورة اندراج الأصغر في الأوسط والأوسط في الأكبر. قوله : ( وهذا الاستدلال إلخ فلا يرد أن الاستدلال المذكور إنما يجري في الشكل الأول قوله : ( واقعاً بقدرته) لا ابتداء لا تولداً من شيء. قوله : ( لا بقدرة العبد لا ابتداء ولا بواسطة النظر الصادر منه فلا يكون النظر مولداً له فتدبر فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام
And here is another doctrine chosen by Imam al-Razi, namely that the knowledge that occurs after reflection is necessary, its occurrence after it is rationally necessary, not generated from it. It was said: This doctrine was taken from al-Qadi al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn, for they said that reflection entails knowledge necessarily, without generation. But it was replied that by necessity they meant customary necessity, not rational. As for its rational necessity, we know by necessary and intuitive knowledge that whoever knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is temporal, and these two premises come together in his mind in this form, it is impossible that he does not know that the world is temporal. This inference applies to all figures and syllogisms when taken together with the data they need. And as for its not being generated from reflection, it is because all possible things and events are traced back to God Almighty directly, so the knowledge after reflection occurs by His power, not by the power of the servant. His saying: (It is impossible that he does not know etc.) by necessity of the subsumption of the minor under the middle and the middle under the major. His saying: (And this inference etc.) so it is not objected that the mentioned inference only applies to the first figure. His saying: (Occurs by His power) directly, not generated from anything. His saying: (not by the power of the servant) not directly nor through the mediation of the reflection issuing from him, so reflection is not generative of it; so ponder, for here feet have slipped.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's view on the relationship between reflection and knowledge. He holds that knowledge necessarily follows reflection (wajib lazim) and is not generated (ghayr mutawallid) from it. The text notes that this view was attributed to al-Qadi al-Baqillani and Imam al-Juwayni, who said reflection entails knowledge necessarily, but it is replied that they meant customary necessity ('adi) rather than rational ('aqli). Imam al-Razi then argues for rational necessity: if one knows that the world is changing and that every changing thing is temporal, and these two premises are present in the mind, it is impossible not to know that the world is temporal. This syllogism works for all logical figures. As for non-generation, he argues that all possible things are traced directly to God; knowledge after reflection occurs by God's power, not by the servant's power. The marginal comments explain the logical necessity (subsumption of terms) and clarify that the knowledge is directly from God, not generated by reflection, warning against misunderstanding.

الوجوب العقلي
الوجوب العادي
التوليد
القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Razi denies any causal link between reflection and knowledge; he affirms a necessary link but denies generation (tawlid).
Correction: Al-Razi holds that reflection does not generate knowledge, but knowledge necessarily follows reflection by rational necessity, directly from God.

Checkpoint

A) Al-Razi denies any necessity between reflection and knowledge.
B) Al-Razi affirms rational necessity while they affirmed customary necessity.
C) Al-Razi claims knowledge is generated from reflection.
D) Al-Razi says knowledge is impossible after reflection.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi argues that knowledge after reflection is rationally necessary and directly from God, not generated by the act of reflection.
