The Condition of Origination Must Be Removed
Al-Jurjani states the thesis that valid reasoning requires removing the condition of origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that reasoning leads to necessary knowledge of God requires removing the condition of origination, and how this allows for tawlīd while preserving divine power.

Core Idea
Removing the condition of direct origination reconciles rational necessity with divine free agency.

Al-Jurjani begins by presenting the problem: both philosophers and Mu‘tazila claim that valid reasoning yields necessary knowledge of God, but they differ on how. The solution, he argues, lies in dropping the requirement that all things depend directly on God. This lesson explores that key insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains both a thesis and a brief justification.
2
Identify the problem
The passage states that both philosophers (who say God is a necessitating cause) and Mu‘tazila (who say no obligation upon God) claim that reasoning yields knowledge of God. What is the issue with these views?
The problem is that these views seem to conflict with divine free agency.
3
Understand the solution: remove the condition of origination
Al-Jurjani says that for this reasoning to be valid, we must remove the condition that all things depend on God directly (ibtidā'). This means we allow some effects to be generated from others (tawlīd).
This does not deny God's ultimate power; it only denies that every effect must directly originate from Him.
4
Reflect on tawlīd and free agency
The passage says that the necessity of some actions from others does not contradict God's being a free agent. Why? Because God can still choose to bring about the causes.
Think of God as the ultimate cause who ordains a causal chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الرابع : كيفية إفادة النظر الصحيح للعلم عن الله كما تزعمه الحكماء القائلون بأنه موجب لا مختار ) ولا ) وجوب (عليه) أيضاً كما تزعمه المعتزلة، وإنما يصح إذا حذف قيد الابتداء في استناد الأشياء إلى الله تخلفه عنه عقلاً، سبحانه وجوز أن يكون لبعض آثاره مدخل في بعض بحيث يمتنع فيكون بعضها متولداً عن بعض وإن كان الكل واقعاً بقدرته كما تقول المعتزلة في أفعال العباد الصادرة عنهم بقدرتهم، ووجوب بعض الأفعال عن بعض لا ينافي قدرة كونه مختاراً لوجوب العلم بعد النظر بمعنى اللزوم العقلي لمنافاته للازمه. قوله : (إذ لا وجوب إلخ) استدلال على انتفاء الوجوب عليه مطلقاً بانتفاء فردية المنحصر فيهما، فلا مصادرة وليس دليلاً لقوله : لا يصح مع القول إلخ أما أولاً فلأنه بعد ملاحظة الاستناد ابتداء، وكونه مختاراً لا يحتاج الحكم بعدم صحة المذهب المذكور إلى دليل، وأما ثانياً فلانه لا ينفي الوجوب من النظر وإنما ينفي الوجوب له كما عرفت فلا يتم التقريب. قوله : ( كما تزعمه المعتزلة) بناء على القول بالحسن والقبح العقليين. قوله: (وإنما يصح إلخ) حصر الصحة على حذف قيد الابتداء، إذ كونه تعالى فاعلاً مختاراً بالمعنى المذكور مما اتفق عليه أهل السنة بخلاف الاستناد ابتداء فإنه قول بعض الأشعرية على ما صرح به في شرح المقاصد . ابتداء . قوله : ( لبعض آثاره (مدخل أي في التأثير بأن يكون علة موجبة له . قوله : ( ووجوب إلخ) يعني أنه قادر مختار فيه بواسطة ما يوجبه وإن لم يكن مختاراً فيه القول بأنه تعالى قادر ،مختار وإنه لا يجب عليه شيء والتقريب ،ظاهر فإن هذا المذهب يشتمل على القول بالوجوب فإما عنه وإما عليه قوله : ( وإنما يصح إذا حذف قيد الابتداء إنما اختار في صحة المذهب المذكور حذف قيد الابتداء، بل حصر الصحة فيه، فلم يذكر حذف أحد القيدين الباقيين، مع أنه ذكر أولاً أنه لا يصح مع القول بالأمور الثلاثة بناء على أن القول باستناد الجميع إليه تعالى ابتداء المعنى المراد ها هنا يستلزم القول بأنه قادر مختار كما سيشير إليه الشارح في بحث القدم، وكذا يستلزم سلب الوجوب عليه تعالى لأن هذا الوجوب متفرع على قاعدة التحسين والتقبيح، وهذه القاعدة تفضي إلى القول باستناد بعض الأشياء إليه تعالى بواسطة بعض كالثواب بواسطة الطاعة، فحذف كل من القيدين الأخيرين يستلزم حذف قيد الابتداء ومما ينبغي أن يعلم أنه أراد ها هنا بالاستناد ابتداء كما دل عليه سياق كلامه أن لا يكون لبعض آثاره مدخل في بعض بحيث يمتنع تخلفه عنه عقلاً كما مذهب الشيخ وغيره من أهل السنة لا أن يكون تعالى هو الموجد هو ابتداء أي من غير واسطة إيجاد شيء آخر، بأن يكون الله تعالى موجداً لشيء وذلك الشيء موجداً لآخر، فيكون الله تعالى موجداً لذلك الآخر، يتوسط الشيء الأول كما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة فعلى هذا يندفع أيضاً اعتراض بعض الأفاضل، بأن ما ذكر من المذهب يصح، وإن لم يحذف قيد الابتداء بناء على أن معنى الاستناد ابتداء هو المعنى الأخير فلا ينافي القول بالتوليد . بأن لا المختار على ذلك الفعل الواجب، إذ يمكنه أن يفعله بإيجاد ما ما يوجبه وأن يتركه يوجد ذلك الموجب، لكن لا يكون تأثير القدرة فيه ابتداء كما هو مذهب الأشعري، وحينئذ يقال : النظر صادر بإيجاد الله تعالى وموجب للعلم بالمنظور فيه إيجابا عقليا بحيث يستحيل أن ينفك عنه
The fifth observatory - the fourth aim: How valid reasoning yields knowledge of God as claimed by the philosophers who say He is necessitating not free, and also no obligation upon Him as claimed by the Mu‘tazila, and it is only valid if the condition of origination is removed in the dependence of things upon God, His causing it to follow from Him rationally, exalted is He, and allowing that some of His effects have an entry into others such that it is impossible, so that some are generated from others even if all occur by His power, as the Mu‘tazila say regarding the actions of servants issuing from them by their power, and the necessity of some actions from others does not contradict the power of His being free, due to the necessity of knowledge after reasoning in the sense of rational entailment contradicting its necessary concomitant. His saying: (since no obligation etc.) is an inference for the negation of obligation upon Him absolutely by the negation of the individuals limited to the two, so no circularity, and it is not evidence for his saying: it is not valid with the statement etc., firstly because after observing the dependence initially, and His being free, the judgment of the invalidity of the mentioned doctrine does not need evidence, and secondly because it does not negate obligation from reasoning but only negates obligation for Him as you have understood, so the approximation is not complete. His saying: (as the Mu‘tazila claim) is based on the statement of rational good and evil. His saying: (and it is only valid etc.) confines validity to the removal of the condition of origination, since His being, exalted, a free agent in the mentioned sense is agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna, unlike the dependence initially, for it is the statement of some Ash‘aris as stated in Sharh al-Maqasid. Initially. His saying: (for some of His effects have an entry) i.e., in causation, that it is a necessitating cause for it. His saying: (and the necessity etc.) means that He is powerful and free in it by means of what necessitates it, even if He is not free in it – the statement that He, exalted, is powerful and free and that nothing is obligatory upon Him, and the approximation is apparent, for this doctrine includes the statement of obligation, so either from Him or upon Him. His saying: (and it is only valid if the condition of origination is removed) he only chose in the validity of the mentioned doctrine the removal of the condition of origination, rather he confined validity to it, so he did not mention removing one of the remaining two conditions, even though he first said that it is not valid with the statement of the three matters, based on the fact that the statement of the dependence of all upon Him, exalted, initially – the intended meaning here – entails the statement that He is powerful and free, as the commentator will indicate in the discussion of pre-eternity, and likewise entails the negation of obligation upon Him, exalted, because this obligation branches from the principle of rational judgment of good and evil, and this principle leads to the statement of dependence of some things upon Him, exalted, through others, like reward through obedience, so removing each of the last two conditions entails removing the condition of origination. And it should be known that he intended here by dependence initially, as indicated by the context of his speech, that some of His effects do not have an entry into others such that its failure to follow from Him is impossible rationally, as is the doctrine of the Shaykh and others of Ahl al-Sunna, not that He, exalted, is the originator initially, i.e., without intermediary of the existence of another thing, such that God, exalted, brings something into existence and that thing brings another into existence, so God, exalted, brings that other into existence through the first thing, as the philosophers held, and on this basis the objection of some virtuous ones is also dismissed, that the mentioned doctrine is valid even if the condition of origination is not removed, based on the fact that the meaning of dependence initially is the latter meaning, so it does not contradict the statement of generation. So that the free agent is not compelled regarding that necessary action, for He can do it by bringing into existence what necessitates it, or leave it by not bringing into existence that necessitating thing, but the power does not have initial effect in it, as is the Ash‘ari doctrine, and then it is said: reasoning issues by God's bringing into existence and is a necessitating cause for knowledge of the object of reasoning in a rational necessitation such that it is impossible for it to be separated from it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the conditions under which valid reasoning (nazar) can lead to necessary knowledge of God. The author begins by stating the problem: the claim that reasoning yields such knowledge is made by the philosophers (who say God is a necessary cause, not a free agent) and the Mu‘tazila (who say nothing is obligatory upon God). He asserts that this claim is only valid if we remove the condition of 'ibtidā'' (origination) from the dependence of things upon God – meaning we cannot require that all things directly proceed from God without any intermediary. It then proceeds in a commentary style, with the author explaining each part of the original statement. He argues that the doctrine of rational necessity (wujūb) contradicts God being a free agent (mukhtār). The only way to reconcile the two is to drop the condition that all things depend on God directly (ibtidā'an), while still maintaining that God is the ultimate power and that some effects can be generated from others (tawlīd) – as the Mu‘tazila say about human actions – provided this does not conflict with God's power. The passage concludes by explaining that the Ash‘ari position allows for an intermediate cause, so God's power is not directly involved in each effect, thus preserving both the validity of reasoning and God's free choice. The author warns that without removing this condition, the argument for necessary knowledge fails because it would imply a necessitation incompatible with divine freedom.

Key Terms
النظر
قيد الابتداء
التوليد
الفاعل المختار

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must be removed for the claim that reasoning leads to necessary knowledge of God to be valid?
A) The condition that God is a free agent.
B) The condition of origination (qayd al-ibtidā').
C) The condition that God has no attributes.
D) The condition that reasoning is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Valid reasoning about God requires removing the condition that all effects must directly originate from Him, allowing for mediated causation without compromising divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the causes of events in your life, do you subconsciously demand that God be the direct cause of everything, or are you willing to see His hand in the chain of secondary causes? How might your trust in Him grow if you removed the condition of direct origination?
?
If rational necessity does not negate God's freedom, then what does that imply about the times you felt trapped by circumstances? Could it be that your own actions, while following a necessary path, are still freely chosen and accountable before God?
?
Al-Jurjani says that the necessity of some actions from others does not contradict God's being free. Have you ever used the excuse of 'inevitability' to avoid taking responsibility for your choices? How does this lesson challenge you to own your actions while trusting that God is ultimately in control?

6 min
The Scope of Removing the Condition: A Detailed Analysis
Al-Jurjani explains why he confined validity to removing the condition of origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani focuses only on removing the condition of origination and not the other two conditions, and how the Ash‘ari position on tawlīd preserves both reasoning and divine freedom.

Core Idea
Only the condition of origination must be removed because the other two are automatically entailed by it.

In the commentary, al-Jurjani clarifies that he only mentions removing the condition of origination because doing so automatically addresses the other two conditions. He also explains the precise meaning of 'dependence initially' to distinguish his view from the philosophers'.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
This passage is a commentary on the earlier thesis. Read it carefully to follow the reasoning about why only one condition needs removal.
The commentary uses a question-and-answer style.
2
Understand why only one condition is mentioned
Al-Jurjani says that removing the condition of origination automatically entails the other two conditions (God being a free agent and no obligation upon Him). Explain the reasoning.
The other conditions are derived from the principle of rational good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ).
3
Learn the precise meaning of 'ibtidā'
Two interpretations: (1) no intermediate causes at all (philosophers), (2) effects cannot fail to follow from God's power directly (Ash‘ari). Al-Jurjani adopts the latter.
This distinction is crucial to dismiss an objection.
4
See how the objection is dismissed
Some object that the doctrine is valid even without removing the condition. Al-Jurjani shows that this is based on a misunderstanding of 'ibtidā'.
The objection wrongly assumes that 'ibtidā' means no intermediate causes, which is the philosophers' view.

The fifth observatory - the fourth aim: How valid reasoning yields knowledge of God as claimed by the philosophers who say He is necessitating not free, and also no obligation upon Him as claimed by the Mu‘tazila, and it is only valid if the condition of origination is removed in the dependence of things upon God, His causing it to follow from Him rationally, exalted is He, and allowing that some of His effects have an entry into others such that it is impossible, so that some are generated from others even if all occur by His power, as the Mu‘tazila say regarding the actions of servants issuing from them by their power, and the necessity of some actions from others does not contradict the power of His being free, due to the necessity of knowledge after reasoning in the sense of rational entailment contradicting its necessary concomitant. His saying: (since no obligation etc.) is an inference for the negation of obligation upon Him absolutely by the negation of the individuals limited to the two, so no circularity, and it is not evidence for his saying: it is not valid with the statement etc., firstly because after observing the dependence initially, and His being free, the judgment of the invalidity of the mentioned doctrine does not need evidence, and secondly because it does not negate obligation from reasoning but only negates obligation for Him as you have understood, so the approximation is not complete. His saying: (as the Mu‘tazila claim) is based on the statement of rational good and evil. His saying: (and it is only valid if the condition of origination is removed) he only chose in the validity of the mentioned doctrine the removal of the condition of origination, rather he confined validity to it, so he did not mention removing one of the remaining two conditions, even though he first said that it is not valid with the statement of the three matters, based on the fact that the statement of the dependence of all upon Him, exalted, initially – the intended meaning here – entails the statement that He is powerful and free, as the commentator will indicate in the discussion of pre-eternity, and likewise entails the negation of obligation upon Him, exalted, because this obligation branches from the principle of rational judgment of good and evil, and this principle leads to the statement of dependence of some things upon Him, exalted, through others, like reward through obedience, so removing each of the last two conditions entails removing the condition of origination. And it should be known that he intended here by dependence initially, as indicated by the context of his speech, that some of His effects do not have an entry into others such that its failure to follow from Him is impossible rationally, as is the doctrine of the Shaykh and others of Ahl al-Sunna, not that He, exalted, is the originator initially, i.e., without intermediary of the existence of another thing, such that God, exalted, brings something into existence and that thing brings another into existence, so God, exalted, brings that other into existence through the first thing, as the philosophers held, and on this basis the objection of some virtuous ones is also dismissed, that the mentioned doctrine is valid even if the condition of origination is not removed, based on the fact that the meaning of dependence initially is the latter meaning, so it does not contradict the statement of generation. So that the free agent is not compelled regarding that necessary action, for He can do it by bringing into existence what necessitates it, or leave it by not bringing into existence that necessitating thing, but the power does not have initial effect in it, as is the Ash‘ari doctrine, and then it is said: reasoning issues by God's bringing into existence and is a necessitating cause for knowledge of the object of reasoning in a rational necessitation such that it is impossible for it to be separated from it.

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the conditions under which valid reasoning (nazar) can lead to necessary knowledge of God. The author begins by stating the problem: the claim that reasoning yields such knowledge is made by the philosophers (who say God is a necessary cause, not a free agent) and the Mu‘tazila (who say nothing is obligatory upon God). He asserts that this claim is only valid if we remove the condition of 'ibtidā'' (origination) from the dependence of things upon God – meaning we cannot require that all things directly proceed from God without any intermediary. It then proceeds in a commentary style, with the author explaining each part of the original statement. He argues that the doctrine of rational necessity (wujūb) contradicts God being a free agent (mukhtār). The only way to reconcile the two is to drop the condition that all things depend on God directly (ibtidā'an), while still maintaining that God is the ultimate power and that some effects can be generated from others (tawlīd) – as the Mu‘tazila say about human actions – provided this does not conflict with God's power. The passage concludes by explaining that the Ash‘ari position allows for an intermediate cause, so God's power is not directly involved in each effect, thus preserving both the validity of reasoning and God's free choice. The author warns that without removing this condition, the argument for necessary knowledge fails because it would imply a necessitation incompatible with divine freedom.

Key Terms

الاستناد ابتداء
الحسن والقبح العقليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dropping the condition of origination denies God's power over all things, but it only denies that every effect must directly originate from Him without any natural or fixed cause.
Correction: In the Ash‘ari view, God remains the ultimate cause, but He may choose to create intermediate causes. His power is not diminished; rather, it is exercised through the chain of causation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Jurjani mean by 'dependence initially' (al-istinād ibtidā’)?
A) That no intermediate causes are possible; everything is directly from God.
B) That effects cannot fail to follow from God's power if they are directly caused, but intermediate causes are allowed.
C) That God creates everything out of nothing without any means.
D) That only the philosophers' view is correct.

Key Takeaway
Dropping the condition of origination does not mean God is not the ultimate cause; it means that some effects can follow from others by divine ordination, preserving both reasoning and divine free agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the events in your life—do you truly believe that nothing happens independently of God, or do you sometimes feel that certain outcomes are purely caused by natural or human factors? How would the Ash'ari distinction between direct and mediated causation change your reliance on God in daily decisions?
?
The passage says that God brings about reasoning as a necessitating cause for knowledge. Reflect on a time you understood something deeply: did you thank God for your ability to reason, or did you take pride in your own intellect? What would it mean for you to attribute even your mental processes to God's creation?
?
If God is not obligated by any external standard, how does that affect your worship? Do you obey God because you expect reward or fear punishment, or simply because He is the Lord who owes nothing? What must change in your heart to submit unconditionally to a God who is bound by no obligation, yet commands you?

intermediate

The Two Conditions for Valid Reasoning
Al-Jurjani enumerates the conditions for reasoning: existence of intellect, absence of its general and specific opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two prerequisites for reasoning to be valid: the existence of intellect and the absence of its opposites, both general and specific.

Core Idea
Reasoning requires not only a working mind but also the absence of obstacles that block or short-circuit the search for knowledge.

Before we can reason correctly, certain conditions must be met. Al-Jurjani here outlines two fundamental prerequisites: first, we must have an intellect—the basic tool of reasoning; second, we must be free from anything that prevents reasoning from functioning. He then breaks down the second condition into general obstacles (like sleep) and a specific obstacle (already knowing the answer or being locked into a wrong certainty). This classification helps us understand why reasoning sometimes fails even when we try.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure: conditions, division of opposites, and objections.
Notice that the passage includes both definitions and responses to objections.
2
Identify the two conditions
The passage states that after life, two conditions are needed: (1) existence of intellect, and (2) absence of its opposite. Note that the first is existential, the second privative.
The condition of intellect is the basis of moral obligation (taklīf).
3
Distinguish general and specific opposites
The opposite of reasoning is divided into general (affecting all perception, like sleep) and specific (affecting reasoning about a particular sought object, like already knowing it or having compound ignorance).
General opposites block perception itself; specific opposites block reasoning about a specific question.
4
Understand the objections and answers
Al-Jurjani addresses two objections: (a) if we already know the sought object, reasoning is impossible; but we need some prior knowledge to seek. (b) A heedless person might still reason, but reasoning is voluntary and requires conceiving a benefit.
These answers refine the condition that the specific opposite must be absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : شرط النظر (شرط النظر إما مطلقا ) سواء كان صحيحاً أو فاسداً فبعد الحياة أمرأن الأول) وجودي وهو (وجود العقل الذي هو مناط التكليف ( وسيأتي تفسيره الثاني) عدمي وهو (عدم ضده أي ضد النظر وهو ما ينافيه (فمنه) ما هو (عام) يضاد النظر وغيره ( وهو كل ما هو ضد الإدراك ) مطلقاً من النوم والغفلة والغشية فإنه يضاد النظر لاستلزامه الإدراك (ومنه) ما هو (خاص) يضاد النظر بخصوصه (وهو العلم بالمطلوب) من حيث هو مطلوب وأما العلم به من وجه، فلا بد منه ليمكن طلبه ( والجهل المركب به أعني الجزم به على خلاف ما هو عليه (إذ صاحبهما لا قوله : (شرط) النظر أي في إفادته العلم بالمطلوب، فلا يرد على الحصر شروط تحققها كالمعلومات والمطلوب والمحل والعقل والزمان والمكان، وبما حررنا اندفع الشكوك التي أوردها بعض الناظرين على قوله وهو العلم بالمطلوب من حيث هو مطلوب، وأما العلم بوجه آخر، فلا بد منه وعلى قوله والجهل المركب كما لا يخفى . قوله: (شرط النظر إما مطلقاً فبعد الحياة أمران أراد شرط النظر من حيث إنه نظر لا من حيث إنه حركة في الكيف، فإنه يحتاج من الحيثية الأخيرة إلى المتحرك وما فيه الحركة ونحو ذلك، ولذلك لم يتعرض للمقدمات والعالم المحل . قوله : ( وهو العلم بالمطلوب من حيث هو مطلوب) قيل عليه : النظر غير مشروط بطلب مطلوب ،معين فيمكن أن ينظر في مقدمات حاصلة عنده لتحصيل مطلوب ما غاية الأمر، أن المطلوب لكونه حاصلاً لا يحصل ثانياً، والجواب إن مطلوباً معيناً إذا كان حاصلاً لم يكن النظر لتحصيله، ولا لتحصيل مطلق موجود في ضمنه فإن كلاً منهما تحصيل الحاصل بل لمطلوب آخر، فالمطلوب من حيث هو مطلوب غير معلوم وهو المطلوب وبالجملة الكلام في النظر المتعارف المشتمل على حركتين ولا يتأتى فيه ما ذكر. قوله : ( وأما العلم به بوجه آخر إلخ) قيل : يرد عليه أن الغافل عن المطلوب ربما تصرف مقدمات حاصلة عنده أو ملقاة إليه ورتبها فادته إلى المطلوب وأنت خبير بأن هذا لا يتأتى على رأي من يوجب في الفعل الاختياري تصور فائدة، فإن النظر فعل اختياري لا بد لفاعله من تصور وصول إلى علم، فقد تحقق علم المطلوب بوجه، فإن قلت: لا يتعين ذلك قلت الكلام في النظر المتعارف .
The fifth aim: The condition of reasoning. (The condition of reasoning either absolutely) whether sound or corrupt, then after life are two things: the first is existential, and it is the existence of intellect which is the basis of obligation, and its interpretation will come. The second is privative, and it is the absence of its opposite, i.e., the opposite of reasoning, which is what contradicts it. (From it) is what is (general) that contradicts reasoning and other things, and it is everything that is the opposite of perception absolutely, like sleep, heedlessness, and fainting, for it contradicts reasoning because it entails perception. (And from it) is what is (specific) that contradicts reasoning specifically, and it is knowledge of the sought object from the perspective that it is sought. As for knowledge of it in some respect, it is necessary for it to be possible to seek it. (And compound ignorance of it, i.e., certainty about it contrary to what it is) for the possessor of both does not... His saying: (the condition of) reasoning, i.e., in its yielding knowledge of the sought object, so the objection regarding the conditions of its realization, such as the known things, the sought object, the locus, the intellect, time, and place, is not applicable. And by what we have clarified, the doubts raised by some investigators regarding his saying 'and it is knowledge of the sought object from the perspective that it is sought, as for knowledge in another respect, it is necessary' and his saying 'and compound ignorance' are repelled, as is not hidden. His saying: (the condition of reasoning either absolutely, then after life are two things) he intended the condition of reasoning insofar as it is reasoning, not insofar as it is a motion in quality, for it needs from the latter perspective the mover, that in which motion occurs, and so on, and therefore he did not address the premises and the knowledgeable locus. His saying: (and it is knowledge of the sought object from the perspective that it is sought) it was objected: reasoning is not conditioned by seeking a specific sought object, for one can reason from premises he has to obtain some sought object; the point is that the sought object, because it is obtained, is not obtained a second time. The answer: if a specific sought object is obtained, reasoning is not for obtaining it, nor for obtaining an absolute existing within it, for each of these is obtaining the obtained, but rather for another sought object; so the sought object from the perspective that it is sought is not known, and it is the sought object. In summary, the discussion is about conventional reasoning that comprises two motions, and what was mentioned does not occur in it. His saying: (and as for knowledge in another respect, etc.) it was objected: this is refuted by the fact that one who is heedless of the sought object may manipulate premises he has or are presented to him and arrange them, and they lead him to the sought object. And you are well aware that this does not obtain according to the view of one who requires in voluntary action the conception of benefit, for reasoning is a voluntary action; its doer must conceive of arriving at knowledge, so knowledge of the sought object in some respect is realized. If you say: it is not specified, I say: the discussion is about conventional reasoning.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the conditions (shurūṭ) for reasoning (nazar) to be valid. The author begins by stating that the conditions for reasoning, whether sound or corrupt, are two after life: first, the existence of the intellect (al-ʿaql), which is the basis of moral obligation; second, the absence of its opposite (ḍidd), which is anything that contradicts reasoning. The opposites are then divided into general (ʿāmm) and specific (khāṣṣ). General opposites are those that contradict perception (idrāk) in general, such as sleep, heedlessness, and fainting, because reasoning requires perception. Specific opposites are those that contradict reasoning specifically, namely knowledge of the sought object (al-maṭlūb) insofar as it is sought, and compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) about it. The author then addresses possible objections: first, that one can reason without having a specific sought object in mind; the answer is that conventional reasoning (al-naẓar al-mutaʿāraf) involves two motions (seeking and obtaining) and requires that the sought object be unknown in that specific respect, although some prior knowledge is needed to initiate the search. Second, that one might be heedless of the sought object yet still reason correctly; the author responds that reasoning is a voluntary act (fiʿl ikhtiyārī) requiring conception of a benefit, so some knowledge of the goal is necessary. The passage thus clarifies the necessary prerequisites for reasoning to lead to knowledge.

Key Terms
النظر
العقل
الضد العام
الضد الخاص
العلم بالمطلوب
الجهل المركب
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having any knowledge of the sought object prevents reasoning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while knowledge of the sought object from the perspective that it is sought blocks reasoning, some prior knowledge (in another respect) is necessary to initiate the search.

Checkpoint
What is the specific opposite of reasoning (al-ḍidd al-khāṣṣ) according to this passage?
A) Sleep and heedlessness
B) Knowledge of the sought object from the perspective that it is sought and compound ignorance
C) Absence of intellect
D) Fainting

Key Takeaway
For reasoning to be valid, the intellect must exist and it must be free from general perceptual blocks and from the specific block of already knowing the sought outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific 'general opposite'—sleep, heedlessness, or distraction—is currently blocking your genuine pursuit of knowledge about your Creator? Will you set aside time today to remove that obstacle and seek with full awareness?
?
You may assume you already know the answers to certain core matters of faith (like the nature of Allah’s attributes or the purpose of trials). Could this be compound ignorance (الجهل المركب) in disguise? Name one belief you hold about Islam that you have never seriously questioned from an opposing perspective. What step will you take this week to challenge that belief in search of true certainty?
?
Al-Jurjani says reasoning requires that the sought object be unknown 'from the perspective that it is sought.' Is there a question about your deen that you have stopped asking because you think you have it figured out? What is that question, and when will you intentionally re-open it as if you are seeking for the first time?

The First Objection: Possibility of Knowing Allah
Al-Iji raises the objection that obligation depends on possibility, and answers that knowledge is theoretical, not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the obligation of knowing Allah and how the response clarifies that knowledge is possible as theoretical, not necessary.

Core Idea
Obligation depends on possibility; knowledge of Allah is theoretical, not necessary.

In this passage, al-Iji raises a critical objection: before we can say that knowing Allah is obligatory, we must first establish that such knowledge is possible. He argues that it is not necessary knowledge, because it requires reflection. The response unpacks the nature of possibility and defends the theoretical path.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand that the objection hinges on the concept of possibility (imkan). The objector says obligation depends on possibility, and knowledge of Allah is not necessary (daruri) because if left without reflection, one would not find it.
The objector uses the fact that knowledge is absent naturally to argue it is not necessary.
2
Identify the key distinction
Note the contrast between necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge. The objector claims possibility must be as theoretical, not necessary.
3
Follow the response
The response explains that possibility is a relation to existence in the mind, not about the manner of acquisition. It also refutes the idea that the conditional is not self-evident.
The response argues that if knowledge were necessary, it would not require reflection, but theoretical knowledge does.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that the objection ultimately becomes a claim of impossibility, which then makes the argument necessary (ilzami). The commentator shows that the objector's aim is to deny possibility altogether.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه إشكالات الأول) أن وجوب المعرفة يتوقف على إمكانها وليس إمكانها باعتبار كونها ضرورية لأن الإنسان لو خلي ودواعي نفسه من مبدأ نشوه من غير نظر، لم يجد من نفسه العلم بذلك أصلاً، والضروري لا يكون كذلك بل باعتبار كونها نظرية مستفادة من النظر فعلى هذا إمكان معرفة الله تعالى إفادة النظر العلم مطلقاً أي في الجملة وفي الإلهيات. قوله : (فيتوقف على إمكانها إذ لا تكليف بالممتنع. قوله: (وليس إمكانها إلخ ) يريد أنها لو كانت ممكنة فالإمكان لكونه نسبة بين الشيء ووجوده يكون بالنسبة إلى كونها وحصولها في الذهن وليس بالنسبة إلى كونه بطريق الضرورة، لأن الإنسان لو خلي من النظر لا يجد من نفسه بعد الالتفات العلم بذلك، أي بكونها ضرورية أي حاصلة في الذهن بدون نظر والضروري لا يكون كذلك أي لا يجد الإنسان من نفسه العلم به بعد الالتفات، وإلا لجاز أن يكون لنا علوم ضرورية لا نعلمها وبما حررنا ظهر أن الاعتراض على ما ذكره الشارح بأن الشرطية ليست ببديهية ولا مبرهن عليها فيرد المنع عليه، بأنا لم نجد من أنفسنا ذلك لكن لم لا يجوز أن يوجد شخص من الأشخاص يجده ليس بشيء منشؤه سوء الفهم، وما قيل : في بيان أن ليس إمكانها باعتبار كونها ضرورية من أنه لو كان كذلك لما لا تتم إلا بالنظر ولأن الضرورية تستلزم عدم المقدورية وغير المقدور لا يكلف به إجماعاً ففيه أن مقصود المعترض عدم إمكان المعرفة في نفس الأمر ليتفرع عليه عدم وجوبه في نفس الأمر، وعلى هذا التقرير يكون إلزامياً
And upon it are objections. The first: that the obligation of knowledge depends on its possibility, and its possibility is not by virtue of it being necessary, because if a person were left to his own inclinations from the beginning of his development without reflection, he would not find in himself knowledge of that at all, and the necessary is not like that. Rather, its possibility is by virtue of it being theoretical, derived from reflection. So on this basis, the possibility of knowing Allah the Exalted is that reflection yields knowledge absolutely, i.e., in general, and in divine matters. His statement: 'so it depends on its possibility, since there is no obligation of the impossible.' His statement: 'and its possibility is not [by virtue of it being necessary] etc.' He means that if it were possible, then possibility, being a relation between a thing and its existence, is with respect to its being and occurring in the mind, not with respect to its being by way of necessity. Because if a person is left from reflection, he does not find in himself after attention knowledge of that, i.e., of it being necessary, i.e., occurring in the mind without reflection. And the necessary is not like that, i.e., a person does not find in himself knowledge of it after attention. Otherwise, it would be possible for us to have necessary sciences that we do not know. And by what we have written, it becomes clear that the objection against what the commentator mentioned—that the conditional is neither self-evident nor demonstrable—is met with denial, because we do not find that in ourselves, but why is it not possible that there exists a person who finds it? That is nothing, its source is poor understanding. And what was said in explaining that its possibility is not by virtue of it being necessary, that if it were so, it would not require reflection, and because the necessary implies being beyond power, and that which is beyond power is not obligated at all by consensus, but it contains that the objector's aim is the non-possibility of knowledge in reality, so that the non-obligation follows in reality. And on this account, it becomes necessary.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (ishkal) against the second path and the response. The objection states that the obligation of knowing Allah depends on the possibility (imkan) of such knowledge. The objector argues that this knowledge is not necessary (daruri) because if a person were left to his natural state without reflection, he would not find any knowledge of God. Necessary knowledge is present without reflection, so it cannot be necessary. Therefore, its possibility must be as theoretical (nazari) knowledge, derived from reflection. The response begins by clarifying that possibility is a relation between the thing and its existence in the mind, not concerning the manner (necessary vs. theoretical). It then refutes the idea that the conditional statement is not self-evident. The response argues that if knowledge were necessary, it would not require reflection, and necessary things are not within our power, and what is not within our power is not obligated by consensus. But the objector's real aim is to deny the possibility of knowledge altogether, so the response is that the objection essentially becomes a claim of impossibility, which then would make the argument necessary (ilzami).

Key Terms
إشكال
إمكان
ضرورية
نظرية
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection denies the obligation itself, but it actually questions the possibility of knowledge.
Correction: The objection is about establishing the possibility of knowledge before obligation; the response shows it is possible as theoretical.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against the second path?
A) Knowledge of Allah is not obligatory because there is no consensus
B) The obligation of knowledge depends on its possibility, and it is not necessary knowledge
C) Reflection is not necessary for knowledge
D) The verse 'Know that...' is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that establishing the possibility of theoretical knowledge is essential before obligation, but the response affirms that knowledge is possible through reflection, refuting the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that knowing Allah is automatic or comes naturally? When was the last time you actively reflected on His existence and attributes, or have you relied on inherited belief without personal investigation? What is stopping you from engaging in that reflection today?
?
The objector doubted the possibility of knowledge because it was not necessary. Have you ever doubted your own ability to truly know Allah, perhaps because faith feels abstract? What would it mean for your relationship with Him if you treat knowledge as a task requiring effort—and when will you start that effort?
?
If the obligation to know Allah depends on its possibility, and that possibility lies in reflection, what have you done this week to fulfill that obligation? If you have not, what does your neglect say about your commitment to knowing Him?

The Eighth Objection: Refuting the Proof for Obligation of Nazar
The commentator presents and answers an objection against the obligation of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the obligation of nazar against the objection that the same proof would also obligate the absence of knowledge and doubt.

Core Idea
Obligation depends on prerequisites within our power, not on mere dependence.

The proof for the obligation of nazar argued that knowledge of God requires nazar, so nazar is obligatory. The objector turns the proof around, claiming that since knowledge also depends on its absence and doubt, those too would be obligatory. The commentator clarifies the nature of obligation and the control we have over prerequisites, preserving the obligation of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the dense argument. Then read the literal translation carefully. Notice the back-and-forth structure between objection and response.
The passage uses technical terms like نظر, معرفة, شك, وجوب.
2
Identify the objection
The objector concedes that knowledge depends on nazar, but argues that the same proof would make the absence of knowledge and doubt obligatory. Find this argument in the text.
The objector says: 'the proof is invalidated by the absence of knowledge and by doubt.'
3
Understand the commentator's distinction
The commentator responds that the obligation here is conditional on what is within our power. The absence of knowledge and doubt are not within our power, so they cannot be obligatory. Focus on the phrase 'the obligation here is conditional upon the absence of what is not within one's power.'
This is the key move: not all prerequisites create obligations.
4
Grasp the final point about agreement
The commentator notes that even according to some like Abu Hashim, the non-obligation of doubt is agreed upon. This shows the objection fails universally.
The passage ends with a note that this does not contradict future discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن) سلمنا أن المعرفة لا تحصل إلا بالنظر لكن لا يلزم من هذا وجوب النظر إذ (الدليل) الذي بنيتموه عليه (منقوض بعدم المعرفة وبالشك ) فإن تحصيل المعرفة كما يتوقف على النظر يتوقف أيضاً على عدمها، لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل وكذلك يتوقف على الشك عند بعضهم مع أنه ليس يلزم من وجوب تحصيل المعرفة وجوب عدم المعرفة لا وجوب الشك اتفاقاً قلنا : الكلام فيما يكون الوجوب مطلقاً والمقدمة) يعني ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به مقدورة والوجوب (هاهنا أي وجوب المعرفة (مقيد بعدم غير مقدورة، وإن كان التوجه الموصل إلى الإلهام والطلب التام الموصل إلى التعليم مقدورين لنا فلا يرد النقض بهما . قوله : (منقوض إلخ) يعني أن الدليل المذكور بعينه جار في عدم المعرفة، والشك مع تخلف الحكم عنه أعني وجوبهما، بأن تقول : معرفة الله واجبة إجماعاً، وهي لا تتم إلا بعدم المعرفة والشك وما لا يتم الواجب المطلق إلا به فهو واجب . قوله : (اتفاقاً) متعلق بقوله : ليس يلزم أي عدم وجوب عدم المعرفة، وعدم وجوب الشك من وجوب تحصيل المعرفة، متفق عليه فلا ينافي ما سيجيء من أن الشك واجب عند أبي هاشم.
(Eighth) We grant that knowledge is not obtained except through speculative reasoning, but this does not entail the obligation of speculative reasoning, for the proof you have built upon it is invalidated by the absence of knowledge and by doubt. For obtaining knowledge, just as it depends on speculative reasoning, also depends on its absence, because the obtained cannot be obtained again, and similarly it depends on doubt according to some, while it is not entailed from the obligation of obtaining knowledge the obligation of the absence of knowledge nor the obligation of doubt, by agreement. We said: The discussion concerns when the obligation is absolute, and the premise—meaning what the obligatory act cannot be completed without—is within one's power, while the obligation here, i.e., the obligation of knowledge, is conditional upon the absence of what is not within one's power. And if the attention that leads to inspiration and the complete request that leads to instruction were within our power, then the invalidation by them would not apply. His statement: (invalidated, etc.) means that the same proof applies to the absence of knowledge and to doubt, while the ruling—i.e., their obligation—is absent. For you say: Knowledge of God is obligatory by consensus, and it is not completed except by the absence of knowledge and doubt, and what the absolute obligatory act cannot be completed without is itself obligatory. His statement: (by agreement) is attached to his saying: it is not entailed—i.e., the non-obligation of the absence of knowledge and the non-obligation of doubt from the obligation of obtaining knowledge is agreed upon, so it does not contradict what will come that doubt is obligatory according to Abu Hashim.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection to the argument for the obligation of speculative reasoning (nazar) and the commentator's response. The objector concedes that knowledge of God requires nazar, but argues that this does not make nazar obligatory because the same reasoning could be used to argue that the absence of knowledge and doubt are obligatory (since knowledge also depends on them). The commentator clarifies that the obligation discussed is for absolute obligations, and the premise (what the obligatory act depends on) must be within one's power. Here, the obligation of knowledge is conditional on the absence of what is not within one's power (like the absence of knowledge or doubt, which are not within human control). He further explains that the objector's argument would only apply if the means to inspiration and instruction were within our power, but they are not. The commentator then glosses the matn's phrase 'invalidated' to show how the same proof could be applied to absence and doubt, but since the ruling (obligation) does not follow, the objection fails. He also notes that the non-obligation of doubt is agreed upon, except for Abu Hashim's view, which will be discussed later.

Key Terms
نظر
معرفة
شك
وجوب
مقدمة
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge depends on both nazar and its absence, then nazar cannot be obligatory because absence would also be obligatory.
Correction: Obligation only applies to prerequisites that are within human power; absence and doubt are not within power, so they are not obligatory, preserving nazar's obligation.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator respond to the objection that the proof for obligation of nazar also implies obligation of absence of knowledge and doubt?
A) By denying that knowledge depends on absence.
B) By stating that the obligation is conditional on what is within one's power.
C) By admitting the objection and changing the definition.
D) By arguing that the proof only applies to absolute obligations.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of nazar is preserved because the prerequisites that are not within our power do not become obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with a difficult religious obligation, have you ever used the excuse that something is 'beyond your power' to avoid effort, even when the real obstacle was your own reluctance? How does this lesson challenge you to reassess what is truly within your control?
?
The lesson shows that not every dependency makes a duty. Are there spiritual responsibilities you have been waiting for perfect conditions to fulfill, when the real prerequisite was your own initiative? What concrete step will you take this week?
?
If knowledge of God depends on your use of intellect and reflection (nazar), and you know it is within your power, what is holding you back from giving it serious time and sincerity? Will you commit to a regular practice of reflection starting today?

The Eighth Objection: Does Reasoning Really Become Obligatory?
Al-Jurjani presents and answers the objection that the proof for the obligation of reasoning is invalidated by the non-obligation of ignorance and doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that the proof for the obligation of reasoning is invalidated by the non-obligation of ignorance and doubt.

Core Idea
Not all prerequisites for an obligation are themselves obligatory.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani addresses the eighth objection against the proof that reasoning (nazar) is obligatory. The objector argues that if knowledge depends on reasoning, then it also depends on the absence of prior knowledge and on doubt, yet these are not obligatory. Al-Jurjani's response distinguishes between absolute and conditional obligations, showing that the obligation of knowledge is conditional on an absence that is not within our power. This lesson unpacks that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector concedes that knowledge requires reasoning, but argues that the same proof would make ignorance and doubt obligatory, which is absurd.
Notice how the objector uses the same logical structure as the original proof.
2
Identify the core of the objection
The proof 'what the obligatory act cannot be completed without is obligatory' is applied to the prerequisites of absence of knowledge and doubt.
This is a classic counterexample (naqd) in kalam.
3
Examine Al-Jurjani's answer
Al-Jurjani distinguishes absolute (mutlaq) from conditional (muqayyad) obligation. The obligation of knowledge is conditional on an absence that is not within our power, so the premise (absence) is not obligatory.
What is not within our power cannot be obligatory.
4
Reflect on the specification
Al-Jurjani notes that the claim can be specified: reasoning is only obligatory for those who have no other path to knowledge.
This avoids the objection by narrowing the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن) سلمنا أن المعرفة لا تحصل إلا بالنظر لكن لا يلزم من هذا وجوب النظر إذ (الدليل) الذي بنيتموه عليه (منقوض بعدم المعرفة وبالشك ) فإن تحصيل المعرفة كما يتوقف على النظر يتوقف أيضاً على عدمها، لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل وكذلك يتوقف على الشك عند بعضهم مع أنه ليس يلزم من وجوب تحصيل المعرفة وجوب عدم المعرفة لا وجوب الشك اتفاقاً قلنا : الكلام فيما يكون الوجوب مطلقاً والمقدمة) يعني ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به مقدورة والوجوب (هاهنا أي وجوب المعرفة (مقيد بعدم غير مقدورة، وإن كان التوجه الموصل إلى الإلهام والطلب التام الموصل إلى التعليم مقدورين لنا فلا يرد النقض بهما . قوله : ( أو قلنا نخصه إلخ) يعني أن المراد أنها لا تتم إلا بالنظر لمن لا طريق له غيره، بناء على أن المدعى خاص وهو وجوب النظر لمن لا طريق له سواه . قوله : (منقوض إلخ) يعني أن الدليل المذكور بعينه جار في عدم المعرفة، والشك مع تخلف الحكم عنه أعني وجوبهما، بأن تقول : معرفة الله واجبة إجماعاً، وهي لا تتم إلا بعدم المعرفة والشك وما لا يتم الواجب المطلق إلا به فهو واجب . قوله : (اتفاقاً) متعلق بقوله : ليس يلزم أي عدم وجوب عدم المعرفة، وعدم وجوب الشك من وجوب تحصيل المعرفة، متفق عليه فلا ينافي ما سيجيء من أن الشك واجب عند أبي هاشم.
(Eighth) We concede that knowledge cannot be attained except through reasoning, but this does not entail the obligation of reasoning, for the proof you have built upon it is invalidated by the absence of knowledge and by doubt. For the attainment of knowledge depends on reasoning but also on the absence of knowledge, because it is impossible to attain what is already possessed; likewise, according to some, it depends on doubt. Yet it is not necessarily entailed from the obligation of attaining knowledge either the obligation of the absence of knowledge or the obligation of doubt, by agreement. We say: The discussion concerns where the obligation is absolute, and the premise—meaning what the obligatory act cannot be completed without—is within one's power, but the obligation here—i.e., the obligation of knowledge—is conditioned upon absence which is not within one's power; and if the orientation leading to inspiration and the complete seeking leading to teaching were within our power, then the objection would not apply. His words: (Or we say we specify it, etc.) mean that the intention is that knowledge is only completed by reasoning for one who has no other path to it, based on the claim being specific, namely the obligation of reasoning for one who has no path other than it. His words: (is invalidated, etc.) mean that the very same proof applies to absence of knowledge and doubt, while the ruling—i.e., their obligation—is absent; for you would say: Knowledge of God is obligatory by consensus, and it is only completed by absence of knowledge and doubt, and whatever the absolute obligatory act cannot be completed without is obligatory. His words: (by agreement) relates to his saying: it is not entailed—i.e., the non-obligation of absence of knowledge and non-obligation of doubt from the obligation of attaining knowledge is agreed upon; thus it does not contradict what will come that doubt is obligatory according to Abu Hashim.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection (al-thamin) against the proof for the obligation of reasoning (nazar). The objector concedes that knowledge of God (ma'rifa) is only attained through reasoning, but argues that this does not entail the obligation of reasoning, because the same proof can be used to claim that the absence of knowledge and doubt are also obligatory—an absurdity. The proof runs: knowledge of God is obligatory, and it depends on both the absence of previous knowledge (to avoid attaining what is already attained) and doubt (according to some). Since the absolute obligatory act (al-wajib al-mutlaq) requires its premises, these would also be obligatory. But they are not obligatory by agreement, so the proof is invalid. The answer (قلنا) distinguishes: the obligation here is not absolute but conditional (muqayyad). The obligation of knowledge is conditioned upon the absence of knowledge, which is not within our power (ghayr maqdoor). What is not within our power is not obligatory. The objector's own example of tawajjuh (orientation) and talab (seeking) as alternative pathways is discussed: even if those are within power, the core point remains that for the common person who has no other path, reasoning is obligatory. The commentary sections (قوله) clarify: 'or we say we specify it' means the claim is specific to those with no alternative. The 'invalidated' section explains how the proof, if applied to absence and doubt, does not entail their obligation because the proof's original premise is about an absolute obligation, whereas here it is conditional. The final note about 'by agreement' confirms that the non-obligation of absence and doubt is universally accepted, and this does not conflict with Abu Hashim's later position on the obligation of doubt (since that is a different context).

المقدمة

النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any condition for an obligation is itself obligatory.
Correction: Only conditions that are within one's power and under an absolute obligation render the condition obligatory. Here, the condition (absence of knowledge) is not within power, so it is not obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why does the proof that knowledge depends on reasoning not entail the obligation of reasoning?
A) Because reasoning is impossible.
B) Because the obligation of knowledge is conditional on the absence of knowledge, which is not within our power.
C) Because doubt is not obligatory at all.
D) Because the objector conceded that knowledge is only attained through reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani defends the obligation of reasoning by showing that the objection fails because it confuses absolute obligation with conditional obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever hide behind the excuse that your faith is already 'complete' or that you have other ways to connect with God, thereby neglecting the intellectual reasoning that this lesson shows is obligatory for you? What would it mean to take that duty seriously starting today?
?
If you realize that the obligation to reason about God applies only to those without alternative paths, how does that shift your responsibility? Are you truly without such paths, or are you avoiding the hard work of understanding?
?
The objector used the fact that knowledge depends on its absence to argue against obligation. In your own life, do you ever use the fact that you already believe to justify not seeking deeper conviction? How will you respond to God if He asks you about the reasoning you could have done but neglected?

Alternative Paths to Knowledge: Objection and Answer
Al-Jurjani refutes objections that purification or education can replace reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why alternative paths to knowledge like spiritual orientation (tawajjuh) and esoteric education (ta'lim) do not negate the obligation of rational inquiry (nazar) for the common person.

Core Idea
Alternative paths to knowledge are either too hard or too exclusive to replace reasoning.

A common objection to the sufficiency of reasoning is that other methods exist: the complete orientation of Hindu sages and the esoteric education of certain groups. Al-Jurjani addresses this challenge directly, arguing that these alternatives do not provide a universal substitute for reasoned argument. In this passage, you'll see how he dismantles the supposed practical equality of these paths.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection presents two paths: complete orientation (tawajjuh) and education (ta'lim). Note that the objector claims they have a customary link to knowledge, similar to reasoning.
The objector is using an Ash'ari-like argument about customary concomitance.
2
Analyze the answer's first part
The answer addresses the path of orientation: either it requires severe struggles (tasfiyah) making it effectively impossible, or it relies on a special spiritual quality not under human control.
Note the disjunctive reasoning: either it's too hard, or it's too special.
3
Analyze the answer's second part
The answer dismisses education (ta'lim) because its proponents (the Mulahida) claim it is confined to a specific group, which makes it problematic as a general path.
The objection's own proponents admit exclusivity, so it cannot be universal.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Neither path provides a viable alternative to reasoning for establishing obligation (taklif) for all people. Therefore, reasoned argument remains necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : طريق حكماء الهند التوجه التام المقدور مع أمور مقدورة كما سبق، وطريق التعليم الطلب والجد ألا ترى أن من طلب وجد فبين الطريقين والإلهام والتعليم لزوم عادي كما هو مذهب الأشعري في النظر، وهذا القدر يكفي في ثبوت التكليف قلت: أما التوجه التام المستتبع للإلهام فإن لزم قرانه للمجاهدات الشاقة والمخاطرات الكثيرة كالتصفية، فهو في حكم ما لا يكون مقدوراً كما صرح به، وإن لم يلزم فحصول مرادهم لخاصية في نفوسهم الخاصة كما صرح به بعضهم، ولا قدرة عليها حينئذ وأما التعليم فالقائلون به أعني الملاحدة يدعون انحصاره في جماعة مخصوصة فأمره حينئذ في غاية الإشكال .
For you might say: The way of the sages of India is complete orientation (tawajjuh) which is within one's power along with other things within one's power as previously mentioned, and the way of education (ta'lim) is seeking and striving. Do you not see that whoever seeks finds? So between the two paths—inspiration (ilham) and education—there is a customary concomitance, just as the Ash'ari view regarding reasoning (nazar). And this much suffices to establish obligation. I say: As for the complete orientation that leads to inspiration, if its conjunction with the arduous struggles and numerous risks like purification (tasfiyah) is necessary, then it is in the same category as what is not within one's power, as he stated. And if it is not necessary, then the attainment of their goal is due to a special quality in their particular souls, as some of them stated, and there is no power over that at that time. As for education, its proponents—namely the Mulahida (atheists)—claim that it is confined to a specific group; therefore its matter is extremely problematic.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents a new objection (فإن قلت) to the necessity of reasoning: alternative paths to knowledge exist, such as the complete orientation (tawajjuh) of the Hindu sages or education (ta'lim). The objector argues that these paths involve ordinary means within human power, and they have a customary link (luzum 'adi) to the desired outcome (inspiration or knowledge), similar to the Ash'ari view on reasoning. This would suffice to establish obligation without reasoning. The answer (قلت) distinguishes: for the path of orientation to produce inspiration, it must be combined with severe struggles and risks (like tasfiyah), which are effectively beyond ordinary power; if it does not require such struggles, then the outcome comes from a special quality in the individual's soul, which is not subject to deliberate power. Thus, it does not constitute a universally accessible means. Regarding education, its proponents (the Mulahida) only claim it works for a specific group, making it deeply problematic to generalize. Therefore, these alternative paths do not undermine the obligation of reasoning for the common person.

Key Terms

إلهام
تعليم
تصفية
لزوم عادي
مقدور
خاصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any personal spiritual practice or esoteric teaching can replace reasoned argument for knowing God.
Correction: The passage shows that such paths are either so demanding as to be practically impossible or rely on exclusive individual qualities, not available to everyone, unlike reasoning which is universally accessible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani's answer, why does the path of complete orientation not serve as a universal alternative to reasoning?
A) It is forbidden by Islamic law.
B) It either requires unbearable struggles or depends on a special quality not under human power.
C) It leads to skepticism.
D) It was invented by the Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
Alternative paths to knowledge like spiritual orientation and esoteric education are either too demanding or too exclusive to replace reasoned argument for the general public.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself avoiding the hard work of rational study, do you rely on the hope of a sudden spiritual opening (ilham) to guide you? How does this passage challenge that hope, especially if you are unwilling to undergo the severe struggles it would require or lack the special quality of soul?
?
Have you ever thought that simply attending a class or following a teacher (ta'lim) is sufficient, without engaging your own intellect? The Mulahida claimed education is confined to a specific group—yet you assume it's for everyone. What does that assumption say about your practice?

The Ninth Objection: Conditions of Obligatory Acts Are Not Automatically Obligatory
Al-Jurjani rejects the premise that any necessary condition for an absolute obligation is itself obligatory, and provides an answer based on the nature of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge, though necessary for the obligatory act of knowing Allah, is not itself legally obligatory, and how al-Jurjani argues that its obligation is indirect through the cause of reasoning.

Core Idea
Legal obligation does not automatically transfer to every condition of an act.

The ninth objection challenges a key premise: that any necessary condition for an absolute obligation is itself legally obligatory. Al-Jurjani responds by distinguishing between direct and indirect obligation. He introduces the concept of knowledge (ma'rifa) as a quality (kayfiyya) not within direct power, and shows that its obligation is fulfilled through the cause that leads to it. This nuanced argument refines our understanding of how legal obligations relate to their prerequisites.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Notice that the objector denies the premise that any necessary condition for an absolute obligation is itself legally obligatory.
2
Understand the response
Pay attention to al-Jurjani's answer: knowledge is not directly within human power (ghayr maqdoorah bi al-dhat) because it is a quality, not an act. Therefore, its obligation is indirect through the cause (reasoning).
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that legal obligation does not automatically apply to all conditions, only to those that are directly within human power. This refines the concept of 'absolute obligatory act'.
The commentary clarifies that 'absolute obligatory act' is defined as something not within one's power by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع لا نسلم أن ما لا يتم الواجب ( المطلق إلا به فهو واجب) شرعاً لأن الوجوب الشرعي إما خطاب الله، أو مترتب عليه، ويجوز أن يتعلق خطابه بشيء ولا يتعلق بما يتوقف عليه ذلك الشيء (قلنا : المعرفة غير مقدورة بالذات) أي لا يمكن أن تتعلق بها القدرة ابتداء (بل) هي مقدورة ( بإيجاب السبب المستلزم إياها ) قوله: (وأيضاً يمكن إلخ) فإنهما غير مقدورين ابتداء وإن كانا مقدورين بقاء. قوله : ( ومن ثمة عرف إلخ) حيث اعتبر فيه قيد الحيثية المشعرة بجواز عدم كونه واجباً مطلقاً من حيثية أخرى . قوله : ( واجب شرعاً) وإن كان واجباً عقلاً بمعنى أنه لا بد منه في حصول الواجب . قوله : (إما خطاب (الله المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين بالاقتضاء أو التخيير، وهذا عند الأصوليين بناء على أن الوجوب نفس الإيجاب الذي هو الأمر والفرق بينهما بالاعتبار. قوله : ( أو مترتب عليه أي عند الفقهاء فإنهم قالوا : الحكم ما ثبت بالخطاب لا نفسه. قوله : (قلنا المعرفة إلخ) خلاصة الجواب تخصيص ما لا يتم بالسبب المستلزم، والواجب المطلق بما لا يكون مقدوراً بذاته، وحينئذ يكون إيجابه الظاهر إيجاباً لذلك السبب حقيقة لعدم مقدوريته إلا من جهة ذلك السبب قوله : ( أي لا يكون إلخ) لعدم كونه فعلاً بل كيفية. قوله : ( بإيجاب السبب ) الصواب بمباشرة السبب .
(Ninth) We do not accept that what the absolute obligatory act cannot be completed without is itself obligatory in the Sharia, because legal obligation (wujub shar'i) is either the address (khitab) of Allah or that which is consequent upon it, and it is permissible that His address pertains to something and does not pertain to that upon which that something depends. (We say: Knowledge is not within one's power by itself (ghayr maqdoorah bi al-dhat), meaning that power cannot be related to it initially; rather, it is within one's power through the obligation of the cause (sabab) that entails it.) His words: (And also it is possible, etc.) because both (absence of knowledge and doubt) are not within one's power initially, although they are within one's power as to continuation. His words: (And from that it is defined, etc.) where the qualification of aspect (al-haythiyya) is considered, indicating the possibility of it not being an absolute obligation from another aspect. His words: (obligatory in the Sharia) even if it is obligatory rationally, meaning that it is indispensable for the attainment of the obligatory act. His words: (Either the address of Allah) related to the actions of the responsible agents by requirement or option, and this is according to the usuliyyun based on the understanding that obligation (wujub) is the same as the imposing (ijab) which is the command, and the difference between them is by consideration. His words: (or consequent upon it) i.e., according to the jurists (fuqaha'), for they said: The ruling (hukm) is what is established by the address, not the address itself. His words: (We say: Knowledge, etc.) The summary of the answer is the specification of what is not completed except by the cause that entails it, and the absolute obligatory act is that which is not within one's power by itself; then its apparent imposition is actually an imposition of that cause, because it is not within one's power except through that cause. His words: (i.e., it is not, etc.) because it is not an act but a quality. His words: (by the obligation of the cause) the correct reading is 'by direct engagement with the cause' (bi mubasharat al-sabab).
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth objection (al-tasi') against the necessity of the premise for the absolute obligatory act. The objector denies that whatever an absolute obligation depends on is itself legally obligatory (wajib shar'an). Legal obligation is either directly God's command (khitab) or consequent upon it, and it is possible for God to command something without commanding its prerequisites. The answer (قلنا) introduces a key distinction: knowledge (ma'rifa) is not intrinsically within human power (ghayr maqdoorah bi al-dhat) because it is a quality (kayfiyya) rather than an act. Therefore, its obligation entails the obligation of its cause (sabab), which is reasoning (nazar). The commentary explains that the absence of knowledge and doubt are not directly within power either, though they may be within power by continuation. The definition of 'absolute obligatory act' (al-wajib al-mutlaq) is refined: it is something that is not within one's power by itself, so its imposition is effectively an imposition of the cause that leads to it. A textual note corrects 'by the obligation of the cause' to 'by direct engagement with the cause' (bi mubasharat al-sabab), emphasizing that the obligation is to perform the act (reasoning) that causes knowledge.

Key Terms
الواجب المطلق
الوجوب الشرعي
الخطاب
غير مقدورة بالذات
السبب المستلزم
الكيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that any necessary condition for an obligatory act is itself obligatory in the same way.
Correction: The passage shows that legal obligation does not automatically transfer to every condition, especially when the condition is a quality not directly within human power; obligation may be indirect through a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is knowledge not directly obligatory despite being necessary?
A) Because knowledge is a quality (kayfiyya) and not an act within direct human power.
B) Because the Sharia only obligates actions, not states.
C) Because knowledge is impossible to attain.
D) Because the objector denied the necessity.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not directly obligatory because it is a quality beyond direct power; its obligation is fulfilled by obligating the cause (reasoning) that produces it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because Allah obligated you to know Him, you must already have that knowledge, and felt despair when you couldn't produce it? This lesson teaches that the obligation is on the journey of reasoning, not the destination of certainty. Where are you avoiding that journey?
?
Do you excuse yourself from seeking knowledge because you think you 'don't have the ability' to understand? The passage says knowledge is not directly within your power—but the cause (reasoning) is. What reasoning are you refusing to do today?
?
If you truly believe that the obligation to know Allah is fulfilled by engaging in the cause of reasoning, what changes about your daily priorities? Will you start today with one genuine reflection on a sign of Allah?

Distinction Between Nazar and Jadal
Defines nazar and jadal and proves nazar's commendability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between nazar (reflection) and jadal (argumentation) and why nazar is commended, not prohibited.

Core Idea
Nazar is reflection, not argumentation; it is praised by the Quran.

This lesson clarifies a common confusion: is all theological reasoning prohibited? The text distinguishes nazar (reflection) from jadal (argumentative debate). While jadal can be blameworthy, nazar is the commended contemplation of creation. Read on to see the definition and proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the core meaning.
Notice the contrast between the two terms.
2
Identify the definitions
Find the definitions of nazar and jadal. Jadal is 'investigation to compel another', nazar is 'reflection'.
These definitions are the foundation of the argument.
3
Analyze the proof
Notice the logical inference: prohibition of jadal does not imply prohibition of nazar. Then see the Quranic evidence praising reflection.
The Quranic verse shows nazar is pleasing to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنظر غير الجدل، فإن الجدل هو المباحثة لإلزام الغير، والنظر هو الفكر، ولا يلزم من كون الجدل منهياً عنه كون النظر كذلك كيف وقد مدحه الله تعالى بقوله: {ويتفكرون في خلق السموات والأرض ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلاً} [آل عمران: ١٩١] فيكون مرضياً لا منهياً.
And nazar is not jadal, for jadal is investigation to compel another, while nazar is reflection. And it does not follow from the fact that jadal is prohibited that nazar is likewise, for Allah has praised it by His saying: {And they reflect on the creation of the heavens and the earth: "Our Lord, You have not created this aimlessly"} [Aal-Imran: 191]. Thus it is pleasing, not prohibited.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and proof to distinguish nazar (theoretical reasoning) from jadal (argumentation). It first defines jadal as 'investigation to compel another' (mubāhatha li ilzām al-ghayr), emphasizing its adversarial and persuasive character. Nazar is defined simply as fikr (reflection). The argument then uses a logical inference: the prohibition of jadal does not entail the prohibition of nazar. The Quranic verse from Aal-Imran 191 is cited as evidence that nazar is commended, as it praises those who 'reflect on the creation of the heavens and the earth'. This shows nazar is pleasing (mardiyy), not prohibited. The logical structure is a straightforward disanalogy: jadal and nazar are distinct in nature and ruling.

Key Terms
النظر
الجدل
المباحثة
إلزام الغير
الفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That theoretical reasoning (nazar) is a form of argumentation and thus also prohibited.
Correction: Nazar is distinct from jadal; it is reflection, not adversarial debate, and it is praised in the Quran.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the passage give to show that nazar is not prohibited?
A) A hadith commanding reflection
B) A verse praising those who reflect on creation
C) The consensus of the scholars
D) A rational proof that nazar is necessary for knowledge

Key Takeaway
Nazar (reflection) is distinct from jadal (argumentation) and is praised in the Quran, making it a pleasing act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about religion, do you catch yourself trying to compel the other person to agree with you (jadal) rather than simply reflecting on the truth (nazar)? What would change if you entered each conversation with the sole intention of reflection?
?
Read the ayah: 'And they reflect on the creation of the heavens and the earth.' When was the last time you spent dedicated time reflecting on a single aspect of creation—not researching facts or arguments, but just pondering? What would it take to make that a daily habit?
?
The passage says nazar is pleasing to Allah. If you examine your typical day, how much time do you spend in silent reflection versus in debate or argument? Does your time allocation reflect a love for what is pleasing to Allah? What one activity can you replace with nazar this week?
