The Mu'tazilite Proof for the Obligation of Speculative Reasoning
Al-Jurjani presents the Mu'tazilite rational argument for the obligation of speculative reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites argued that knowledge of God is rationally obligatory, and therefore speculative reasoning is obligatory.

Core Idea
Fear of harm from possible divine punishment makes knowledge of God a rational duty.

In this lesson, we explore the Mu'tazilite method for establishing the obligation of speculative reasoning. They do not rely on revelation or consensus; instead, they argue that reason itself demands that we acquire knowledge of God to avoid harm. The passage presents a chain: fear arises from disagreement and blessings, fear is harm, repelling harm is obligatory, knowledge repels harm, and knowledge requires speculation—so speculation is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the argument builds step by step.
The passage is a single continuous argument, not a list of separate points.
2
Identify the two sources of fear
Find the two causes of fear that the Mu'tazilites mention: disagreement about the existence and attributes of God, and the observation of blessings implying a Bestower.
These are the starting points that trigger the rational obligation.
3
Understand the link from fear to harm
Note that fear itself is considered harm. The rational person, when afraid of a possible punishment, experiences harm, and reason obligates repelling harm.
This step is crucial: the argument moves from a possible danger to an actual duty.
4
Follow the chain to knowledge and speculation
Since repelling harm requires knowing God (to avoid punishment or fulfill gratitude), knowledge becomes obligatory. And since knowledge is only attained through speculation, speculation becomes obligatory too.
Each link depends on the previous one, forming a logical chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة فهذه الطريقة التي معتمد الأصحاب في إثبات وجوب النظر، وهي الاستدلال بوجوب المعرفة على وجوبه. طريقتهم أيضاً في إثباته إلا أنهم يقولون: المعرفة واجبة عقلاً، أي يتمسكون في إثبات وجوبها بالعقل لا بالإجماع والآيات، لأنها دافعة للخوف الحاصل من الاختلاف، أي من اختلاف الناس في إثبات الصانع وصفاته، وإيجابه علينا معرفته. فإن العاقل إذا اطلع على هذا الاختلاف الواقع فيما بين الناس جوز أن يكون له صانع قد أوجب عليه معرفته، فإن لم يعرفه ذمه وعاقبه، فيحصل له خوف. وغيره، أي الخوف الحاصل من غير الاختلاف كالنعم الظاهرة والباطنة، فإن العاقل إذا شاهدها جوز أن يكون المنعم بها قد طلب الشكر عليها، فإن لم يعرفه ولم يشكره عليها سلبها عنه وعاقبه، فيحصل له من ذلك أيضاً خوف. وهو أي الخوف ضرر للعاقل، ودفع الضرر عن النفس مع القدرة عليه واجب عقلاً، فإن العاقل إذا لم يدفع ضرره مع قدرته عليه ذمه العقلاء بأسرهم ونسبوه إلى ما يكرهه، وهذا معنى الوجوب العقلي. ولما كانت المعرفة واجبة عقلاً وكانت لا تتم إلا بالنظر كان النظر أيضاً واجباً عقلاً، لما عرفت. هكذا تمسكوا بهذه الطريقة.
As for the Mu'tazilites, this is the method upon which the masters rely in proving the obligation of speculative reasoning (nazar), and it is the inference from the obligation of knowledge (ma'rifa) to its obligation. Their method also in proving it is that they say: knowledge is obligatory by reason ('aql), meaning they adhere in proving its obligation to reason, not to consensus (ijma') and the verses (ayat), because it repels the fear (khawf) arising from disagreement (ikhtilaf), i.e., from people's disagreement concerning the affirmation of the Maker (al-Sani') and His attributes, and His imposition upon us of knowledge of Him. For the rational person, when he becomes aware of this disagreement occurring among people, considers it possible that he has a Maker who has imposed upon him knowledge of Him, and if he does not know Him, He will blame him and punish him, so fear occurs to him. And other than it, i.e., the fear arising from other than disagreement, such as the apparent and hidden blessings (ni'am), for the rational person, when he observes them, considers it possible that the Bestower has requested gratitude for them, and if he does not know Him and does not thank Him, He will strip them from him and punish him, so fear also occurs to him from that. And it, i.e., fear, is harm (darar) to the rational person, and repelling harm from oneself, with the ability to do so, is obligatory by reason. For the rational person, if he does not repel his harm while able to do so, all rational people would blame him and attribute to him what he dislikes, and this is the meaning of rational obligation. And since knowledge is obligatory by reason and is not completed except through speculation, speculation is also obligatory by reason, as you have known. Thus they adhered to this method.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite argument for the obligation of speculative reasoning. It begins by stating that their method is to infer the obligation of speculation from the obligation of knowledge. They argue that knowledge of God is obligatory by reason, not by revelation or consensus, because it prevents fear. Fear arises from two sources: first, the disagreement among people about the existence of God and His attributes; second, the blessings that imply a Bestower who may require gratitude. The rational person, realizing these possibilities, fears harm if he does not know God. Since fear is harm, and repelling harm is rationally obligatory, knowledge becomes rationally obligatory. Since knowledge is only attained through speculation, speculation is also obligatory. This argument assumes that rational obligation is established by universal blame of the one who fails to act.

Key Terms
معرفة
نظر
خوف
واجب عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites base the obligation of knowledge and speculation solely on revelation or consensus (ijma').
Correction: The passage explicitly says they adhere to reason, not consensus or verses, because reason alone establishes the obligation to avoid harm.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite argument presented, what are the two sources of fear that make knowledge of God obligatory?
A) Fear of punishment in the afterlife and fear of loss of blessings
B) Fear arising from disagreement about the existence of God and fear arising from blessings that imply a Bestower
C) Fear of God's wrath and fear of ignorance
D) Fear of social blame and fear of logical contradiction

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that because knowledge of God is necessary to avoid rational harm, and speculation is the only path to that knowledge, speculative reasoning becomes a rational obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt the fear of not knowing your Creator strongly enough to compel you to seek knowledge? What specific step will you take today to act on that fear?
?
Consider the countless blessings you enjoy—health, wealth, family. Do you truly fear that ignoring the Bestower might lead to their loss? How does that fear translate into a concrete plan to learn about God?
?
The rational person repels harm even if uncertain. Are you avoiding the speculative reasoning that could bring you certainty about God? What harm are you risking by your procrastination?

The Ash'ari Response: Denying the Premise of Fear
Al-Jurjani refutes the Mu'tazilite argument by denying that fear necessarily occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the Mu'tazilite argument from fear by denying its universal occurrence, based on empirical observation of most people's heedlessness.

Core Idea
Fear does not follow awareness; most people are heedless, so the argument fails.

In response to the Mu'tazilite argument that fear of punishment makes knowledge of God obligatory, al-Jurjani offers a direct empirical refutation. He accepts that reason can judge good and evil, but denies that fear universally occurs. This passage illustrates a key Ash'ari method: challenging the factual basis of a rational claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how al-Jurjani begins by conceding part of the Mu'tazilite position before making his denial.
The concessive opening is a rhetorical strategy.
2
Identify the key denial
Find where al-Jurjani says 'we deny the occurrence of the mentioned fear'. What is the reason given?
3
Understand the reason: lack of awareness
Al-Jurjani argues that fear does not occur because most people lack the awareness (shu'ur) that would cause it. They do not conceive of disagreement or the blessings implying a bestower.
This is an empirical claim about ordinary human experience.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider: if the fear that makes knowledge obligatory is not universal, then the Mu'tazilite argument fails to establish a universal rational obligation. This does not deny rational obligation altogether, only its particular proof from fear.
This connects to common misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نقول: بعد تسليم حكم العقل بالحسن والقبح في الأفعال وما يتفرع عليهما من الوجوب والحرمة وغيرهما، نمنع حصول الخوف المذكور لعدم الشعور بما جعلوا الشعور به سبباً له من الاختلاف وغيره. ودعوى ضرورة الشعور من العاقل ممنوعة. ذلك لعدم الخطور في الأكثر، فإن أكثر الناس لا يخطر ببالهم أن هناك اختلافاً بين الناس فيما ذكر، وأن لهذه النعم منعماً قد طلب منهم الشكر عليها، بل هم ذاهلون عن ذلك، فلا يحصل لهم خوف أصلاً.
We say: after conceding reason's judgment of good (husn) and evil (qubh) in actions and what branches from them of obligation (wujub), prohibition (hurma), etc., we deny the occurrence of the mentioned fear due to the absence of awareness of that which they made awareness of it a cause, from disagreement and other than it. And the claim that awareness is necessary for the rational person is denied. That is because in most cases the thought does not occur, for most people do not conceive in their minds that there exists disagreement among people in what was mentioned, and that these blessings have a bestower who has requested gratitude from them; rather they are heedless of that, so no fear occurs to them at all.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the first Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite argument. It begins by accepting the premise that reason can judge good and evil, and the resulting obligations. However, it denies the occurrence of the fear that the Mu'tazilites claim. The reason is that most people do not have the awareness required to generate such fear. They do not think about disagreement about God or about the blessings implying a Bestower. Therefore, the fear that would make knowledge obligatory does not universally occur. The claim that such awareness is necessary for every rational person is denied.

Key Terms
خوف
شعور
واجب عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this response denies rational obligation altogether.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly concedes rational judgments of good and evil; he only denies the specific argument from fear, not rational obligation itself.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Jurjani deny the occurrence of fear in most people?
A) Because they are not rational
B) Because they lack awareness of the disagreement and the blessings
C) Because they already know God
D) Because fear is not a valid basis for obligation

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani denies the universal fear premise by pointing to most people's heedlessness, thereby refuting the Mu'tazilite argument without rejecting rational obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that many people are heedless of the greatest realities. Are you one of them? Do you simply go through the motions of life without ever questioning why you exist or who gave you everything? What will it take for you to wake up from this heedlessness starting today?
?
You have just read that most people do not even conceive of the Bestower's request for gratitude. Is your heart aware of that request every day? If you are honest, do you live as if God has no claim on your thanks? Commit now to a specific action that will keep this awareness alive in your daily life.

The Risk of Speculation and Compound Ignorance
Al-Jurjani argues that speculation may not remove fear and can worsen ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speculation (nazar) can be dangerous, potentially leading to compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), which is worse than simple ignorance.

Core Idea
Speculation can backfire, producing worse ignorance than not speculating at all.

In this passage, al-Jurjani continues the Ash'ari defense against rationalists who claim that speculation necessarily yields sound knowledge and removes fear. He argues that speculation can err, leading to compound ignorance, and that the simple-minded may be better off. A prophetic hadith supports this caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its structure
Read the Arabic passage carefully: al-Jurjani concedes fear but denies that speculation removes it. Then he anticipates and refutes an objection. Finally, he cites a hadith.
Notice the logical flow: concession → denial → objection → refutation → evidence from revelation.
2
Understand compound vs. simple ignorance
Focus on the distinction between compound ignorance (jahl murakkab: being ignorant and thinking one knows) and simple ignorance (jahl basit: being aware of one's ignorance). The passage argues that the former is more dangerous.
Compound ignorance is a state where a person is certain of a falsehood.
3
Reflect on the hadith about the simple-minded
Consider the Prophet's saying that most of Paradise's inhabitants are the simple-minded. This suggests that intellectual sophistication in theology is not always beneficial and can be harmful.
This is a key proof that abstaining from speculation can be safer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سلم حصول الخوف، فلا نسلم أنه (أي العرفان الحاصل بالنظر) يدفعه (أي الخوف) إذ قد يخطئ فلا يقع العرفان على وجه الصواب لفساد النظر، فيكون الخوف حينئذ أكثر. لا يقال: الناظر فيه (أي في عرفانه تعالى) أحسن حالاً قطعاً من المعرض عنه بالكلية، لأنا نقول: ذلك ممنوع، لأن النظر قد يؤدي إلى الجهل المركب الذي هو أشد خطراً من الجهل البسيط، والبلاهة أدنى إلى الخلاص من فطانة بتراء. ألا ترى إلى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "أكثر أهل الجنة البله". ثم لنا في أنه (يعني النظر أو العرفان) لا يجب عقلاً، بل سمعاً: قوله تعالى: {وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولاً} [الإسراء: ١٥]، نفي الله سبحانه وتعالى التعذيب مطلقاً دنيوياً كان أو آخروياً.
And if the occurrence of fear is conceded, we do not concede that it (i.e., the knowledge obtained by speculation) repels it (i.e., fear), because it may err, so the knowledge does not occur correctly due to the corruption of speculation, and then fear would be even greater. It cannot be said: the one who speculates is in a definitely better state than the one who abstains entirely, for we say: that is denied, because speculation may lead to compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), which is more dangerous than simple ignorance (jahl basit), and stupidity is closer to salvation than a curtailed intelligence. Do you not see his saying (peace be upon him): 'Most of the people of Paradise are the simple-minded.' Then we have [the proof] that it (i.e., speculation or knowledge) is not obligatory by reason, but rather by revelation: His statement, the Exalted: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (Quran 17:15). Allah, glorified and exalted, negated punishment absolutely, whether worldly or otherworldly.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage continues the Ash'ari response with two further points. First, even if we grant that fear occurs, it does not follow that knowledge obtained through speculation removes it. The speculation might be erroneous, leading to incorrect knowledge and resulting in compound ignorance, which actually intensifies fear. The objection that the speculator is better than the one who abstains is denied, because speculation can lead to compound ignorance which is worse than simple ignorance. The Prophet's hadith that most of Paradise's inhabitants are the simple-minded indicates that those who do not engage in detailed speculation are not necessarily worse off. Second, the passage presents a positive argument from revelation: nothing is obligatory by reason alone. The Quranic verse (17:15) negates punishment before the sending of a messenger, implying that obligation and punishment depend on revelation, not reason. Therefore, both speculation and knowledge of God are obligatory only by revelation, not by reason.

Key Terms
جهل مركب
جهل بسيط
نظر
عرفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speculation always leads to correct knowledge and is always beneficial.
Correction: Speculation can err and lead to compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), which is worse than simple ignorance. It may increase fear rather than remove it.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what is the danger of speculation?
A) It always leads to disbelief
B) It may lead to compound ignorance, which is worse than simple ignorance
C) It is forbidden by revelation
D) It creates fear in the heart

Key Takeaway
Speculation is not guaranteed to produce sound knowledge; it can produce compound ignorance, which is spiritually more harmful than simple ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain about a religious matter, only to later discover you were wrong? How did that affect your trust in your own reasoning and your relationship with Allah? Are you holding onto any beliefs today that you have not critically examined against the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The Prophet said that most of the people of Paradise are the simple-minded (al-bulh). Does this make you feel defensive about your intellectual achievements? Could your pride in theological knowledge be a barrier to humility and salvation? Are you willing to embrace a simpler faith if it means being safer?
?
If speculation can produce compound ignorance—a state worse than simple ignorance—what does that mean for how you learn about your religion? Do you ever pretend to know something about Islam to avoid embarrassment? Can you admit your ignorance and seek knowledge with humility, even if it means starting from scratch?

Obligation is Only by Revelation
Al-Jurjani proves that obligation is only by revelation, citing Quran 17:15.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that no act is obligatory by reason alone; obligation depends on revelation, as proven by Quran 17:15.

Core Idea
No act is obligatory by reason; obligation comes only through divine revelation.

In this shorter passage, al-Jurjani presents a positive proof for the Ash'ari position that theological obligations (like knowing God) are not imposed by reason but by revelation. He cites the Quranic verse 'We were not to punish until We send a messenger' to show that punishment and obligation require revealed guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof from Quran
Read the Arabic and translation: al-Jurjani states that the act of knowing God is not obligatory by reason but by revelation, and then quotes Quran 17:15.
The verse is the core evidence; understand its implication.
2
Analyze the logical implication
The verse negates punishment before sending a messenger. This implies that there is no obligation (which entails punishment for violation) without revelation. Thus, reason alone cannot make an act obligatory.
Punishment is linked to obligation; if no punishment before revelation, then no obligation before revelation.
3
Apply to the context of knowing God
Al-Jurjani specifically means that speculation (nazar) and knowledge of God are not obligatory by reason, but only by revelation. This counters the rationalist view that reason itself imposes the duty to know God.

Source Text

Flawed Reasoning Increases Fear
Al-Jurjani explains the objection and responds that knowledge may not remove fear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why flawed reasoning about God can actually increase fear and why the one who reasons is not automatically better than the one who does not.

Core Idea
Flawed reasoning leads to compounded ignorance, which is worse than simple ignorance.

Al-Jurjani confronts a key objection to the obligation of reasoning about God. The objector claims that if reasoning produces true knowledge, it would remove the fear of ignorance. But what if reasoning goes wrong? This passage explores the dangerous consequences of flawed reasoning and challenges the assumption that any reasoning is inherently beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection is stated, then the response refutes it in two ways.
The passage uses classical argumentative style.
2
Identify the two points in the response
Find where al-Jurjani first argues that flawed reasoning increases fear, and second denies that the reasoner is better off. Mark these in the translation.
The second point is supported by a hadith.
3
Understand compounded vs. simple ignorance
Reflect on the distinction: compounded ignorance is thinking you know when you don't, which is more dangerous than simple ignorance (not knowing). Why is it more dangerous?
This is the core of the argument.
4
Consider the hadith about the simple-minded
The prophet said most of Paradise's inhabitants are the simple-minded (al-bulh). Why does this support the argument? It shows that sophisticated reasoning is not necessary for salvation.
This challenges the superiority of the reasoner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وإن سلم حصول الخوف (فلا نسلم أنه) أي العرفان الحاصل بالنظر ( يدفعه ) أي الخوف (إذ قد يخطئ فلا يقع العرفان على وجه الصواب لفساد النظر فيكون الخوف حينئذ أكثر ) لا يقال : الناظر فيه) أي في عرفانه تعالى (أحسن حالاً قطعاً من المعرض ) عنه بالكلية ( لأنا نقول ) ذلك (ممنوع) لأن النظر قد يؤدي إلى الجل المركب الذي هو أشد خطراً من الجهل البسيط ) والبلاهة أدنى إلى الخلاص من فطانة (بتراء ) ألا ترى إلى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام ( أكثر أهل الجنة البله )
And if it is granted that fear occurs, we do not grant that it [i.e., the knowledge obtained through reasoning] removes it—that is, the fear—because it may err and the knowledge may not occur correctly due to corruption of reasoning, so the fear would then be greater. One cannot say: the one who reasons about it—i.e., about knowing God—is certainly in a better state than the one who turns away from it entirely, for we say: that is denied, because reasoning may lead to compounded ignorance, which is more dangerous than simple ignorance. And simple-mindedness is closer to deliverance than a cut-off intelligence. Do you not see his saying, peace be upon him: 'Most of the inhabitants of Paradise are the simple-minded.'
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding the obligation to know God through reasoning. The objection is that even if one experiences fear due to not knowing God, obtaining knowledge through reasoning would remove that fear. The response refutes this on two grounds. First, reasoning can be flawed, leading to incorrect knowledge, which actually increases fear because one may have compounded ignorance (believing he knows when he does not). Second, the claim that the one who reasons is better than the one who does not is denied, because reasoning can lead to compounded ignorance, which is worse than simple ignorance. The commentary supports this with a hadith: 'Most of the inhabitants of Paradise are the simple-minded,' meaning those who do not engage in detailed reasoning but have simple faith. Thus, fear is not necessarily removed by reasoning.

Correction: Flawed reasoning leads to compounded ignorance, which increases fear and is spiritually dangerous.

Key Takeaway
Flawed reasoning about God leads to compounded ignorance, which is spiritually more dangerous than simple ignorance and actually increases fear, so the one who reasons is not automatically better off than the one who abstains.

The Quranic Proof that Obligation is Based on Revelation
Al-Jurjani presents the proof from the verse negating punishment before mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani uses the Quranic verse 'We were not to punish until We send a messenger' to argue that obligation is based on revelation, not reason, and how he refutes objections.

Core Idea
The Quran itself proves that no duty is obligatory until a messenger is sent.

Al-Jurjani continues his defense of the Ash'ari position that rational obligation is not established. He now presents a scriptural proof from the Quran, specifically the verse that negates punishment before the sending of a messenger. This passage shows how the verse is used to deny that any obligation is known by reason alone, and it addresses potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Jurjani uses a very compressed argument style with parenthetical interruptions.
2
Follow the logical steps of the proof
Identify the main claim, the Quranic verse, and how the verse negates punishment. Understand why punishment is a necessary consequence of obligation, and how that leads to the conclusion that obligation is not rational.
The proof relies on the Mu'tazilite premise that there is no forgiveness for leaving an obligatory duty.
3
Examine the objections and refutations
Notice the two objections: interpreting 'messenger' as reason, and limiting punishment to scriptural duties. See how al-Jurjani rejects both as contrary to the literal meaning and principle.
This demonstrates the importance of adhering to the apparent meaning unless evidence requires otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لنا في أنه ( يعني النظر أو العرفان ( لا يجب عقلاً) بل في أنه لا يجب شيء عقلاً ( بل سمعاً قوله تعالى : وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولاً ﴾ [الإسراء : ١٥] نفي الله سبحانه وتعالى ( التعذيب) مطلقاً دنيوياً كان أو أخروياً قبل البعثة وهو من لوازم الوجوب) بشرط ترك الواجب (عندهم) إذ لا يجوزون العفو ( فينتفي الوجوب قبل البعثة ) لانتفاء لازمه ) وهو ينفي كونه بالعقل) إذ لو كان الوجوب بالعقل لكان ثابتاً معه قبل بعثة الرسل ومحصوله أنه لو كان وجوب عقلي لثبت قبل البعثة، ولا شبهة في أن العقلاء كانوا يتركون الواجبات حينئذ، فيلزم أن يكونوا معذبين قبلها، وهو باطل بالآية ( لا يقال : المراد بالرسول ) في الآية الكريمة هو (العقل لاشتراكهما في الهداية (أو المراد من الآية (ما كنا معذبين بترك الواجبات الشرعية ) وليس يلزم من ذلك نفي التعذيب بترك الواجبات العقلية ( لأنا نقول كل واحد من حمل الرسول على العقل وتقييد التعذيب بترك الواجب الشرعي خلاف الوضع والأصل وحينئذ ( لا يجوز صرف الكلام إليه إلا لدليل) ولا دليل
Then we have [the argument] that it—meaning reasoning or knowledge—is not obligatory rationally; rather, nothing is obligatory rationally, but [it is obligatory] scripturally, by His saying: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' [al-Isra 15]. God, exalted, negated punishment absolutely, whether worldly or otherworldly, before the mission [of the messenger], and it [punishment] is a necessary consequence of obligation, given the condition of leaving the duty—according to them [the Mu'tazila] since they do not permit forgiveness. So obligation ceases before the mission due to the cessation of its necessary consequence. And this negates its being by reason, for if obligation were by reason, it would be established with reason before the mission of messengers. The summary: if it were a rational obligation, it would be established before the mission, and there is no doubt that rational beings would have left duties at that time, so it would follow that they would be punished before it, which is false by the verse. One cannot say: what is meant by 'messenger' in the noble verse is reason, because both share in guidance; or what is meant by the verse is 'We were not to punish for leaving scriptural duties,' and that does not entail negation of punishment for leaving rational duties. For we say: each of these—interpreting 'messenger' as reason and restricting punishment to leaving scriptural duties—is contrary to the literal usage and the principle. Therefore, it is not permissible to divert the speech to that without evidence, and there is no evidence.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents the central proof that the obligation to know God is not based on reason but on revelation (sam'). The proof uses the Quranic verse "وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولاً" (We were not to punish until We send a messenger). The argument proceeds: 1) The verse negates punishment absolutely before the sending of a messenger. 2) Punishment is a necessary concomitant of obligation (when the duty is left), according to the Mu'tazila who deny forgiveness. 3) Therefore, obligation must also be negated before the sending of a messenger. 4) If obligation were by reason, it would be present before any messenger, leading to punishment before messengers, which contradicts the verse. The passage then addresses two objections: first, that 'messenger' might mean reason itself; second, that the punishment might be limited to scriptural duties. Both are rejected as contrary to the literal meaning and principle, and no evidence supports them. Thus, the proof stands that obligation is scriptural, not rational.

بعثة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse only negates punishment for not following a prophet, but it does not address rational obligations.
Correction: The argument uses the negation of punishment to negate rational obligation because punishment follows from leaving any obligatory duty, so if obligation were rational, punishment would be due before any messenger, contradicting the verse.

Checkpoint
How does the verse 'وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا' prove that obligation is not rational?
A) It shows that punishment only occurs after sending a messenger, but if obligation were rational, punishment would be due before messengers.
B) It says reason is a messenger.
C) It only applies to worldly punishment.
D) It proves that obligation is rational because reason guides like a messenger.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse 'We were not to punish until We send a messenger' proves that no obligation is established by reason alone, because punishment for leaving an obligation is negated before a messenger arrives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on your own judgment to justify something you knew in your heart might be wrong, thinking 'it's not explicitly forbidden'? How does this lesson's insistence that obligation comes only from revelation challenge that habit?
?
The verse says God does not punish until He sends a messenger. Does this give you a sense of comfort or does it increase your responsibility? How are you truly responding to the message you have received?
?
Al-Jurjani dismisses the idea that reason can be a 'messenger' because it would violate the literal meaning of the Quran. Is there an area in your life where you are tempted to reinterpret clear divine commands to fit your own reasoning? What would it cost you to submit fully to the revelation?

The Binding Nature of the Proof: Ilzami vs Tahqiqi
Al-Jurjani explains the proof's dependence on Mu'tazila's denial of forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof about punishment before prophetic mission is considered a dialectical argument (ilzami) against the Mu'tazila rather than a direct rational proof (tahqiqi).

Core Idea
The proof only works against those who deny forgiveness.

Al-Jurjani delves into the nature of a key proof concerning punishment before a prophet is sent. He explains that the proof is not a stand-alone rational demonstration but a dialectical argument designed specifically to refute the Mu'tazila, who deny the possibility of divine forgiveness. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the different parts: the objection about Adam, the ilzami/tahqiqi distinction, the alternative formulation, and the discussion about the premise.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the ilzami vs tahqiqi distinction
Focus on the line 'إذ لا يجوزون العفو فالدليل إلزامي لا تحقيقي'. Ask yourself why the proof is only binding (ilzami) and not a real proof (tahqiqi). Relate this to the Mu'tazila's denial of forgiveness.
3
Examine the alternative real formulation
Look at the attempt to reformulate the proof as a real proof. Notice how it leads to the conclusion that one would not be safe from punishment. Then see how the promise of the Lawgiver provides a counter.
This shows the limits of pure reason in theology.
4
Analyze the necessary premise about rational beings
Read the last part about the premise that rational beings leave duties. Understand why this premise is needed to complete the argument. If punishment is denied, one might also deny the leaving of duties.
This highlights the careful structure of kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( قبل البعثة ولو كان مبعوثاً إلى نفسه كآدم عليه السلام ففي حقه نفي التعذيب قبل بعثته فما قيل : التعذيب قبل البعثة محال لأن أول المكلفين آدم عليه السلام فلا فائدة في نفيه ليس بشيء. قوله : (إذ لا يجوزون العفو فالدليل إلزامي لا تحقيقي إذ لا يجوز أن يكون استحقاق التعذيب متحققاً قبل البعثة بمجرد العقل، ويكون وقوعه منتفياً قبل البعثة قيل يمكن أن يقرر الدليل بوجه يكون تحقيقياً بأن يقال : لو وجب لاستحق العذاب بتركه، ولم يأمن وقوعه والتالي باطل لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا إذ به يحصل الأمن وفيه أن عدم الأمن من الوقوع بالنظر إلى الوجوب العقلي لا ينافي حصوله بوعد الشارع. قوله : ( ولا شبهة في أن العقلاء إلخ هذه المقدمة : مما لا بد منها لأن التعذيب ليس من لوازم الوجوب نفسه بل شرط ترك الواجب فلعل من نفي التعذيب يلتزم نفي الترك، فلا يتم الدليل إلا بضم هذه المقدمة، ولذا قال الشارح ومحصوله إشارة إلى أن ما ذكره المصنف ليس بتمام من غير عناية .
His saying: 'Before the mission even if he [the messenger] was sent to himself, like Adam, peace be upon him, then in his case the negation of punishment before his mission [applies]. So what is said that punishment before the mission is impossible because the first of the obligated is Adam, so there is no benefit in negating it, is not valid.' His saying: 'Since they do not permit forgiveness, the proof is binding (ilzami), not real (tahqiqi), because it cannot be that the deserving of punishment is actual before the mission solely by reason, and its occurrence is negated before the mission.' It was said that the proof can be formulated in a real way: if it were obligatory, then one would deserve punishment for leaving it, and one would not be safe from its occurrence. The consequent is false by His saying: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' because by it security is obtained. But [there is a consideration:] that lack of safety from occurrence with respect to rational obligation does not contradict its being obtained by the promise of the Lawgiver. His saying: 'And there is no doubt that rational beings...' This premise is indispensable because punishment is not a necessary concomitant of obligation itself, but rather a condition of leaving the duty. So perhaps one who negates punishment commits to negating the leaving [of the duty]. Thus the proof is not completed except by adding this premise. That is why the commentator said: 'And its summary' is an indication that what the author mentioned is not complete without attention.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage provides additional commentary on the proof from the Quranic verse. First, it addresses a minor objection about Adam being the first obligated, showing that the negation of punishment applies before his messengership. Then, it discusses the nature of the proof: it is a binding (ilzami) argument against the Mu'tazila because they deny God's forgiveness, but it is not a real (tahqiqi) proof in itself. The alternative real formulation is considered: if rational obligation were true, then one would deserve punishment for leaving it and would not be safe from punishment. However, the promise of the Lawgiver (God) could provide security, so the argument is not purely rational. The passage also emphasizes that the premise about rational beings leaving duties is necessary because punishment is not directly from obligation but from leaving the duty. Thus, if one negates punishment, it might imply that no one left the duty, which would break the argument. So the commentator notes that the author's discussion is not complete without this attention.

Misconception: The proof is a direct rational proof (tahqiqi) that stands on its own.
Correction: It is actually a dialectical argument (ilzami) that only works against those who deny forgiveness, like the Mu'tazila.

The proof about punishment before prophetic mission is a dialectical argument that only works against those who deny divine forgiveness, and it requires a specific premise about rational beings leaving duties.

?
Rational obligation alone does not guarantee safety—only the promise of the Lawgiver does. Have you placed your trust in your own reasoning about what you deserve, rather than humbly accepting God’s terms? What will you change after this lesson?

The Mu'tazilite Circularity Objection and the Ash'arite Response of Naqd
Al-Iji presents the Mu'tazilite circularity objection and the first answer (naqd).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the Ash'arite position on the obligation of reflection leads to a vicious cycle, and the Ash'arite first response of naqd (counterexample) that turns the argument back on the Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
The Ash'arites silence the Mu'tazilite objection by showing their own view suffers the same cycle.

This lesson examines a classic dialectical exchange in kalam. The Mu'tazilites argue that if reflection (nazar) is only obligatory by revelation, a cycle arises that would 'silence' the prophets—they cannot prove their prophethood. Al-Iji presents this objection and then the first Ash'arite response: the counterexample (naqd). By the end, you will see how this defense works and the depth of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
Read the opening argument carefully. The Mu'tazilites claim that if nazar is only obligatory by revelation, a cycle (dawr) occurs: nazar is needed to establish revelation, but revelation is needed to obligate nazar. This makes it impossible for a prophet to prove his prophethood.
Focus on the phrase 'لا أنظر ما لم يجب النظر علي' to grasp the refusal.
2
Identify the meaning of 'ifham'
The consequence of the cycle is 'ifham' — silencing the prophet. The mukallaf can simply refuse to reflect, leaving the prophet with no argument. Recognize why this is a serious challenge.
'Ifham' is a technical term meaning to render someone speechless in debate.
3
Examine the Ash'arite response (naqd)
The first answer is 'naqd' (counterexample). The Ash'arite points out that the same cycle occurs for the Mu'tazilites: their obligation of nazar by reason itself requires nazar to be known. So the objection is common to both positions.
The response does not deny the cycle but shows it is not unique to the Ash'arites.
4
Consider the rebuttal about fitri al-qiyas
The passage ends by considering a possible Mu'tazilite rebuttal: perhaps the obligation of nazar is known by a 'foundational syllogism' (fitri al-qiyas), making it self-evident. Reflect on whether this truly avoids the cycle.
The text leaves this open, showing the debate is nuanced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المعتزلة بأنه لو لم يجب النظر إلا بالشرع لزم إفحام الأنبياء وعجزهم عن إثبات نبوتهم في مقام المناظرة إذ يقول المكلف حين يأمره النبي بالنظر في معجزته وفي جميع ما تتوقف عليه نبوته من ثبوت الصانع وصفاته ليظهر له صدق دعواه لا أنظر ما لم يجب النظر علي فإن ما ليس بواجب علي لا أقدم عليه ولا يجب النظر علي ما لم يثبت الشرع عندي لأن المفروض أن لا وجوب إلا به ولا يثبت الشرع عندي ما لم أنظر لأن ثبوته نظري فيتوقف كل واحد من وجوب النظر وثبوت الشرع على الآخر وهو محال ويكون هذا كلاماً حقاً لا قدرة للنبي على دفعه وهو معنى إفحامه وأجيب عنه بوجهين الأول النقض وهو إنه أي ما ذكرتم من لزوم إفحام الأنبياء مشترك بين الوجوب الشرعي الذي هو مذهبنا والوجوب العقلي الذي هو مذهبكم فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا وإنما كان مشتركاً إذ لو وجب النظر بالعقل فبالنظر اتفاقاً لأن وجوبه ليس معلوماً بالضرورة بل بالنظر فيه والاستدلال عليه بمقدمات مفتقرة إلى أنظار دقيقة من أن المعرفة واجبة وإنها لا تتم إلا بالنظر وأن ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب فيقول المكلف حينئذ لا أنظر أصلاً ما لم يجب ولا يجب ما لم أنظر فيتوقف كل واحد من وجود النظر مطلقاً ووجوبه على الآخر لا يقال قد يكون وجوب النظر فطري القياس أي من القضايا التي قياساتها معها فيضع النبي له مقدمات ينساق ذهنه إليها بلا تكلف وتفيده العلم بذلك ضرورة فيكون الحكم بوجوب النظر ضرورياً محتاجاً إلى تنبيه على تلك المقدمات أو نظرياً قريباً من الضروري محتاجاً إلى أدنى التفات يحصل مع تلك المقدمات
The Mu'tazilites argued: If reflection (nazar) is not obligatory except by revelation (shar'), it would necessitate silencing the prophets (ifham al-anbiya') and their inability to prove their prophethood in the context of debate, because the legally responsible person (mukallaf), when the prophet commands him to reflect on his miracle and on everything on which his prophethood depends, such as the existence of the Maker and His attributes, to show him the truth of his claim, would say: I will not reflect as long as reflection is not obligatory upon me, for what is not obligatory upon me I do not undertake; and reflection is not obligatory upon me as long as revelation is not established according to me, because the presupposition is that there is no obligation except through it; and revelation is not established according to me as long as I do not reflect, because its establishment is theoretical (nazari); thus each of the obligation of reflection and the establishment of revelation depends on the other, which is impossible, and this would be a correct statement that the prophet has no power to repel, and that is the meaning of silencing him. And it was answered in two ways: The first: the counterexample (naqd), which is that it (i.e., what you mentioned about the necessity of silencing the prophets) is common between the revelation-based obligation (al-wujub al-shar'i) which is our doctrine, and the rational obligation (al-wujub al-'aqli) which is your doctrine. So whatever is your answer is our answer. And it is common because if reflection is obligatory by reason, then [it is known] by reflection unanimously, because its obligation is not known necessarily (daruratan), but rather by reflecting on it and inferring it from premises that require exact reflections: that knowledge is obligatory, that it is not complete except through reflection, and that what the obligatory cannot be completed except by is obligatory. So the legally responsible person would then say: I will not reflect at all as long as it is not obligatory, and it is not obligatory as long as I do not reflect; thus each of the existence of reflection absolutely and its obligation depends on the other. It cannot be said: Perhaps the obligation of reflection is [known] by a foundational syllogism (fitri al-qiyas), i.e., from the propositions whose syllogisms are with them; so the prophet lays out for him premises that his mind flows to without effort and that give him necessary knowledge of that. Then the judgment of the obligation of reflection would be necessary, needing a reminder of those premises, or theoretical but close to necessary, needing the slightest attention that occurs with those premises.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument. It begins with the Mu'tazilite objection against the Ash'arite position that rational reflection (nazar) is only obligatory by revelation. The objection claims that if nazar is not obligatory by reason, a cycle (dawr) arises: the prophet's command to reflect on his miracle requires the obligation of nazar, but that obligation depends on the establishment of revelation, which itself requires nazar. Thus the mukallaf can refuse to reflect, leaving the prophet powerless—this is 'silencing' (ifham). The Ash'arite response is given in two ways, but only the first is fully detailed here: the counterexample (naqd). The response points out that the same cycle occurs for the Mu'tazilites because they hold nazar to be obligatory by reason, but the obligation of nazar itself is not self-evident; it must be known through nazar and premises. Hence the mukallaf can again refuse, creating an identical cycle. The passage then anticipates a possible rebuttal: perhaps the obligation of nazar is known by a 'foundational syllogism' (fitri al-qiyas)—a proposition that inherently contains its own proof—so the prophet can present premises that make the obligation obvious. The response implicitly questions whether this truly avoids the cycle, but the text leaves the resolution open, noting that the judgment may be either necessary after a reminder or theoretical but very close to necessary.

Key Terms
النظر
الإفحام
الوجوب الشرعي
الوجوب العقلي
الدور
النقض
فطري القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'arite response merely rejects the Mu'tazilite premise, but it actually turns the same argument against them.
Correction: The response uses naqd (counterexample) by showing that the Mu'tazilite position also leads to a cycle, so the objection is not unique to the Ash'arites.

Checkpoint
What is the first response (al-naqd) to the Mu'tazilite objection that if nazar is only obligatory by revelation, it leads to silencing the prophets?
A) It denies that silencing would occur.
B) It argues that the same cycle occurs for the Mu'tazilites because their own obligation of nazar requires nazar.
C) It claims that nazar is not necessary for proving prophethood.
D) It accepts the cycle but says it is harmless.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite response of naqd shows that the Mu'tazilite objection applies equally to their own position, demonstrating the dialectical power of symmetrical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself demanding that Allah show you a clear sign before you obey, just like the skeptic in this passage? In that moment, were you truly seeking truth, or were you protecting your own pride from submitting?
?
The Ash'arite response shows that the skeptic's own reasoning is trapped in the same cycle. Where in your life might you be holding others to a standard that you yourself cannot meet—especially in matters of faith and obedience?
?
The passage forces you to see that the demand for certainty before action can be a form of self-deception. Is there an area where you have delayed obedience waiting for 'proof' while ignoring that your very delay proves your heart's rebellion? What step of faith will you take today?

Obligation Depends on Capacity to Know, Not Actual Knowledge
Refutation of the objection that obligation requires actual knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of a duty does not depend on a person's actual knowledge of it, but rather on the objective existence of the law and the capacity to know.

Core Idea
Obligation depends on the capacity to know, not on actual knowledge.

This lesson addresses a common objection: if obligation is not dependent on awareness, then nothing would be obligatory for a disbeliever. The author refutes this by distinguishing between the objective establishment of the law and the person's knowledge, and introduces the key condition of tamakkun (capacity).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and find the objection that if obligation does not depend on knowledge, circularity would result and nothing would be obligatory for a disbeliever.
2
Understand the author's response
Notice how the author separates 'obligation in itself' from the person's awareness. The law is established objectively regardless of knowledge.
3
Clarify the term 'ghafil'
The author defines the heedless as one who does not even conceive of obligation, not one who merely disbelieves. This prevents the charge of obligating the heedless.
4
Grasp the condition of tamakkun
The key solution: the condition for obligation is capacity (tamakkun) to know, not actual knowledge. This also refutes the Mu'tazilite objection.
Tamakkun means the possibility or potential to acquire knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لزم (الدور ولزم أيضاً أن لا يجب شيء على الكافر، بل نقول : الوجوب في نفس الأمر يتوقف على ثبوت الشرع في نفس الأمر والشرع ثابت في نفس الأمر علم المكلف ثبوته أو لم يعلم نظر فيه أو لم ينظر وكذلك الوجوب، وليس يلزم هذا تكليف من الغافل، لأن الغافل من لم يتصور التكليف لا من لم يصدق به كما مر، وهذا معنى قيل أن شرط التكليف هو التمكن من العلم به لا العلم به، وبهذا الحل أيضاً يندفع الإشكال عن المعتزلة، فيقال : قولك لا يجب النظر على ما لم أنظر باطل، لأن الوجوب ثابت بالعقل في نفس الأمر ولا يتوقف على علم المكلف بالوجوب والنظر فيه . قوله : ( وكذلك الوجوب) أي ثابت في نفس الأمر علم المكلف أو لم يعلم، نظر فيه أو لم ينظر، لكونه أثر الثبوت الشرع. قوله : ( وليس يلزم إلخ) دفع لما يتوهم من أنه لو لم يتوقف الوجوب على علم المكلف به يلزم تكليف الغافل وذا لا يجوز .
There occurred (circularity?) and also that nothing would be obligatory upon the disbeliever. Rather we say: Obligation in itself depends on the establishment of the Law in itself, and the Law is established in itself whether the accountable person knows its establishment or does not know, whether he reflects on it or does not reflect, and similarly obligation. And this does not entail obligating the heedless, because the heedless is one who does not conceive of obligation, not one who does not believe in it, as has passed. And this is the meaning of what was said: that the condition of obligation is the capacity to know it, not the knowledge of it. And by this solution the difficulty against the Mu'tazila is also repelled. So it is said: Your statement 'reflection is not obligatory upon me as long as I have not reflected' is false, because obligation is established by reason in itself and does not depend on the accountable person's knowledge of the obligation and reflection on it. His statement: 'and similarly obligation' meaning established in itself whether the accountable person knows or does not know, reflects or does not reflect, because it is the effect of the establishment of the Law. His statement: 'and this does not entail' is a refutation of what might be imagined: that if obligation did not depend on the accountable person's knowledge of it, then it would entail the obligation of the heedless, and that is not permissible.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and answer to an objection. The objector argues that if obligation is not dependent on the person's knowledge, circularity would result and nothing would be obligatory for a disbeliever. The author responds by distinguishing between obligation in itself (which depends on the objective existence of the Law) and the person's awareness. The Law is established in reality regardless of whether the accountable person knows or reflects. The term 'ghafil' (heedless) is clarified as one who does not even conceive of obligation, not one who merely disbelieves. The key solution is that the condition for obligation is 'tamakkun' (capacity or possibility to know), not actual knowledge. This also resolves the Mu'tazilite objection. The author then explains the commentary phrases: 'and similarly obligation' means it is established objectively; 'and this does not entail' blocks the misunderstanding that it would obligate the heedless.

Key Terms

تكليف
غافل

شرط التكليف

Checkpoint

A) Actual knowledge of the obligation.
B) The capacity (tamakkun) to know the obligation.
C) Having reflected on the obligation.
D) Being a believer.

Key Takeaway
Obligation is established objectively by the Law and depends on the capacity to know, not on actual knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you feel no inner urge to pray or reflect on Allah, do you assume that means no duty? Or could it be that you are suppressing your natural capacity—your tamakkun—to know the truth?
?
The heedless are those who cannot conceive of obligation. But you can conceive it. So today, what one concrete step will you take to fulfill the obligation you know you have, but have been avoiding?

The Necessity of Means: Intention as a Non-Necessitating Cause
Author argues that only a necessitating cause yields obligation; intention is not such.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intention (qasd) is not considered a necessitating cause and how this refutes the claim that reflection must be the first obligation.

Core Idea
Only a necessitating cause creates obligation; intention is not one.

The debate about the first obligatory duty often centers on whether it is reflection or knowledge. The author begins by clarifying a key principle: a premise (muqaddima) is only obligatory if it necessarily leads to its effect. Intention, being a voluntary act, does not meet this condition. This lesson examines that principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about necessitating cause
Read the passage and find where the author states that a premise is only obligatory if it is a necessitating cause (sabab mustalzim). Note the phrase 'إنما يتم في السبب المستلزم'.
This is the foundational principle.
2
Understand why intention differs
The author argues that intention (qasd) is not a necessitating cause. Reflect on why intention does not necessitate its effect—it is a voluntary act that can be withheld.
Intention is part of the condition, not a cause.
3
See how this refutes the first duty claim
The passage refutes the view that reflection is the first duty because it is preceded by ignorance. Since intention is not a necessitating cause, its obligation does not follow, and the argument for reflection fails.
This paves the way for the verbal dispute resolution.

His statement: (It is only complete in the necessitating cause, and intention is not like that, so its obligation is not necessary, and by this is also repelled what is said: that reflection is conditioned by the absence of knowledge, meaning simple ignorance of the sought thing in terms of its being sought, so it should be the first of obligations, while it is not voluntary, rather it occurs before capacity and will. Otherwise, meaning if that is not intended, but rather the first of duties absolutely, then the intention to reflect, because it is a premise for reflection which is absolutely obligatory, so it is also obligatory. And you have known that the obligation of the premise is only complete in the necessitating cause, not others. Then the author wrote in his manuscript thus: 'Otherwise, if we stipulate that it is voluntary, then reflection; otherwise, the intention to reflect.' Perhaps this is the meaning of those who said: Volitional acts that are not intended essentially, like intention, do not need another intention. And if we grant the necessity of infinite regress in attachments, we do not grant its impossibility because it is in mental considerations. His statement: (by consensus) i.e., from the people of the religion. The obligation of the condition or the part is impossible. I say: The impossible is the obligation of the conditioned or the whole together with the obligation of not (doing) the part or the condition, not with the absence of obligation for both, because obligation is the attachment of the address of God Almighty, and it is permissible that it attaches to something and does not attach to its part and condition, and its verification has passed. His statement: (for encompassing the three schools) i.e., the considered schools which are the madhhabs of the considered scholars. As for the statement that the obligatory is the first part of reflection, it is not regarded, for it is not hidden that the obligation's attachment to the whole is the primary intention, and to the part is implicit and secondary. And if you wish to include this madhhab as well, say: after his statement 'otherwise, if we stipulate that it is voluntary', then if it is not stipulated that it is a complete and original and intended obligation, then it is part of reflection; and if stipulated, then it is reflection. His statement: (and even if it can be directed etc.) is an indication of weakness because volitional acts that are not intended essentially, like intention, do not need another intention.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion on the necessity of means (muqaddima) and a resolution of the dispute. It begins by stating that a premise is only obligatory if it is a necessitating cause (sabab mustalzim). Intention (qasd) is not such a cause, so its obligation does not follow. This refutes the claim that reflection must be the first duty because it is preceded by the absence of knowledge. The author then presents the resolution: the dispute is verbal. If 'first obligatory duty' means what is primarily intended, it is knowledge. If it means absolutely first, it is intention (qasd) to reflect, since reflection is voluntary and requires intention. The author's own manuscript states: if we stipulate that it is voluntary, then reflection; otherwise, intention. This covers three schools but implies intention is involuntary while being obligatory. The rest of the passage defends this: the problem of infinite regress is avoided because intention itself does not require another intention—the will's attachment to itself is direct. The commentary further clarifies that intention is not a cause for reflection, and even if it were, it is not necessitating. The passage concludes by integrating the fourth school (first part of reflection) as part of the resolution.

Key Terms
سبب مستلزم
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every premise leading to an obligatory act is itself obligatory.
Correction: Only a premise that is a necessitating cause (sabab mustalzim) is obligatory. Intention is not necessitating, so it is not obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for a premise to be obligatory?
A) It must be voluntary.
B) It must be a necessitating cause.
C) It must be intended.
D) It must be the first step.

Key Takeaway
A premise is only obligatory if it is a necessitating cause; intention does not meet this condition, so its obligation does not follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The dispute about the first duty is verbal, but the real duty is knowledge. Do you possess the knowledge of Allah that you claim? If not, what concrete step will you take today to acquire it?

Verbal Dispute Resolved: The Three Schools on the First Duty
Author resolves the dispute verbally and provides a classification of three schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's resolution that the dispute over the first obligatory duty is verbal, and how his classification accommodates the three main schools of thought.

Core Idea
The disagreement over the first duty is simply about what 'first' means.

Having established the principle of necessitating cause, the author now tackles the famous debate: Is the first obligation knowledge of God or reflection? He reveals that the dispute is verbal, turning on the meaning of 'first'. This lesson presents his resolution and the classification he derives from his own manuscript.

Guided Path
1
Find the resolution statement
Read the passage and locate where the author says 'the dispute is verbal' (والنزاع لفظي). Understand why he says this.
This is the key to clarifying the debate.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'first'
The author distinguishes between 'first intended and essential' (المقصودة أولاً وبالذات) and 'first absolutely' (أول الواجبات مطلقاً). Identify these two meanings.
The first meaning gives knowledge; the second gives intention.
3
See how it covers the three schools
The passage mentions three 'considered schools' (المذاهب الثلاثة المعتبرة). They correspond to: 1) knowledge as first intended, 2) intention as first absolutely, and 3) reflection if voluntary is stipulated. Note this.
The author's manuscript formula captures them.
4
Notice the inclusion of the fourth school
The author also explains how to include the view that the first part of reflection is the duty, by stipulating it is not complete and original.
This shows the author's classification is comprehensive.

مسبوق : ( بالقصد المتقدم فيه أن التقدم لا ينفع ما لم يثبت كونه واجباً.
قوله : ( وقد عرفت إلخ) والقصد ليس سبباً للنظر، ولو سلم فليس مستلزماً له، ولو سلم فالنظر ليس غير مقدور حتى تكون مقدوريته باعتبار مقدورية ،مقدمته، ولو سلم فمقدورية المقدمة أعني القصد ممنوعة.
قوله : ( وإن أمكن توجيهه إشارة إلى ضعفه بأن يقال : لا نسلم لزوم التسلسل، بأن يكون قصد القصد عينه يعني أن كل ما سوى القصد أعني تعلق الإرادة يحتاج في كونه مقصوداً، ومراداً إلى تعلق الإرادة وأما تعلق الإرادة فلا يحتاج إلى إرادة أخرى، ولعل هذا مراد من قال: إن الإرادة الاختيارية إذا لم تكن مقصودة بالذات مثل القصد لا تحتاج إلى قصد آخر، ولو سلم لزوم التسلسل في التعلقات فلا نسلم استحالته لكونه في الأمور الاعتبارية. قوله : (اتفاقاً) أي من أهل الملة.
التكليف بالشرط أو الجزء ،محال قلت المحال هو التكليف بالمشروط أو الكل مع التكليف بعدم الجزء أو الشرط لا مع عدم التكليف بهما لأن التكليف تعلق خطاب الله تعالى ويجوز أن يتعلق بشيء ولا يتعلق بجزئه وشرطه وقد مر تحقيقه.
قوله : ( لشموله المذاهب الثلاثة (المعتبرة التي هي مذاهب العلماء المعتبرين، وأما القول بأن الواجب أول جزء من النظر، فلا يعتد به إذ لا يخفى أن الوجوب تعلقه بالكل هو القصد الأصلي، وبالجزء ضمني وتبعي، وإن شئت أن تدرج هذا المذهب أيضاً فقل : بعد قوله وإلا فإن شرطنا كونه مقدوراً، فإن لم يشترط كونه واجباً تاماً وأصلياً قصدياً فهو جزء النظر، وإن شرط فهو النظر.
قوله : ( وإن أمكن توجيهه إلخ إشارة إلى الضعف لأن الأمور الاختيارية إذا لم تكن مقصودة بالذات مثل القصد لا تحتاج إلى قصد آخر.
His statement: (It is only complete in the necessitating cause, and intention is not like that, so its obligation is not necessary, and by this is also repelled what is said: that reflection is conditioned by the absence of knowledge, meaning simple ignorance of the sought thing in terms of its being sought, so it should be the first of obligations, while it is not voluntary, rather it occurs before capacity and will. And if granted, then the obligation of reflection is restricted by it to prevent the acquisition of what is already obtained, so it is not a premise for the absolute obligation, and its continuation, even if it is voluntary by leaving the direct causes of obtaining knowledge, is not a premise. For if you say: intention is part of the condition of the necessitating cause or a condition of it, and the obligation of the conditioned or the whole without... the intention to reflect because reflection is a voluntary act preceded by intention which precedes its first parts. And the dispute is verbal. For if what is meant by the obligatory duty by the first intention, i.e., the first of the duties intended primarily and essentially, then it is knowledge by agreement. Otherwise, meaning if that is not intended, but rather the first of duties absolutely, then the intention to reflect, because it is a premise for reflection which is absolutely obligatory, so it is also obligatory. And you have known that the obligation of the premise is only complete in the necessitating cause, not others. Then the author wrote in his manuscript thus: 'Otherwise, if we stipulate that it is voluntary, then reflection; otherwise, the intention to reflect.' This is more fitting to the context because it encompasses the three considered schools, except that it indicates that intention is not voluntary while being obligatory. Its being involuntary, even if it can be directed by saying that if it were voluntary it would need another intention and choice, leading to infinite regress, but that the obligatory being involuntary is false by consensus. Imam al-Razi said: If what is meant is the first of obligations... Preceded by: (by the preceding intention, in which the precedence is of no benefit unless its being obligatory is established. His statement: (and you have known etc.) And intention is not a cause for reflection, and if granted, it is not necessitating for it, and if granted, reflection is not involuntary such that its volitionality is by virtue of the volitionality of its premise, and if granted, the volitionality of the premise, i.e., intention, is denied. His statement: (and even if it can be directed) is an indication of its weakness by saying: We do not accept the necessity of infinite regress, because the intention of the intention is the same; meaning that everything other than intention, i.e., the attachment of the will, needs in being intended and willed the attachment of the will; but the attachment of the will itself does not need another will. Perhaps this is the meaning of those who said: Volitional acts that are not intended essentially, like intention, do not need another intention. And if we grant the necessity of infinite regress in attachments, we do not grant its impossibility because it is in mental considerations. His statement: (by consensus) i.e., from the people of the religion. The obligation of the condition or the part is impossible. I say: The impossible is the obligation of the conditioned or the whole together with the obligation of not (doing) the part or the condition, not with the absence of obligation for both, because obligation is the attachment of the address of God Almighty, and it is permissible that it attaches to something and does not attach to its part and condition, and its verification has passed. His statement: (for encompassing the three schools) i.e., the considered schools which are the madhhabs of the considered scholars. As for the statement that the obligatory is the first part of reflection, it is not regarded, for it is not hidden that the obligation's attachment to the whole is the primary intention, and to the part is implicit and secondary. And if you wish to include this madhhab as well, say: after his statement 'otherwise, if we stipulate that it is voluntary', then if it is not stipulated that it is a complete and original and intended obligation, then it is part of reflection; and if stipulated, then it is reflection. His statement: (and even if it can be directed etc.) is an indication of weakness because volitional acts that are not intended essentially, like intention, do not need another intention.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion on the necessity of means (muqaddima) and a resolution of the dispute. It begins by stating that a premise is only obligatory if it is a necessitating cause (sabab mustalzim). Intention (qasd) is not such a cause, so its obligation does not follow. This refutes the claim that reflection must be the first duty because it is preceded by the absence of knowledge. The author then presents the resolution: the dispute is verbal. If 'first obligatory duty' means what is primarily intended, it is knowledge. If it means absolutely first, it is intention (qasd) to reflect, since reflection is voluntary and requires intention. The author's own manuscript states: if we stipulate that it is voluntary, then reflection; otherwise, intention. This covers three schools but implies intention is involuntary while being obligatory. The rest of the passage defends this: the problem of infinite regress is avoided because intention itself does not require another intention—the will's attachment to itself is direct. The commentary further clarifies that intention is not a cause for reflection, and even if it were, it is not necessitating. The passage concludes by integrating the fourth school (first part of reflection) as part of the resolution.

Key Terms
النزاع لفظي
أول الواجبات
القصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scholars genuinely disagree on whether knowledge or reflection is the first duty, with no way to reconcile.
Correction: The dispute is verbal; once 'first' is clarified (as primarily intended vs. absolutely first), the schools agree. Knowledge is first when intended, intention is first when absolutely first.

Checkpoint
According to the author's resolution, if 'first obligatory duty' means the duty intended primarily and essentially, what is it?
A) Reflection
B) Intention to reflect
C) Knowledge of God
D) First part of reflection

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the first obligation is resolved by clarifying the meaning of 'first'; the author's classification covers all major schools.

Does this lesson's classification of 'first' (by priority, by time, or by intention) change your personal religious priorities? Will you consciously reassess what you consider 'first' in your life today?

Does this make you more mindful of the weight of your intentions, even if they arise without choice? How will you hold yourself accountable for the intentions you harbour?

Refuting the Infinite Regress Objection in Intention
Commentary on the objection that intention is not possible and the response to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that intention is impossible due to infinite regress is refuted, and why infinite regress in conceptual matters is not necessarily impossible.

Core Idea
Intention avoids infinite regress because the will's attachment to itself does not need another will.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if intention (qasd) is obligatory but requires another intention to be chosen, we get an infinite regress. The commentator, following Imam al-Razi, shows that this regress is only apparent because the 'intention of intention' is the same act. Even if regress occurs, it is in conceptual matters (umur i'tibariyya), which do not entail impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the first part: the author's earlier statement implies that intention is not possible while being obligatory. This leads to an objection—if intention were possible, it would need another intention, causing infinite regress.
Notice the phrase 'غير مقدور مع كونه واجباً'—this is the tension.
2
Understand al-Razi's clarification
Imam al-Razi distinguishes between 'first obligations by first intention' and 'first obligations however they may be'. This shows the dispute is partly verbal.
Al-Razi's quote helps pinpoint the real point of contention.
3
Grasp the key refutation
The commentator responds: the 'intention of intention' is the same thing. Voluntary acts like intention do not need another intention because they are not intended by essence.
The phrase 'قصد القصد عينه' is crucial—it stops the regress.
4
Appreciate the final concession
Even if one concedes infinite regress in attachments of will, it occurs in conceptual matters (amr i'tibari), which are not impossible.
This refutes the claim that regress is always impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أنه يدل على أن القصد غير مقدور مع كونه واجباً، وعدم مقدوريته وإن أمكن توجيهه بأنه لو كان مقدوراً لاحتاج إلى قصد واختيار آخر، ويلزم التسلسل لكن كون الواجب غير مقدور باطل اتفاقا. قال الإمام الرازي : إن أريد أول الواجبات المقصودة بالقصد الأول فهو المعرفة عند من يجعلها مقدورة، والنظر عند من لا يجعل العلم الحاصل عقيبه مقدورا بل واجب الحصول، وإن أريد أول الواجبات كيف كانت فهو القصد. قوله : ( وإن أمكن توجيهه إشارة إلى ضعفه بأن يقال : لا نسلم لزوم التسلسل، بأن يكون قصد القصد عينه يعني أن كل ما سوى القصد أعني تعلق الإرادة يحتاج في كونه مقصوداً ومراداً إلى تعلق الإرادة وأما تعلق الإرادة فلا يحتاج إلى إرادة أخرى، ولعل هذا مراد من قال: إن الإرادة الاختيارية إذا لم تكن مقصودة بالذات مثل القصد لا تحتاج إلى قصد آخر، ولو سلم لزوم التسلسل في التعلقات فلا نسلم استحالته لكونه في الأمور الاعتبارية.
Except that it indicates that the intention is not possible while being obligatory. And its non‑possibility, even if it can be explained by saying: if it were possible, it would need another intention and choice, leading to an infinite regress. But an obligatory thing being impossible is false by agreement. Imam al‑Razi said: If by the first obligations are meant those intended by the first intention, then it is knowledge for those who consider it possible, and reflection for those who do not consider the ensuing knowledge possible but rather necessarily obtained. And if by the first obligations are meant however they may be, then it is the intention. His statement: 'And even if it can be explained' is an indication of its weakness by saying: we do not concede the necessity of infinite regress, because the intention of intention is the same thing; that is, everything other than the intention, namely the attachment of will, needs in being intended and willed another attachment of will. But the attachment of will does not need another will. Perhaps this is what is meant by the one who said: voluntary matters that are not intended by essence, like intention, do not need another intention. And even if we concede the necessity of infinite regress in attachments, we do not concede its impossibility because it is in conceptual matters.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation regarding the possibility of intention. It begins by noting a problem: the author's earlier statement implies that intention is not possible while being obligatory, which is false by agreement. Imam al‑Razi is then quoted to show that the dispute is verbal and to clarify the different positions. The commentary then explains the response to the infinite regress objection: voluntary acts like intention do not need another intention because the attachment of the will itself is not intended by essence. Even if regress is conceded, it occurs in conceptual matters, which are not impossible. This refutes the claim that intention is impossible.

Abu Hashim's View: The First Obligation Is Doubt
Abu Hashim argues that the first obligation is doubt, with a proof by elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that doubt is the first obligation for theological reflection, and why it is a necessary precondition.

Core Idea
Without prior doubt, reflection is impossible—either you already know or you cannot seek.

Abu Hashim presents a compelling case that the very act of seeking knowledge through reflection requires a starting point of uncertainty. He argues that if you already have certainty, reflection is pointless; if you are certain of the opposite, it's futile. Only doubt opens the door to valid inquiry. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
2
Identify the three possibilities
After conceiving the two ends of the desired object, the passage lists three possibilities: certainty, certainty of the opposite, and hesitation. Note how each affects the validity of reflection.
3
Understand why doubt is necessary
Consider why only hesitation (doubt) permits valid reflection. The other two possibilities make reflection either redundant or impossible.
This is the core of Abu Hashim's proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
If doubt is the only state that allows valid reflection, then it must be the first obligation—the starting point of theological inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم هو أي أول الواجبات الشك لأن القصد إلى النظر بلا سابقة شك، يقتضي طلب تحصيل الحاصل أو وجود النظر مع ما يمنعه. ألا ترى أنك مع عدم لزوم قوله : ( ألا ترى إلخ) تنوير للزوم أحد الأمرين عند عدم سابقة الشك، وحاصله أنه لا بد للناظر لتحصيل المعرفة من تصور طرفي المطلوب، فبعد تصورهما إما أن يحصل له الجزم بالنسبة فتكون المعرفة حاصلة له بالبديهة، فيمتنع النظر فيه لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، وإما أن يحصل له الجزم بنقيضه، فيمتنع النظر حينئذ منه لتحصيل المعرفة لامتناع طلب ما جزم بانتفائه، أو لا يحصل له الجزم بشيء من طرفي النسبة فيكون متردداً فيه، فيصح النظر منه حينئذ وهو المعنى بالشك.
And Abu Hashim said: It—that is, the first of obligations—is doubt, because intending to reflect without prior doubt necessitates seeking what is already obtained or the existence of reflection along with what prevents it. Do you not see that with absence of necessity? His statement: 'Do you not see etc.' is an illumination of the necessity of one of two things when prior doubt is absent. Its summary is that the one who reflects to obtain knowledge must conceive the two ends of the desired object. After conceiving them, either he obtains certainty about the relation, so knowledge is obtained by him intuitively, and reflection is then impossible because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible; or he obtains certainty about its opposite, so reflection is then impossible from him to obtain knowledge because seeking what he is certain is absent is impossible; or he does not obtain certainty about any side of the relation, so he is in hesitation, and then reflection is valid from him, and that is the meaning of doubt.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's view that the first obligation is doubt (shakk). It is a proof by necessity: without prior doubt, the intention to reflect would lead to a contradiction—either seeking what is already known (if one is already certain) or seeking what is impossible (if one is certain of the opposite). The only scenario where reflection is valid is when one is in a state of hesitation (taraddud), i.e., doubt. The commentary then explains the three possibilities after conceiving the two ends of the proposition: certainty, certainty of the opposite, or hesitation. Only hesitation permits useful reflection. Thus, the first obligation must be doubt.

الشك
تحصيل الحاصل
