Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doubt is always a sinful state that should be avoided at all costs.
Correction: In kalam, doubt (shakk) is the absence of certainty and a necessary starting point for valid reflection; it is not blameworthy in itself.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, why is doubt the first obligation?
A) Because knowledge requires prior ignorance.
B) Because without doubt one either seeks what is already obtained or what is impossible.
C) Because reflection is impossible without certainty.
D) Because doubt is the most pious state.

Key Takeaway
Doubt is the only state that allows for valid theological reflection, making it the first obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where in your life are you avoiding the first obligation of doubt—and what truth might you discover if you dared to confess your uncertainty before God?
?
Abu Hashim teaches that seeking what you already have is futile. In your daily religious practice, are you going through motions of reflection on matters you have already closed off? What would it cost you to reopen the door of doubt in a way that leads to deeper certainty?
?
If doubt is the first obligation, are you willing to start from a place of not-knowing? What specific belief about Allah or the unseen do you hold so tightly that you refuse to examine it? What would it look like to subject that belief to genuine inquiry this week?

The Proof that Doubt is Necessary for Seeking Knowledge
Commentator explains the logical necessity of doubt before valid inquiry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why genuine doubt is a necessary condition for valid rational inquiry in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Without doubt, inquiry is either redundant or impossible.

The commentator presents a crisp logical argument to establish that doubt is the only mental state that makes rational inquiry meaningful. Before we can seek knowledge through reasoning, we must first recognize what we do not know. This passage shows the three possible outcomes of conceptualizing a question and why only one leads to valid inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
The passage uses a trilemma structure.
2
Identify the three cases
After conceiving the two ends of the sought matter, the inquirer can have certainty, certainty of the opposite, or no certainty. Note which case leads to valid inquiry.
3
Reflect on the refutation
The commentator refutes the idea that certainty from blind following (taqlid) can coexist with seeking knowledge. Think about why this is rejected.
Also note the role of conjecture (zann) as distinct from doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ألا ترى إلخ) تنوير للزوم أحد الأمرين عند عدم سابقة الشك، وحاصله أنه لا بد للناظر لتحصيل المعرفة من تصور طرفي المطلوب، فبعد تصورهما إما أن يحصل له الجزم بالنسبة فتكون المعرفة حاصلة له بالبديهة، فيمتنع النظر فيه لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، وإما أن يحصل له الجزم بنقيضه، فيمتنع النظر حينئذ منه لتحصيل المعرفة لامتناع طلب ما جزم بانتفائه، أو لا يحصل له الجزم بشيء من طرفي النسبة فيكون متردداً فيه، فيصح النظر منه حينئذ وهو المعنى بالشك، فاندفع ما قيل إنه يجوز أن يكون الجزم بالتقليد فيطلب المعرفة، وإنه كلام. إذا تصورت طرفي المطلوب فإن جزمت به كان حاصلاً، وإن جزمت بنقيضه كان مانعاً، وأنت تعلم أن انتفاء الجزم لا يستلزم الشك، لجواز أن يكون هناك ظن بالمطلوب أو بنقيضه، فيجوز القصد إلى النظر لتحصيل العلم
His statement: 'Do you not see, etc.' is an illumination of the necessity of one of two matters when there is no prior doubt. Its summary is that the viewer, to acquire knowledge, must conceive the two ends of the sought matter. After conceiving them, either he obtains certainty of the relation, so knowledge is obtained by him intuitively, and inquiry into it is impossible because obtaining the obtained is impossible; or he obtains certainty of its opposite, so inquiry is then impossible for him to acquire knowledge because it is impossible to seek what he is certain is non-existent; or he does not obtain certainty of either side of the relation, so he is hesitant about it, and inquiry from him is then valid, and that is the meaning of doubt. So what was said—that it is permissible for one to be certain by blind following and still seek knowledge—is refuted, and it is speech. If you conceive the two ends of the sought matter, if you are certain of it, it is already present; if you are certain of its opposite, it is a hindrance; and you know that the absence of certainty does not entail doubt, because there may be conjecture (ẓann) about the sought matter or its opposite, so intending inquiry to acquire knowledge is permissible.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the necessity of doubt (shakk) as a prerequisite for valid inquiry (naẓar) and the acquisition of knowledge (ma‘rifa). The commentator begins by explaining the statement 'Do you not see' (alā tarā) from the base text. He clarifies that for an inquirer to acquire knowledge, he must first conceive both ends (ṭarafī al-maṭlūb) of the proposition under investigation. After conceptualization, three cases are possible:

1) He obtains certainty (jazzm) of the relation—knowledge is already intuitively present, so inquiry is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil).
2) He obtains certainty of its opposite—inquiry is also impossible because one cannot seek what one knows to be false.
3) He does not obtain certainty either way—he is hesitant (mutaraddid) and thus in a state of doubt (shakk). In this case, inquiry is valid and leads to knowledge.

The commentator then refutes an objection: one might be certain through blind following (taqlīd) yet still seek knowledge. He dismisses this as mere speech (kalam), implying that such certainty is not genuine. The passage then restates the proof in first person: if you are certain of the matter, it is already present; if certain of its opposite, it is an obstacle; lack of certainty does not imply doubt only, as there could be conjecture (ẓann). Nonetheless, the intention to seek knowledge is permissible in such cases. The proof follows a logical trilemma: only the doubtful person can properly engage in inquiry.

Key Terms
الشك
النظر
المعرفة
الجزم
التقليد
الظن
الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that certainty from taqlid (blind following) suffices for inquiry.
Correction: The passage shows that genuine doubt is required for valid inquiry; taqlid-based certainty is not genuine and does not permit meaningful inquiry.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that inquiry is impossible when one is already certain of the matter?
A) Because certainty is a form of knowledge that cannot be obtained again.
B) Because the inquirer would be seeking what he already has, which is impossible.
C) Because certainty prevents action.
D) Because certainty leads to arrogance.

Key Takeaway
For rational inquiry to be meaningful, the inquirer must be in a state of genuine doubt, not possessing certainty or its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or listen to a lecture, do you find yourself already certain of the meaning before really investigating, perhaps because of upbringing or habit? Could that be a form of taqlīd that hinders true understanding? Reflect on a time you confidently affirmed a belief without genuine inquiry. How can you cultivate a state of honest doubt that leads to deeper conviction?
?
The passage says that if you are certain of the truth, inquiry is impossible. But often we think we are certain. How certain are you really of the foundations of your faith? Is it possible that your certainty is actually a comfortable taqlīd that stops you from seeking a more personal knowledge? What would it mean to doubt honestly without losing faith?
?
The author dismisses the objection that one can be certain through taqlīd and still seek knowledge. Are there areas in your life where you are content with second-hand certainty? What is one belief you hold based on authority that you could re-examine with genuine inquiry?

The First Objection: Is Doubt Within Power?
Objection and counter-argument about the power over doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to Abu Hashim's view that doubt is the first obligation, and why the commentator finds it questionable based on the equal relation of power to opposites.

Core Idea
Power relates equally to opposites; denying it for doubt implies denying it for knowledge.

The first objection claims that doubt is not within human power, so it cannot be obligatory. However, the commentator points out a problem: if doubt were not within power, then knowledge, its opposite, would also not be within power—contradicting Abu Hashim's own view that knowledge is within power. This lesson examines the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage from '(ورد) قول أبي هاشم ( بوجهين الأول أن الشك غير مقدور' up to 'فلا نسلم كونه غير مقدور'. Notice the objection and the immediate response.
The commentator does not accept the objection at face value.
2
Understand the principle of power and opposites
The commentator invokes the principle that power (qudra) relates equally to opposites. Why does this principle matter?
This is a key concept in kalam.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the objection were accepted, it would force Abu Hashim to deny that knowledge is within power, which he affirms. So the objection is self-defeating.
This shows the importance of consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ورد) قول أبي هاشم ( بوجهين الأول أن الشك غير مقدور فلا يكون واجباً إجماعاً ( وفيه نظر إذ لو لم يكن) الشك ( مقدوراً لم يكن العلم أيضاً (مقدوراً لأن القدرة نسبتها إلى الضدين سواء) عند أبي هاشم والعلم مقدور عنده فيكون الشك عنده أيضاً مقدوراً، فلا نسلم كونه غير مقدور، قال الآمدي : ( والحق أن ابتداء الشك غير مقدور للعبد بل هو واقع بغير اختياره إلا أن يجوز أن يكون النظر عند الجزم بالنقيض للتقوية، فأصاب والبحث الذي أورده الشارح لقوله وأنت تعلم إلخ . قوله : ( والعلم مقدور عنده رد لما في شرح المقاصد من أن العلم غير مقدور عنده، إنما المقدور تحصيله لمباشرة الأسباب فاعتراض المواقف ساقط . قوله : (بل هو واقع بغير اختياره في شرح المقاصد أن تحصيله واستدامته مقدور، بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين ويترك النظر في النسبة، وفيه أن اللازم منه عدم حصول النسبة لا التردد فيه وتجويز الطرفين. قوله : ( وأنت خبير إلخ) يعني أنه إن كان مقصود الآمدي بيان الواقع، فهو حق وإن كان مقصوده دفع الاعتراض عن أبي هاشم فلا ينفع، لأن الشرط عنده ابتداء الشك بمعنى التردد في النسبة.
The statement of Abu Hashim was refuted in two ways. First, that doubt (al-shakk) is not within power (ghayr maqdūr), so it cannot be obligatory by consensus. But there is consideration: if doubt were not within power, then knowledge would also not be within power, because the capacity (al-qudra) relates to opposites equally, and according to Abu Hashim, knowledge is within power, so doubt would also be within power according to him. So we do not concede that it is not within power. Al-Amidi said: 'The truth is that the beginning of doubt is not within the slave's power, rather it occurs involuntarily, unless it is permissible that inquiry be for strengthening when certain of the opposite.' He was correct, and the discussion that the commentator brought for his statement 'and you know, etc.' His statement: 'And knowledge is within power according to him' is a response to what is in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that knowledge is not within power according to him; rather, what is within power is its acquisition by directly engaging causes, so the objection from al-Mawāqif is invalid. His statement: 'Rather it occurs involuntarily'—in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, its acquisition and continuance are within power, by conceiving the two ends and leaving inquiry into the relation. But the necessary consequence of that is not obtaining the relation, not hesitation about it and considering both sides. His statement: 'And you are well-acquainted, etc.' means that if al-Amidi intended to state the reality, it is true, but if he intended to defend Abu Hashim, it is of no use, because the condition according to him is the beginning of doubt in the sense of hesitation about the relation.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning Abu Hashim's view that the first obligation is doubt (shakk). The commentator presents two objections. The first objection: doubt is not within one's power (ghayr maqdūr), so it cannot be obligatory by consensus. However, the commentator notes that this objection itself is open to consideration: if doubt were not within power, then knowledge would also not be within power, because power (qudra) relates to opposites equally. Since Abu Hashim holds that knowledge is within power, he must also hold that doubt is within power. So the objection fails.

Al-Amidi then intervenes with a clarification: the beginning of doubt (ibtidā’ al-shakk) is indeed involuntary, not within the slave's power, unless one considers inquiry even when certain of the opposite as a means of strengthening (taqwīya). The commentator then addresses further sub-issues:
- The statement 'knowledge is within power according to him' refutes a claim in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that knowledge is not within power; rather, its acquisition through causes is within power.
- The claim that doubt is involuntary is qualified: its acquisition and continuance can be within power by conceiving the two ends and leaving inquiry, but this leads to lack of obtaining the relation, not hesitation.
- The final comment evaluates al-Amidi's intent: if he meant to describe reality, he is correct; if he meant to defend Abu Hashim, it fails because the condition for Abu Hashim is the beginning of doubt as hesitation (taraddud) in the relation, which is not within power. Thus the discussion reinforces that doubt is not a voluntary act that can be obligated.

Key Terms
الشك
المقدور
القدرة
الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doubt is entirely involuntary and therefore cannot be an obligation.
Correction: The commentator shows that this reasoning would also make knowledge involuntary, which is inconsistent with Abu Hashim's position.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the first objection that doubt is not within power?
A) Because doubt is always voluntary.
B) Because if doubt is not within power, knowledge would also not be within power, contradicting Abu Hashim's view that knowledge is within power.
C) Because the objection is based on a consensus that is unreliable.
D) Because doubt is the opposite of knowledge and power relates to opposites equally.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because it conflicts with the principle that power relates equally to opposites and with Abu Hashim's own commitments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience doubt about your faith, do you treat it as something that simply happens to you, or do you recognize that you have the capacity to address it through seeking knowledge? What would change if you truly internalized that doubt, like knowledge, is within your power?
?
How often do you excuse yourself from pursuing certainty in matters of belief by saying 'I just can't help having doubts'? In light of the principle that power relates equally to opposites, is that excuse valid? What step can you take today to transform a specific doubt into certainty?
?
If you accept that knowledge of fundamental truths is a moral obligation, then doubt is a failure to act on your capacity. What are the doubts you have left unaddressed? What concrete action—reading, asking a scholar, or reflecting—will you commit to this week to replace that doubt with knowledge?

Al-Amidi's Clarification and the Final Assessment
Al-Amidi's truth and its limitation as a defense of Abu Hashim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's distinction between the beginning of doubt and its acquisition, and why the commentator argues it does not defend Abu Hashim's view.

Core Idea
Involuntary beginning of doubt cannot be obligated, but indirect acquisition might be.

Al-Amidi clarifies that the beginning of doubt is involuntary, but its acquisition and continuance may be within power. The commentator examines this distinction and evaluates whether it truly supports Abu Hashim's position that the first obligation is doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's statement
Read from 'قال الآمدي' to the end of the passage. Notice his claim about the beginning of doubt.
Al-Amidi distinguishes between initial doubt and its maintenance.
2
Understand the commentator's analysis
The commentator addresses each part: the response about knowledge, the involuntary nature, and the final verdict.
The commentator uses cross-references to other works.
3
Evaluate the defense
Ask yourself: Does al-Amidi's clarification actually save Abu Hashim's view? The commentator says it does not if the condition is the beginning of doubt as hesitation.
This is the key point.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning Abu Hashim's view that the first obligation is doubt (shakk). The commentator presents two objections. A) Because al-Amidi's statement is incorrect.
B) Because the condition for Abu Hashim is the beginning of doubt as hesitation, which is not within power.
C) Because knowledge is not within power.
D) Because doubt is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's distinction does not rescue Abu Hashim's view because the original obligation requires the initial, involuntary doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time when a doubt about your faith first arose—did you choose it or did it come to you involuntarily? How does this lesson's distinction between the involuntary beginning and your subsequent response change how you will handle such a moment next time? Before moving on, commit to one concrete action you will take when doubt next arises.
?
Al-Amidi says that inquiry is permissible only for strengthening when you are already certain. Are you actively seeking to strengthen your faith, or are you coasting on past certainties? Take a moment to identify one belief for which you will seek deeper understanding this week, and write down a specific resource or step you will use.
?
If the beginning of doubt is not within your power, then you cannot be blamed for the initial thought—but you are responsible for what you do next. Are you feeding your doubts with endless speculation, or are you turning to inquiry that strengthens? Honestly assess: is there a hesitation in obedience that you have used as an excuse to avoid seeking the truth? Decide now: will you remain in passive hesitation, or will you actively pursue clarity?

Certainty, Doubt, and the First Obligation
Al-Amidi establishes the first obligation and introduces the controversy over doubt's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amālī's initial framework for the first obligation and the debate over whether doubt is within human power.

Core Idea
The first obligation hinges on certainty; doubt's being within power is contested.

This lesson opens a critical debate in kalām: what is the first duty of the accountable person? Al-Āmidī presents the initial framework of certainty and doubt, then introduces Abū Hāshim's position that doubt is not within power, leading to a nuanced discussion on the nature of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the main claim about certainty
Notice that certainty either attains the required or is an impediment; absence of certainty does not imply doubt.
3
Understand Abū Hāshim's argument
He claims doubt is not within power (maqdūr), so it cannot be obligatory. Find the two 'aspects' (wajhayn).
4
See the counterargument
If knowledge is within power (as Abū Hāshim holds), then doubt should also be within power because power relates equally to opposites.
This is the key logical point.
5
Reflect on the implication
Why does this matter for the first obligation? If doubt is not within power, it cannot be commanded; if it is, then perhaps it could be required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بالمطلوب أو بنقيضه، فيجوز القصد إلى النظر لتحصيل العلم (ورد) قول أبي هاشم ( بوجهين الأول أن الشك غير مقدور فلا يكون واجباً إجماعاً ( وفيه نظر إذ لو لم يكن) الشك ( مقدوراً لم يكن العلم أيضاً (مقدوراً لأن القدرة نسبتها إلى الضدين سواء) عند أبي هاشم والعلم مقدور عنده فيكون الشك عنده أيضاً مقدوراً، فلا نسلم كونه غير مقدور، قال الآمدي : ( والحق أن ابتداء الشك غير مقدور للعبد بل هو واقع بغير اختياره إلا أن يجوز أن يكون النظر عند الجزم بالنقيض للتقوية، فأصاب والبحث الذي أورده الشارح لقوله وأنت تعلم إلخ . قوله : ( والعلم مقدور عنده رد لما في شرح المقاصد من أن العلم غير مقدور عنده، إنما المقدور تحصيله لمباشرة الأسباب فاعتراض المواقف ساقط . قوله : (بل هو واقع بغير اختياره في شرح المقاصد أن تحصيله واستدامته مقدور، بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين ويترك النظر في النسبة، وفيه أن اللازم منه عدم حصول النسبة لا التردد فيه وتجويز الطرفين. قوله : ( وأنت خبير إلخ) يعني أنه إن كان مقصود الآمدي بيان الواقع، فهو حق وإن كان مقصوده دفع الاعتراض عن أبي هاشم فلا ينفع، لأن الشرط عنده ابتداء الشك بمعنى التردد في النسبة . قوله : ( فإن جزمت به كان حاصلاً قيل التقليد غير المعرفة، فلعل الجازم مقلد، فيطلب المعرفة مع انتفاء الشك، وقد نبهت على جوابه فيما سبق. قوله : ( وإن جزمت بنقيضه كان مانعاً) قيل عليه : النظر الآخر للتأييد والتقوية واقع كثير، كما سبق فلعل الجاهل قصد التأييد، فنظر فأصاب والحاصل أن مقدمة الواجب النظر المطلق، لا النظر لأجل تحصيل المعرفة فليتأمل. قوله : ( وأنت تعلم أن انتفاء الجزم إلخ) قد يدفع بأن المراد بالشك هو التردد في إما على أستواء وهو الشك المحض أو رجحان لأحد الجانبين، وهو الظن والوهم قال البيضاوي : في تفسيره الشك قد يطلق على ما يقابل العلم، ولهذا أكد قوله تعالى : ولفي شك منهم في قوله تعالى: وإن الذين اختلفوا فيه لفي شك منه ﴾ [النساء ۱۵۹]، بقوله ما لهم به من علم . قوله : ( فيكون الشك عنده أيضاً مقدوراً) قيل : الشك من الكيفيات النفسانية كالعلم لا من الأفعال الاختيارية فلا يكون النظر في النسبة ويمكن أن يقال : ليس الشك من المعاني التي يطلبها العاقل ويحكم باستحقاق تاركه الذم، وأيضاً إنه دوامه مقدور إذ له أن يترك النظر فيدوم ) الشك ) أو أن ينظر فيزول ) الشك، وأنت خبير بأن ما قاله : لا ينفع أبا هاشم لأن الذي يجب أن يتقدم عنده على القصد إلى النظر، هو ابتداء الشك لا دوامه الثاني وهو الصواب) في الرد عليه أو وجوب المعرفة) عنده (مقيد بالشك) على ما تقتضيه قاعدته لأن الخوف الزكاة لما كان مشروطاً) ومقيداً ( بحصول النصاب لم يكن إيجاباً لتحصيل النصاب ) ولا مستلزماً لإيجاب تحصيله اتفاقاً ( فرع إن قلنا : الواجب ) الأول ( النظر فيمن أمكنه زمان يسع فيه النظر التام والتوصل به إلى معرفة الله تعالى ( ولم ينظر) في ذلك الزمان، ولم يتوصل بلا عذر ( فهو عاص) بلا شبهة ( ومن لم يمكنه زمان (أصلاً) بأن مات حال البلوغ ( فهو كالصبي ) الذي مات في صباه ( ومن أمكنه من الزمان (ما يسع بعض النظر دون تمامه ) فإن شرع فيه بلا تأخير واخترمته المنية قبل انقضاء النظر وحصول المعرفة فلا عصيان قوله : (إن وجوب المعرفة إلخ) في شرح المقاصد أن وجوب النظر مقيد بالشك، فهو لا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق، والحق ما في المتن لأن النظر ليس من الواجبات أولا، وبالذات بل وجوبه لكونه مقدمة للواجب المطلق، وكذا القصد والشك لكونهما المقدمة فالتقييد والإطلاق لا بد من اعتباره في الواجب أولا، وبالذات كما يستفاد من الدليل الذي ذكره الشارح . قوله : (بالشك) أي بالتردد لأن الخوف إنما ينشأ من مطلق التردد الشامل للوهم، والظن أيضاً، وهذا التردد حاصل للمقلد ، وصاحب الجهل المركب ابتداء عند تصور الطرفين والنسبة فقد وجب عليه المعرفة ثم بعد ذلك يقلد أو ينظر نظراً فاسداً يفيد الجهل، فلا يرد ما قيل: إنه يلزم من ذلك أن لا تجب المعرفة عند الظن والوهم والتقليد والجهل المركب مع ظهور بطلانه، بقي أنه يلزم من ذلك أن لا تجب على العاقل الجاهل لعدم تحقق المقدمة أعني التردد، لكن القائلين بوجوب المعرفة عقلاً يدعون الضرورة في حصول الخوف لكل عاقل بعد سماعه الاختلاف ورؤية آثار النعم . وإن كان مقدمة فليس من الأسباب ليكون إيجاب النظر إيجاباً له، بمعنى تعلق خطاب الشرع، إن قلت مراد أبي هاشم هو الوجوب العقلي كالنظر قلت : معنى الوجوب العقلي عندهم أن يحكم العقل بأن تركه سبب للعقاب في حكم الله تعالى سواء ورد الشرع أم لا على ما سيعلم . قوله : ( وأنت خبير إلخ) اعتراض إن كان قول الآمدي والحق توجيهاً لقول أبي هاشم وتحقيق إن كان قوله : اعتراضا على أبي هاشم، وقد يقال : كون أول الشك مقدمة غير لازم بل غير معقول إذ لا بد من مدة بعد أدلة يقع فيها طلب المبادئ وترتيبها حتى يحصل تمام النظر.
The fifth observation - the seventh purpose: They differed concerning the first obligation upon the accountable person. If you conceptualize the two ends of the required, then if you are certain of it, it is attained; and if you are certain of its opposite, it is an impediment. And you know that the absence of certainty does not necessitate doubt, because there may be conjecture (ẓann) of the required or its opposite, so it is permissible to intend speculation (naẓar) to acquire knowledge (ʿilm). (It is reported) the statement of Abū Hāshim (with two aspects: first, that doubt (shakk) is not within power (maqdūr), so it is not obligatory by consensus. (And there is consideration in it: if) doubt (were not within power, then knowledge would also not be within power, because power's relation to opposites is equal) according to Abū Hāshim, and knowledge is within power according to him, so doubt according to him would also be within power, so we do not accept that it is not within power. Al-Āmidī said: (And the truth is that the beginning of doubt is not within power for the servant; rather it occurs without his choice, except that it is possible that speculation when certain of the opposite is for reinforcement, so he was correct.) And the investigation that the commentator presented regarding his statement 'and you know' etc. His statement: (And knowledge is within power according to him) is a refutation of what is in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that knowledge is not within power according to him; rather what is within power is its acquisition by directly engaging causes, so the objection of al-Mawāqif is invalid. His statement: (Rather it occurs without his choice) in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: its acquisition and continuation are within power, by his acquiring the conceptualization of the two ends and leaving speculation concerning the relation; and in it is that what necessarily follows from it is the non-attainment of the relation, not the hesitation in it and the allowance of both ends. His statement: (And you are aware, etc.) means that if al-Āmidī's intention was to state the reality, it is true; and if his intention was to repel the objection against Abū Hāshim, it does not benefit, because the condition according to him is the beginning of doubt in the sense of hesitation concerning the relation. His statement: (For if you are certain of it, it is attained) it was said: taqlīd (blind following) is not knowledge; perhaps the one who is certain is a blind follower, so he seeks knowledge with the absence of doubt; and I have alerted to its answer previously. His statement: (And if you are certain of its opposite, it is an impediment) it was said against it: the other speculation for confirmation and reinforcement occurs often, as previously; perhaps the ignorant one intended confirmation, so he speculated and was correct. And the conclusion is that the prerequisite of the obligatory is absolute speculation, not speculation for the sake of acquiring knowledge; so let it be considered. His statement: (And you know that the absence of certainty, etc.) it may be repelled by that what is meant by doubt is hesitation concerning either on equality – which is pure doubt – or preponderance of one side – which is conjecture (ẓann) and illusion (wahm). Al-Bayḍāwī said in his commentary: doubt may be applied to what is opposite of knowledge; and for this reason He, exalted, emphasized His statement 'and they are in doubt of it' in His statement 'and indeed those who differed about it are in doubt of it' [al-Nisā' 4:159] by His statement 'they have no knowledge of it'. His statement: (So doubt according to him would also be within power) it was said: doubt is among the psychic qualities like knowledge, not among voluntary actions; so none of them are within power at all; so how does Abū Hāshim say with them? And it was answered that the being-within-power of the prerequisite is by ability to acquire it, like purity and possession of the nisāb (minimum threshold), not that it is a voluntary act. And doubt is not among what one can acquire, by his acquiring the conceptualization of the two ends and leaving the speculation concerning the relation. And it can be said: doubt is not among the meanings that the rational person seeks and judges that its abandoner deserves blame. Also, its continuation is within power, for he can leave speculation so doubt remains, or he can speculate so doubt disappears. And you are aware that what he said does not benefit Abū Hāshim, because what must precede according to him the intention to speculate is the beginning of doubt, not its continuation. The second (and it is correct) in refuting him: (or the obligation of knowledge) according to him (is conditional upon doubt) as his principle requires, because the fear that necessitates the obligation of knowledge according to him arises only from the doubt resulting from awareness of people's disagreement about the Maker and from seeing the traces of blessings. And if its obligation is conditional upon doubt according to him, then its being obligatory is not an obligation of doubt nor does it necessitate its obligation, (like the obligation of zakāt when it was conditional) and restricted (upon the possession of nisāb; it was not an obligation to acquire the nisāb) nor does it necessitate its acquisition by agreement. (A branch: If we say: the first obligation is speculation) for one who has time that suffices for complete speculation and reaching thereby knowledge of God, exalted, (and he did not speculate) in that time and did not reach without excuse, (then he is disobedient) without doubt. (And one for whom there is no time at all, such as one who dies upon reaching puberty, (then he is like the child) who died in his childhood. (And one for whom there is time that suffices for some speculation but not its completion, and he began it without delay and death came before the completion of speculation and acquisition of knowledge, then no disobedience. His statement: (Indeed the obligation of knowledge, etc.) in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: the obligation of speculation is conditional upon doubt, so it is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligatory. And the truth is what is in the text, because speculation is not among the obligations primarily and essentially; rather its obligation is because it is a prerequisite for the absolute obligatory. Likewise intention (qaṣd) and doubt (shakk) because they are the prerequisite. So conditioning and absoluteness must be considered in the obligation primarily and essentially, as understood from the proof mentioned by the commentator. His statement: (by doubt) i.e., by hesitation, because fear arises only from absolute hesitation encompassing illusion (wahm) and conjecture (ẓann) as well. And this hesitation is present in the blind follower and the one with compounded ignorance initially when conceptualizing the two ends and the relation; so knowledge becomes obligatory upon him; then after that he follows blindly or speculates a corrupt speculation that yields ignorance; so what was said – that it follows from this that knowledge would not be obligatory in the case of conjecture, illusion, blind following, and compounded ignorance, despite its falsity being apparent – is not admitted. It remains that it follows from this that it would not be obligatory upon the rational ignorant person due to the non-actualization of the prerequisite, namely hesitation. But those who affirm the obligation of knowledge rationally claim that fear necessarily arises for every rational person after hearing the disagreement and seeing the traces of blessings. And if it is a prerequisite, it is not among the causes such that making speculation obligatory is an obligation of it, in the sense of the attachment of the legal address. If you say: Abū Hāshim's meaning is the rational obligation like speculation; I say: the meaning of rational obligation according to them is that the intellect judges that its abandonment is a cause for punishment in the judgment of God, exalted, whether the law comes or not, as will be known. His statement: (And you are aware, etc.) is an objection. If al-Āmidī's statement 'the truth' was a justification of Abū Hāshim's statement and a verification; if his statement was an objection against Abū Hāshim. And it may be said: the fact that the beginning of doubt is a prerequisite is not necessary, rather it is unintelligible, for there must be a period after evidence in which occurs the seeking of principles and their arrangement until the completion of speculation.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered discussion from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif on the first obligation upon the accountable person (al-mukallaf). It begins by stating that if one conceptualizes the two ends of the required (the proposition in question), certainty (jism) of it yields attainment, while certainty of its opposite is an impediment. The absence of certainty does not necessarily entail doubt; it could be conjecture (ẓann). Thus, intending speculation (naẓar) to acquire knowledge is permissible.

The text then reports Abū Hāshim's view that doubt is not within human power (ghayr maqdūr) and therefore cannot be obligatory by consensus. The commentator responds: if doubt were not within power, then knowledge would also be not within power, because power relates equally to opposites (according to Abū Hāshim), and since Abū Hāshim holds knowledge is within power, his position implies doubt is also within power. Al-Āmidī interjects that the 'beginning' of doubt is involuntary, but speculation in the presence of certainty of the opposite is permissible for reinforcement.

Subsequent sections (marked by 'قوله') comment on specific phrases: that knowledge being within power is a refutation of Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid; that the continuation of doubt is within power; that al-Āmidī's statement either clarifies reality or fails to help Abū Hāshim because the relevant condition is the initial doubt, not its persistence. Further comments address objections about blind following (taqlīd) and the possibility of speculation for confirmation, distinguishing between absolute speculation and speculation for the sake of acquiring knowledge.

The discussion then turns to the definition of doubt (shakk), citing al-Bayḍāwī: doubt can mean what is opposite of certainty, including hesitation, pure doubt, conjecture, and illusion. The objection that doubt is a psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya) and not a voluntary action is answered: the 'being within power' of a prerequisite is by ability to acquire it (like ritual purity or possession of nisāb), not by being a direct voluntary act. The continuation of doubt is within power by either leaving or engaging in speculation.

A pivotal conclusion: Abū Hāshim's principle requires that the obligation of knowledge is conditional upon the initial doubt (the 'fear' arising from awareness of disagreement and divine blessings). This conditionality means that obligating knowledge does not obligate doubt itself, analogous to zakāt being conditional on nisāb without obligating its acquisition.

Finally, a branch (far') addresses practical cases: if one has time for complete speculation and neglects it, they are disobedient; if no time (e.g., dies at puberty), they are like a child; if time for partial speculation but death intervenes during the process, no sin. The passage ends by clarifying that the obligation of speculation is conditional on doubt, but the primary obligation is the required itself (knowledge of God), with speculation as a prerequisite. The commentator affirms that conditioning applies to the primary obligatory, not the prerequisite, and that fear arises from absolute hesitation, which applies even to blind followers and the compound ignorant initially, refuting objections that such cases would exempt them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think doubt is always voluntary and thus can be commanded; the passage shows the initial doubt is not within power.
Correction: Al-Āmidī later clarifies that the beginning of doubt is involuntary, but its continuation can be within power.

Checkpoint
According to Abū Hāshim, why is doubt not obligatory?
A) It is not within power.
B) It leads to knowledge.
C) It is a prerequisite for knowledge.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first obligation framework begins with certainty and doubt; Abū Hāshim's claim that doubt is not within power is refuted by the argument that if knowledge is within power, doubt must also be.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you using uncertainty about your religious duties as an excuse to avoid action, or are you actively seeking knowledge? What specific step will you take today to move from conjecture to certainty about an obligation you know you have?
?
Think of a time you felt certain about something required of you, yet failed to act. What inner impediment did you allow to block attainment? How can you remove that impediment now?
?
Abū Hāshim believed doubt was beyond your control, but the argument shows doubt is within your power. What does this mean for the doubts you entertain about your faith or practices? Will you continue to treat them as passive states or take responsibility for seeking clarity?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
7 min
The Nature and Role of Doubt
Al-Amidi examines the nature of doubt and its condition for obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's distinction between initial doubt (involuntary) and its continuation (voluntary), and how knowledge is conditional upon doubt.

Core Idea
Initial doubt is involuntary, but its continuation is within power; knowledge is conditional on doubt.

This lesson dives deeper into the nature of doubt. Al-Āmidī asserts that the beginning of doubt is involuntary, but its continuation can be within one's power. The discussion also clarifies that the obligation of knowledge is conditional upon the presence of initial doubt, as fear arises from doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Focus on al-Āmidī's statement about the truth (al-ḥaqq) and the subsequent commentary.
2
Distinguish initial doubt from continuation
Notice that the beginning of doubt is involuntary, but one can choose to continue or remove it through speculation.
3
Understand the condition for obligation
Knowledge becomes obligatory only when doubt exists; the fear that necessitates knowledge arises from doubt.
4
Examine the analogy with zakāt
Just as zakāt is conditional on possession of nisāb without obligating acquiring the nisāb, knowledge is conditional on doubt without obligating doubt.
5
Reflect on the definition of doubt
Al-Bayḍāwī clarifies that doubt includes hesitation, pure doubt, conjecture, and illusion.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد السابع: اختلف في أول واجب على المكلف إذا تصورت طرفي المطلوب فإن جزمت به كان حاصلاً، وإن جزمت بنقيضه كان مانعاً، وأنت تعلم أن انتفاء الجزم لا يستلزم الشك، لجواز أن يكون هناك ظن بالمطلوب أو بنقيضه، فيجوز القصد إلى النظر لتحصيل العلم (ورد) قول أبي هاشم ( بوجهين الأول أن الشك غير مقدور فلا يكون واجباً إجماعاً ( وفيه نظر إذ لو لم يكن) الشك ( مقدوراً لم يكن العلم أيضاً (مقدوراً لأن القدرة نسبتها إلى الضدين سواء) عند أبي هاشم والعلم مقدور عنده فيكون الشك عنده أيضاً مقدوراً، فلا نسلم كونه غير مقدور، قال الآمدي : ( والحق أن ابتداء الشك غير مقدور للعبد بل هو واقع بغير اختياره إلا أن يجوز أن يكون النظر عند الجزم بالنقيض للتقوية، فأصاب والبحث الذي أورده الشارح لقوله وأنت تعلم إلخ . قوله : ( والعلم مقدور عنده رد لما في شرح المقاصد من أن العلم غير مقدور عنده، إنما المقدور تحصيله لمباشرة الأسباب فاعتراض المواقف ساقط . قوله : (بل هو واقع بغير اختياره في شرح المقاصد أن تحصيله واستدامته مقدور، بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين ويترك النظر في النسبة، وفيه أن اللازم منه عدم حصول النسبة لا التردد فيه وتجويز الطرفين. قوله : ( وأنت خبير إلخ) يعني أنه إن كان مقصود الآمدي بيان الواقع، فهو حق وإن كان مقصوده دفع الاعتراض عن أبي هاشم فلا ينفع، لأن الشرط عنده ابتداء الشك بمعنى التردد في النسبة . قوله : ( فإن جزمت به كان حاصلاً قيل التقليد غير المعرفة، فلعل الجازم مقلد، فيطلب المعرفة مع انتفاء الشك، وقد نبهت على جوابه فيما سبق. قوله : ( وإن جزمت بنقيضه كان مانعاً) قيل عليه : النظر الآخر للتأييد والتقوية واقع كثير، كما سبق فلعل الجاهل قصد التأييد، فنظر فأصاب والحاصل أن مقدمة الواجب النظر المطلق، لا النظر لأجل تحصيل المعرفة فليتأمل. قوله : ( وأنت تعلم أن انتفاء الجزم إلخ) قد يدفع بأن المراد بالشك هو التردد في إما على أستواء وهو الشك المحض أو رجحان لأحد الجانبين، وهو الظن والوهم قال البيضاوي : في تفسيره الشك قد يطلق على ما يقابل العلم، ولهذا أكد قوله تعالى : ولفي شك منهم في قوله تعالى: وإن الذين اختلفوا فيه لفي شك منه ﴾ [النساء ۱۵۹]، بقوله ما لهم به من علم . قوله : ( فيكون الشك عنده أيضاً مقدوراً) قيل : الشك من الكيفيات النفسانية كالعلم لا من الأفعال الاختيارية فلا يكون شيء منها مقدوراً البتة فكيف يقول أبو هاشم بها وأجيب بأن مقدورية المقدمة بالتمكن من تحصيلها كالطهارة، وملك النصاب لا أن يكون فعلاً اختيارياً، والشك ليس مما يتمكن من تحصيله بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين، ويترك النظر في النسبة ويمكن أن يقال : ليس الشك من المعاني التي يطلبها العاقل ويحكم باستحقاق تاركه الذم، وأيضاً إنه دوامه مقدور إذ له أن يترك النظر فيدوم ) الشك ) أو أن ينظر فيزول ) الشك، وأنت خبير بأن ما قاله : لا ينفع أبا هاشم لأن الذي يجب أن يتقدم عنده على القصد إلى النظر، هو ابتداء الشك لا دوامه الثاني وهو الصواب) في الرد عليه أو وجوب المعرفة) عنده (مقيد بالشك) على ما تقتضيه قاعدته لأن الخوف المقتضي لوجوب المعرفة إنما نشأ عنده من الشك الحاصل من الشعور باختلاف الناس في الصانع ومن رؤية آثار النعم، وإذا كان وجوبها مقيدا بوجوب الشك عنده فلا يكون إيجابها إيجاباً له ولا مقتضياً لإيجابه ( كإيجاب الزكاة لما كان مشروطاً) ومقيداً ( بحصول النصاب لم يكن إيجاباً لتحصيل النصاب ) ولا مستلزماً لإيجاب تحصيله اتفاقاً ( فرع إن قلنا : الواجب ) الأول ( النظر فيمن أمكنه زمان يسع فيه النظر التام والتوصل به إلى معرفة الله تعالى ( ولم ينظر) في ذلك الزمان، ولم يتوصل بلا عذر ( فهو عاص) بلا شبهة ( ومن لم يمكنه زمان (أصلاً) بأن مات حال البلوغ ( فهو كالصبي ) الذي مات في صباه ( ومن أمكنه من الزمان (ما يسع بعض النظر دون تمامه ) فإن شرع فيه بلا تأخير واخترمته المنية قبل انقضاء النظر وحصول المعرفة فلا عصيان قوله : (إن وجوب المعرفة إلخ) في شرح المقاصد أن وجوب النظر مقيد بالشك، فهو لا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق، والحق ما في المتن لأن النظر ليس من الواجبات أولا، وبالذات بل وجوبه لكونه مقدمة للواجب المطلق، وكذا القصد والشك لكونهما المقدمة فالتقييد والإطلاق لا بد من اعتباره في الواجب أولا، وبالذات كما يستفاد من الدليل الذي ذكره الشارح . قوله : (بالشك) أي بالتردد لأن الخوف إنما ينشأ من مطلق التردد الشامل للوهم، والظن أيضاً، وهذا التردد حاصل للمقلد ، وصاحب الجهل المركب ابتداء عند تصور الطرفين والنسبة فقد وجب عليه المعرفة ثم بعد ذلك يقلد أو ينظر نظراً فاسداً يفيد الجهل، فلا يرد ما قيل: إنه يلزم من ذلك أن لا تجب المعرفة عند الظن والوهم والتقليد والجهل المركب مع ظهور بطلانه، بقي أنه يلزم من ذلك أن لا تجب على العاقل الجاهل لعدم تحقق المقدمة أعني التردد، لكن القائلين بوجوب المعرفة عقلاً يدعون الضرورة في حصول الخوف لكل عاقل بعد سماعه الاختلاف ورؤية آثار النعم . وإن كان مقدمة فليس من الأسباب ليكون إيجاب النظر إيجاباً له، بمعنى تعلق خطاب الشرع، إن قلت مراد أبي هاشم هو الوجوب العقلي كالنظر قلت : معنى الوجوب العقلي عندهم أن يحكم العقل بأن تركه سبب للعقاب في حكم الله تعالى سواء ورد الشرع أم لا على ما سيعلم . قوله : ( وأنت خبير إلخ) اعتراض إن كان قول الآمدي والحق توجيهاً لقول أبي هاشم وتحقيق إن كان قوله : اعتراضا على أبي هاشم، وقد يقال : كون أول الشك مقدمة غير لازم بل غير معقول إذ لا بد من مدة بعد أدلة يقع فيها طلب المبادئ وترتيبها حتى يحصل تمام النظر.
The fifth observation - the seventh purpose: They differed concerning the first obligation upon the accountable person. If you conceptualize the two ends of the required, then if you are certain of it, it is attained; and if you are certain of its opposite, it is an impediment. And you know that the absence of certainty does not necessitate doubt, because there may be conjecture (ẓann) of the required or its opposite, so it is permissible to intend speculation (naẓar) to acquire knowledge (ʿilm). (It is reported) the statement of Abū Hāshim (with two aspects: first, that doubt (shakk) is not within power (maqdūr), so it is not obligatory by consensus. (And there is consideration in it: if) doubt (were not within power, then knowledge would also not be within power, because power's relation to opposites is equal) according to Abū Hāshim, and knowledge is within power according to him, so doubt according to him would also be within power, so we do not accept that it is not within power. Al-Āmidī said: (And the truth is that the beginning of doubt is not within power for the servant; rather it occurs without his choice, except that it is possible that speculation when certain of the opposite is for reinforcement, so he was correct.) And the investigation that the commentator presented regarding his statement 'and you know' etc. His statement: (And knowledge is within power according to him) is a refutation of what is in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that knowledge is not within power according to him; rather what is within power is its acquisition by directly engaging causes, so the objection of al-Mawāqif is invalid. His statement: (Rather it occurs without his choice) in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: its acquisition and continuation are within power, by his acquiring the conceptualization of the two ends and leaving speculation concerning the relation; and in it is that what necessarily follows from it is the non-attainment of the relation, not the hesitation in it and the allowance of both ends. His statement: (And you are aware, etc.) means that if al-Āmidī's intention was to state the reality, it is true; and if his intention was to repel the objection against Abū Hāshim, it does not benefit, because the condition according to him is the beginning of doubt in the sense of hesitation concerning the relation. His statement: (For if you are certain of it, it is attained) it was said: taqlīd (blind following) is not knowledge; perhaps the one who is certain is a blind follower, so he seeks knowledge with the absence of doubt; and I have alerted to its answer previously. His statement: (And if you are certain of its opposite, it is an impediment) it was said against it: the other speculation for confirmation and reinforcement occurs often, as previously; perhaps the ignorant one intended confirmation, so he speculated and was correct. And the conclusion is that the prerequisite of the obligatory is absolute speculation, not speculation for the sake of acquiring knowledge; so let it be considered. His statement: (And you know that the absence of certainty, etc.) it may be repelled by that what is meant by doubt is hesitation concerning either on equality – which is pure doubt – or preponderance of one side – which is conjecture (ẓann) and illusion (wahm). Al-Bayḍāwī said in his commentary: doubt may be applied to what is opposite of knowledge; and for this reason He, exalted, emphasized His statement 'and they are in doubt of it' in His statement 'and indeed those who differed about it are in doubt of it' [al-Nisā' 4:159] by His statement 'they have no knowledge of it'. His statement: (So doubt according to him would also be within power) it was said: doubt is among the psychic qualities like knowledge, not among voluntary actions; so none of them are within power at all; so how does Abū Hāshim say with them? And it was answered that the being-within-power of the prerequisite is by ability to acquire it, like purity and possession of the nisāb (minimum threshold), not that it is a voluntary act. And doubt is not among what one can acquire, by his acquiring the conceptualization of the two ends and leaving the speculation concerning the relation. And it can be said: doubt is not among the meanings that the rational person seeks and judges that its abandoner deserves blame. Also, its continuation is within power, for he can leave speculation so doubt remains, or he can speculate so doubt disappears. And you are aware that what he said does not benefit Abū Hāshim, because what must precede according to him the intention to speculate is the beginning of doubt, not its continuation. The second (and it is correct) in refuting him: (or the obligation of knowledge) according to him (is conditional upon doubt) as his principle requires, because the fear that necessitates the obligation of knowledge according to him arises only from the doubt resulting from awareness of people's disagreement about the Maker and from seeing the traces of blessings. And if its obligation is conditional upon doubt according to him, then its being obligatory is not an obligation of doubt nor does it necessitate its obligation, (like the obligation of zakāt when it was conditional) and restricted (upon the possession of nisāb; it was not an obligation to acquire the nisāb) nor does it necessitate its acquisition by agreement. (A branch: If we say: the first obligation is speculation) for one who has time that suffices for complete speculation and reaching thereby knowledge of God, exalted, (and he did not speculate) in that time and did not reach without excuse, (then he is disobedient) without doubt. (And one for whom there is no time at all, such as one who dies upon reaching puberty, (then he is like the child) who died in his childhood. (And one for whom there is time that suffices for some speculation but not its completion, and he began it without delay and death came before the completion of speculation and acquisition of knowledge, then no disobedience. His statement: (Indeed the obligation of knowledge, etc.) in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: the obligation of speculation is conditional upon doubt, so it is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligatory. And the truth is what is in the text, because speculation is not among the obligations primarily and essentially; rather its obligation is because it is a prerequisite for the absolute obligatory. Likewise intention (qaṣd) and doubt (shakk) because they are the prerequisite. So conditioning and absoluteness must be considered in the obligation primarily and essentially, as understood from the proof mentioned by the commentator. His statement: (by doubt) i.e., by hesitation, because fear arises only from absolute hesitation encompassing illusion (wahm) and conjecture (ẓann) as well. And this hesitation is present in the blind follower and the one with compounded ignorance initially when conceptualizing the two ends and the relation; so knowledge becomes obligatory upon him; then after that he follows blindly or speculates a corrupt speculation that yields ignorance; so what was said – that it follows from this that knowledge would not be obligatory in the case of conjecture, illusion, blind following, and compounded ignorance, despite its falsity being apparent – is not admitted. It remains that it follows from this that it would not be obligatory upon the rational ignorant person due to the non-actualization of the prerequisite, namely hesitation. But those who affirm the obligation of knowledge rationally claim that fear necessarily arises for every rational person after hearing the disagreement and seeing the traces of blessings. And if it is a prerequisite, it is not among the causes such that making speculation obligatory is an obligation of it, in the sense of the attachment of the legal address. If you say: Abū Hāshim's meaning is the rational obligation like speculation; I say: the meaning of rational obligation according to them is that the intellect judges that its abandonment is a cause for punishment in the judgment of God, exalted, whether the law comes or not, as will be known. His statement: (And you are aware, etc.) is an objection. If al-Āmidī's statement 'the truth' was a justification of Abū Hāshim's statement and a verification; if his statement was an objection against Abū Hāshim. And it may be said: the fact that the beginning of doubt is a prerequisite is not necessary, rather it is unintelligible, for there must be a period after evidence in which occurs the seeking of principles and their arrangement until the completion of speculation.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered discussion from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif on the first obligation upon the accountable person (al-mukallaf). It begins by stating that if one conceptualizes the two ends of the required (the proposition in question), certainty (jism) of it yields attainment, while certainty of its opposite is an impediment. The absence of certainty does not necessarily entail doubt; it could be conjecture (ẓann). Thus, intending speculation (naẓar) to acquire knowledge is permissible.

The text then reports Abū Hāshim's view that doubt is not within human power (ghayr maqdūr) and therefore cannot be obligatory by consensus. The commentator responds: if doubt were not within power, then knowledge would also be not within power, because power relates equally to opposites (according to Abū Hāshim), and since Abū Hāshim holds knowledge is within power, his position implies doubt is also within power. Al-Āmidī interjects that the 'beginning' of doubt is involuntary, but speculation in the presence of certainty of the opposite is permissible for reinforcement.

Subsequent sections (marked by 'قوله') comment on specific phrases: that knowledge being within power is a refutation of Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid; that the continuation of doubt is within power; that al-Āmidī's statement either clarifies reality or fails to help Abū Hāshim because the relevant condition is the initial doubt, not its persistence. Further comments address objections about blind following (taqlīd) and the possibility of speculation for confirmation, distinguishing between absolute speculation and speculation for the sake of acquiring knowledge.

The discussion then turns to the definition of doubt (shakk), citing al-Bayḍāwī: doubt can mean what is opposite of certainty, including hesitation, pure doubt, conjecture, and illusion. The objection that doubt is a psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya) and not a voluntary action is answered: the 'being within power' of a prerequisite is by ability to acquire it (like ritual purity or possession of nisāb), not by being a direct voluntary act. The continuation of doubt is within power by either leaving or engaging in speculation.

A pivotal conclusion: Abū Hāshim's principle requires that the obligation of knowledge is conditional upon the initial doubt (the 'fear' arising from awareness of disagreement and divine blessings). This conditionality means that obligating knowledge does not obligate doubt itself, analogous to zakāt being conditional on nisāb without obligating its acquisition.

Finally, a branch (far') addresses practical cases: if one has time for complete speculation and neglects it, they are disobedient; if no time (e.g., dies at puberty), they are like a child; if time for partial speculation but death intervenes during the process, no sin. The passage ends by clarifying that the obligation of speculation is conditional on doubt, but the primary obligation is the required itself (knowledge of God), with speculation as a prerequisite. The commentator affirms that conditioning applies to the primary obligatory, not the prerequisite, and that fear arises from absolute hesitation, which applies even to blind followers and the compound ignorant initially, refuting objections that such cases would exempt them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that doubt is always voluntary and can be commanded as an obligation.
Correction: The passage shows that initial doubt is involuntary, but its continuation is within power. The obligation of knowledge is conditional on doubt, not an obligation to create doubt.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the obligation of knowledge (maʿrifa) according to Abū Hāshim's principle?
A) The presence of initial doubt (shakk)
B) The ability to perform speculation
C) The absence of certainty
D) The presence of fear

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is obligatory only when initial doubt exists; this condition does not obligate the creation of doubt itself, just as zakāt does not obligate acquiring nisāb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience doubt about fundamental beliefs, do you embrace it as an opportunity to seek knowledge, or do you suppress it out of discomfort? How might this lesson change your response?
?
The passage says that the continuation of doubt is within your power. Are you actively engaging in speculation to resolve your doubts, or are you passively allowing them to persist? What step will you take today to confront one doubt?
?
Knowledge is obligatory only when doubt exists. If you are certain about something, does that mean you are excused from seeking deeper understanding? Examine your heart: are you using 'certainty' as an excuse to avoid the hard work of true knowledge?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
Practical Scenarios of Obligation
Al-Amidi provides practical rulings for the first obligation in different time scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical application of the first obligation in scenarios of time availability and the nature of the obligatory speculation.

Core Idea
Obligation to speculate depends on time; absolute speculation is the prerequisite.

This lesson applies the theoretical discussion to concrete cases. If a person has sufficient time for complete speculation and neglects it, they are disobedient. If no time (e.g., dies at puberty), they are like a child. If only partial time and death intervenes, no sin. The passage also clarifies that the prerequisite for the first obligation is absolute speculation, not speculation for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Focus on the branch (farʿ) that outlines three scenarios.
2
Identify the three scenarios
Case 1: enough time for complete speculation – if neglected, disobedient. Case 2: no time at all – like a child. Case 3: partial time and death during speculation – no sin.
3
Understand the rationale
The obligation is tied to the ability to complete speculation; if impossible due to time, no fault.
4
Examine the nature of the obligatory speculation
The passage emphasizes that the prerequisite (muqaddima) is absolute speculation (al-naẓar al-muṭlaq), not speculation specifically for acquiring knowledge.
This resolves a confusion about what is truly required.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
The first obligation is ultimately about attaining knowledge of God; speculation is a means, but the obligation is not absolute if conditions (like time) are not met.

الآخر للتأييد والتقوية واقع كثير، كما سبق فلعل الجاهل قصد التأييد، فنظر فأصاب والحاصل أن مقدمة الواجب النظر المطلق، لا النظر لأجل تحصيل المعرفة فليتأمل. قوله : ( وأنت تعلم أن انتفاء الجزم إلخ) قد يدفع العاقل الجاهل لعدم تحقق المقدمة أعني التردد، لكن القائلين بوجوب المعرفة عقلاً يدعون الضرورة في حصول الخوف لكل عاقل بعد سماعه الاختلاف ورؤية آثار النعم . وإن كان مقدمة فليس من الأسباب ليكون إيجاب النظر إيجاباً له، بمعنى تعلق خطاب الشرع، إن قلت مراد أبي هاشم هو الوجوب العقلي كالنظر قلت : معنى الوجوب العقلي عندهم أن يحكم العقل بأن تركه سبب للعقاب في حكم الله تعالى سواء ورد الشرع أم لا على ما سيعلم . قوله : ( وأنت خبير إلخ) اعتراض إن كان قول الآمدي والحق توجيهاً لقول أبي هاشم وتحقيق إن كان قوله : اعتراضا على أبي هاشم، وقد يقال : كون أول الشك مقدمة غير لازم بل غير معقول إذ لا بد من مدة بعد أدلة يقع فيها طلب المبادئ وترتيبها حتى يحصل تمام النظر.
The fifth observation - the seventh purpose: They differed concerning the first obligation upon the accountable person. If you conceptualize the two ends of the required, then if you are certain of it, it is attained; and if you are certain of its opposite, it is an impediment. And you know that the absence of certainty does not necessitate doubt, because there may be conjecture (ẓann) of the required or its opposite, so it is permissible to intend speculation (naẓar) to acquire knowledge (ʿilm). (It is reported) the statement of Abū Hāshim (with two aspects: first, that doubt (shakk) is not within power (maqdūr), so it is not obligatory by consensus. (And there is consideration in it: if) doubt (were not within power, then knowledge would also not be within power, because power's relation to opposites is equal) according to Abū Hāshim, and knowledge is within power according to him, so doubt according to him would also be within power, so we do not accept that it is not within power. Al-Āmidī said: (And the truth is that the beginning of doubt is not within power for the servant; rather it occurs without his choice, except that it is possible that speculation when certain of the opposite is for reinforcement, so he was correct.) And the investigation that the commentator presented regarding his statement 'and you know' etc. His statement: (And knowledge is within power according to him) is a refutation of what is in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that knowledge is not within power according to him; rather what is within power is its acquisition by directly engaging causes, so the objection of al-Mawāqif is invalid. His statement: (Rather it occurs without his choice) in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: its acquisition and continuation are within power, by his acquiring the conceptualization of the two ends and leaving speculation concerning the relation; and in it is that what necessarily follows from it is the non-attainment of the relation, not the hesitation in it and the allowance of both ends. His statement: (And you are aware, etc.) means that if al-Āmidī's intention was to state the reality, it is true; and if his intention was to repel the objection against Abū Hāshim, it does not benefit, because the condition according to him is the beginning of doubt in the sense of hesitation concerning the relation. His statement: (For if you are certain of it, it is attained) it was said: taqlīd (blind following) is not knowledge; perhaps the one who is certain is a blind follower, so he seeks knowledge with the absence of doubt; and I have alerted to its answer previously. His statement: (And if you are certain of its opposite, it is an impediment) it was said against it: the other speculation for confirmation and reinforcement occurs often, as previously; perhaps the ignorant one intended confirmation, so he speculated and was correct. And the conclusion is that the prerequisite of the obligatory is absolute speculation, not speculation for the sake of acquiring knowledge; so let it be considered. His statement: (And you know that the absence of certainty, etc.) it may be repelled by that what is meant by doubt is hesitation concerning either on equality – which is pure doubt – or preponderance of one side – which is conjecture (ẓann) and illusion (wahm). Al-Bayḍāwī said in his commentary: doubt may be applied to what is opposite of knowledge; and for this reason He, exalted, emphasized His statement 'and they are in doubt of it' in His statement 'and indeed those who differed about it are in doubt of it' [al-Nisā' 4:159] by His statement 'they have no knowledge of it'. His statement: (So doubt according to him would also be within power) it was said: doubt is among the psychic qualities like knowledge, not among voluntary actions; so none of them are within power at all; so how does Abū Hāshim say with them? And it was answered that the being-within-power of the prerequisite is by ability to acquire it, like purity and possession of the nisāb (minimum threshold), not that it is a voluntary act. And doubt is not among what one can acquire, by his acquiring the conceptualization of the two ends and leaving the speculation concerning the relation. And it can be said: doubt is not among the meanings that the rational person seeks and judges that its abandoner deserves blame. Also, its continuation is within power, for he can leave speculation so doubt remains, or he can speculate so doubt disappears. And you are aware that what he said does not benefit Abū Hāshim, because what must precede according to him the intention to speculate is the beginning of doubt, not its continuation. The second (and it is correct) in refuting him: (or the obligation of knowledge) according to him (is conditional upon doubt) as his principle requires, because the fear that necessitates the obligation of knowledge according to him arises only from the doubt resulting from awareness of people's disagreement about the Maker and from seeing the traces of blessings. And if its obligation is conditional upon doubt according to him, then its being obligatory is not an obligation of doubt nor does it necessitate its obligation, (like the obligation of zakāt when it was conditional) and restricted (upon the possession of nisāb; it was not an obligation to acquire the nisāb) nor does it necessitate its acquisition by agreement. (A branch: If we say: the first obligation is speculation) for one who has time that suffices for complete speculation and reaching thereby knowledge of God, exalted, (and he did not speculate) in that time and did not reach without excuse, (then he is disobedient) without doubt. (And one for whom there is no time at all, such as one who dies upon reaching puberty, (then he is like the child) who died in his childhood. (And one for whom there is time that suffices for some speculation but not its completion, and he began it without delay and death came before the completion of speculation and acquisition of knowledge, then no disobedience. His statement: (Indeed the obligation of knowledge, etc.) in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: the obligation of speculation is conditional upon doubt, so it is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligatory. And the truth is what is in the text, because speculation is not among the obligations primarily and essentially; rather its obligation is because it is a prerequisite for the absolute obligatory. Likewise intention (qaṣd) and doubt (shakk) because they are the prerequisite. So conditioning and absoluteness must be considered in the obligation primarily and essentially, as understood from the proof mentioned by the commentator. His statement: (by doubt) i.e., by hesitation, because fear arises only from absolute hesitation encompassing illusion (wahm) and conjecture (ẓann) as well. And this hesitation is present in the blind follower and the one with compounded ignorance initially when conceptualizing the two ends and the relation; so knowledge becomes obligatory upon him; then after that he follows blindly or speculates a corrupt speculation that yields ignorance; so what was said – that it follows from this that knowledge would not be obligatory in the case of conjecture, illusion, blind following, and compounded ignorance, despite its falsity being apparent – is not admitted. It remains that it follows from this that it would not be obligatory upon the rational ignorant person due to the non-actualization of the prerequisite, namely hesitation. But those who affirm the obligation of knowledge rationally claim that fear necessarily arises for every rational person after hearing the disagreement and seeing the traces of blessings. And if it is a prerequisite, it is not among the causes such that making speculation obligatory is an obligation of it, in the sense of the attachment of the legal address. If you say: Abū Hāshim's meaning is the rational obligation like speculation; I say: the meaning of rational obligation according to them is that the intellect judges that its abandonment is a cause for punishment in the judgment of God, exalted, whether the law comes or not, as will be known. His statement: (And you are aware, etc.) is an objection. If al-Āmidī's statement 'the truth' was a justification of Abū Hāshim's statement and a verification; if his statement was an objection against Abū Hāshim. And it may be said: the fact that the beginning of doubt is a prerequisite is not necessary, rather it is unintelligible, for there must be a period after evidence in which occurs the seeking of principles and their arrangement until the completion of speculation.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered discussion from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif on the first obligation upon the accountable person (al-mukallaf). It begins by stating that if one conceptualizes the two ends of the required (the proposition in question), certainty (jism) of it yields attainment, while certainty of its opposite is an impediment. The absence of certainty does not necessarily entail doubt; it could be conjecture (ẓann). Thus, intending speculation (naẓar) to acquire knowledge is permissible.

The text then reports Abū Hāshim's view that doubt is not within human power (ghayr maqdūr) and therefore cannot be obligatory by consensus. The commentator responds: if doubt were not within power, then knowledge would also be not within power, because power relates equally to opposites (according to Abū Hāshim), and since Abū Hāshim holds knowledge is within power, his position implies doubt is also within power. Al-Āmidī interjects that the 'beginning' of doubt is involuntary, but speculation in the presence of certainty of the opposite is permissible for reinforcement.

Subsequent sections (marked by 'قوله') comment on specific phrases: that knowledge being within power is a refutation of Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid; that the continuation of doubt is within power; that al-Āmidī's statement either clarifies reality or fails to help Abū Hāshim because the relevant condition is the initial doubt, not its persistence. Further comments address objections about blind following (taqlīd) and the possibility of speculation for confirmation, distinguishing between absolute speculation and speculation for the sake of acquiring knowledge.

The discussion then turns to the definition of doubt (shakk), citing al-Bayḍāwī: doubt can mean what is opposite of certainty, including hesitation, pure doubt, conjecture, and illusion. The objection that doubt is a psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya) and not a voluntary action is answered: the 'being within power' of a prerequisite is by ability to acquire it (like ritual purity or possession of nisāb), not by being a direct voluntary act. The continuation of doubt is within power by either leaving or engaging in speculation.

A pivotal conclusion: Abū Hāshim's principle requires that the obligation of knowledge is conditional upon the initial doubt (the 'fear' arising from awareness of disagreement and divine blessings). This conditionality means that obligating knowledge does not obligate doubt itself, analogous to zakāt being conditional on nisāb without obligating its acquisition.

Finally, a branch (far') addresses practical cases: if one has time for complete speculation and neglects it, they are disobedient; if no time (e.g., dies at puberty), they are like a child; if time for partial speculation but death intervenes during the process, no sin. The passage ends by clarifying that the obligation of speculation is conditional on doubt, but the primary obligation is the required itself (knowledge of God), with speculation as a prerequisite. The commentator affirms that conditioning applies to the primary obligatory, not the prerequisite, and that fear arises from absolute hesitation, which applies even to blind followers and the compound ignorant initially, refuting objections that such cases would exempt them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the first obligation is always and unconditionally speculation.
Correction: The passage shows that the obligation is conditional upon time and ability; if time is insufficient or death intervenes, no sin.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a person who has time for complete speculation but does not speculate without excuse?
A) They are like a child.
B) They are disobedient.
C) There is no sin.
D) They must repeat the speculation.

Key Takeaway
The practical application of the first obligation: the duty to speculate is contingent on having sufficient time, and the primary obligation is knowledge, with speculation as its prerequisite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly use the time you have to seek knowledge of Allah, or do you squander it in distractions, knowing that the obligation to speculate is tied to the time you possess?
?
When doubt enters your heart about matters of faith, do you immediately turn to sincere inquiry (naẓar) as required, or do you remain in comfortable ignorance, ignoring the fear that Allah placed within you as a prod?
?
If you were to die tomorrow after only partial progress in seeking certainty, would this passage's assurance comfort you, or would it convict you of neglect because you did not even begin? How urgent is your pursuit of the knowledge of Allah right now?

The First Obligation: Knowledge Conditioned by Doubt
Iji presents the dispute and critiques Abu Hashim's conditionality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central dispute about the first obligation upon the mukallaf—whether it is knowledge or reflection—and the conditionality of the obligation of knowledge upon the presence of doubt.

Core Idea
The first duty is knowledge, but only when doubt arises—reflection is a means, not an end.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a foundational debate in Islamic theology: what is the very first thing a legally responsible person (mukallaf) must do? Is it to know God, or to engage in reasoning? Iji introduces the views, particularly the position of Abu Hashim that knowledge is obligatory only when conditioned by doubt, and then offers a critique. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the starting point of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening dispute
Read the first sentence carefully: Iji states that the first duty is debated. Notice how he presents the possibility of doubt continuing or ceasing.
The doubt here refers to uncertainty about the Creator.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's position
Find where Abu Hashim's view is described: the obligation of knowledge is conditioned by doubt. Understand why he ties it to fear arising from disagreement and blessings.
Abu Hashim's principle is that fear motivates seeking knowledge.
3
Grasp the critique
Iji argues that conditioning knowledge on doubt does not make doubt itself obligatory, using the analogy of zakat. Reflect on why this analogy is used.
Just as zakat is not required until nisab is reached, knowledge is not required until doubt occurs.
