The Fifth Observatory - The Seventh Goal: They differed regarding the first obligatory duty upon the mukallaf. Its continuation is within capacity, for he may leave reflection and thus doubt continues, or he may reflect and thus doubt ceases. And you are expert that what he said does not benefit Abu Hashim because what must precede his intention to reflect is the beginning of doubt, not its continuation. The second (and it is correct) in refuting him: or the obligation of knowledge according to him is conditioned by doubt, as required by his principle, because fear which necessitates the obligation of knowledge only arises in his view from the doubt resulting from awareness of people's disagreement about the Creator and from seeing the effects of blessings. And if its obligation is conditioned by the occurrence of doubt in his view, then its requiring does not require it, nor does it necessitate its requirement, like the obligation of zakat, since it is conditioned and restricted by the attainment of the nisab, it is not an obligation to acquire the nisab, nor does it necessitate the obligation of its acquisition unanimously. (Branch: If we say the first obligatory duty is reflection, then one who could have time sufficient for complete reflection and attaining thereby knowledge of God, and did not reflect in that time, and did not attain it without excuse, is disobedient without doubt. And one who could not have time at all, such as one who died upon reaching puberty, is like a child who died in childhood. And one who could have time for some reflection but not its completion: if he started it without delay and death overtook him before the completion of reflection and attainment of knowledge, there is no disobedience. His statement: (Indeed the obligation of knowledge, etc.) in Sharh al-Maqasid: that the obligation of reflection is conditioned by doubt, so it is not a premise for the absolute obligation. And the truth is what is in the text because reflection is not among the obligations primarily and essentially, rather its obligation is because it is a premise for the absolute obligation, and likewise intention and doubt because they are premises. So conditioning and absoluteness must be considered in the obligation primarily and essentially, as understood from the proof mentioned by the commentator. His statement: (by doubt) i.e., by hesitation, because fear only arises from absolute hesitation which includes illusion and assumption as well. And this hesitation is present in the muqallid and the one with compound ignorance initially when considering the two sides and the relation, so knowledge becomes obligatory upon him, then after that he engages in taqlid or defective reflection that yields ignorance, so it is not refutable that which was said: it follows from that that knowledge is not obligatory in the case of assumption, illusion, taqlid, and compound ignorance, despite the apparent falsity of that. It remains that it follows from that that it is not obligatory upon the intelligent ignorant person due to the lack of the premise, i.e., hesitation. But those who affirm the intellectual obligation of knowledge claim necessity in the occurrence of fear for every intelligent person after hearing the disagreement and seeing the effects of blessings. And if it is a premise, it is not among the causes such that requiring reflection would require it, in the sense of the attachment of the address of the Lawgiver. If you say: Abu Hashim's intended meaning is the intellectual obligation like reflection, I say: the meaning of intellectual obligation according to them is that the intellect judges that its abandonment is a cause for punishment in the judgment of God, whether the Law comes or not, as will be known. His statement: (And you are expert, etc.) is an objection if the saying of al-Amidi and the truth is a justification of Abu Hashim's statement, and a verification if his statement is an objection against Abu Hashim. And it may be said: that the beginning of doubt being a premise is not necessary, rather it is unintelligible, for there must be a period after evidence in which the seeking of principles and their arrangement occurs until complete reflection is attained. As for if he does not start it but delays it without excuse and dies, then there is a probability, but the more apparent is his disobedience due to his negligence in delaying, even if it becomes clear that there was insufficient time for fulfilling the obligation, like a woman who becomes pure in the morning and breaks her fast, then menstruates that day: she is disobedient even if it appears that she could not have completed the fast. And this branch was specified for reflection because it requires time in which the mentioned detail applies, unlike intention. As for knowledge, starting it reverts to starting reflection. And it may be said: in this specification there is an indication that it is the chosen view, for intention to reflect is part of its completion; how, if it were made an independent obligation, one would have to intend to acquire it, and intention would have to be preceded by another intention.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses the dispute over the first obligatory duty upon the mukallaf (the legally responsible person). It begins by stating that the continuation of doubt is within the mukallaf's capacity, as he can either abstain from reflection and remain in doubt, or engage in reflection and remove doubt. The author then critiques Abu Hashim's position: Abu Hashim argued that the first duty is knowledge conditioned by doubt, but the author points out that what should precede the intention to reflect is the beginning of doubt, not its continuation. The correct view is that the obligation of knowledge is indeed conditioned by doubt, according to Abu Hashim's own principle, because the fear that makes knowledge obligatory stems from doubt arising from awareness of differing opinions about the Creator and from seeing the effects of blessings. Since the obligation of knowledge is conditional on the occurrence of doubt, requiring knowledge does not entail requiring doubt itself, just as the obligation of zakat, which is conditional on possessing the nisab, does not obligate acquiring the nisab. The passage then presents a subsidiary issue (far'): if the first duty is reflection, then the ruling on one who has sufficient time for complete reflection but fails to reflect is clear disobedience. If one dies upon reaching puberty without time, he is like a child. If one has time for partial reflection but dies before completing it, he is not disobedient. The author then provides a commentary (qala) explaining that the obligation of reflection is conditioned by doubt, so it cannot be a premise for an absolute obligation. The truth is that reflection is not obligatory per se but only as a means. The term 'doubt' is interpreted as hesitation, which includes both wahm (illusion) and zann (assumption). This hesitation exists in the muqallid (blind follower) and in one with compound ignorance initially, so knowledge becomes obligatory upon them; if they then follow a defective path, they are still accountable. The objection that knowledge would not be required in cases of zann, wahm, taqlid, and compound ignorance is refuted because hesitation covers the first two, and the latter two involve defective reasoning that still requires correct reflection. The commentary also addresses the criticism that the intelligent but ignorant person lacks the premise of doubt, but proponents of intellectual obligation claim that fear arises necessarily in every intelligent person after hearing differences and seeing blessings. Finally, the author discusses the case of delaying reflection: if one delays without excuse and dies, the apparent ruling is disobedience, analogized to a woman who breaks her fast after becoming pure and then menstruates—she is culpable even if the fast becomes impossible later. The specification of reflection is due to its temporal nature, unlike intention. The passage concludes that the intention to reflect is part of the reflection process, so if it were a separate obligation, it would require another intention, leading to infinite regress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reflection itself is the first absolute duty without any condition.
Correction: The correct first duty is knowledge, but it is only obligatory when doubt is present—reflection is a means to that end, not an unconditional first duty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the obligation of knowledge according to Abu Hashim?
A) The ability to reflect
B) The presence of doubt (shakk)
C) Complete certainty
D) Hearing the call of Islam

Key Takeaway
The first duty is knowledge of God, but it is only obligatory when doubt arises; reflection is a means to acquire that knowledge, not an unconditional first obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you actively reflecting on God's existence only when you feel doubt, or do you seek certainty even when you are comfortable? What would change if you treated reflection as a continuous duty rather than a response to doubt?
?
When you learn about God, do you do it because you fear the consequences of ignorance, or because you genuinely desire knowledge? Examine your motivation: is it fear of punishment or love of truth?
?
If the obligation of knowledge is conditioned by doubt, then what excuse do you have for not deepening your understanding when doubts inevitably arise? Will you let them linger, or will you act with urgency to resolve them?

Reflection as the Means: Timing and Exceptions
Iji presents a subsidiary branch on the obligation of reflection and discusses timing, exceptions, and the nature of doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rulings on the obligation of reflection, including who is considered disobedient for delaying it and the scenarios where reflection is not required or excused.

Core Idea
Reflection is obligatory as a means, but its obligation depends on time and capacity; delay without excuse incurs sin.

After establishing that knowledge conditioned on doubt is the first duty, Iji now turns to a subsidiary issue: if we say reflection is the first duty (as some hold), what are the specific rulings? He discusses cases where one has sufficient time, insufficient time, or dies during the process. He also addresses the nature of doubt and the case of the muqallid. This passage clarifies the practical legal implications of the debate on the first obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the branch on reflection
Notice the branching from the previous section. Iji presents three scenarios: one who has full time and fails to reflect, one who has no time at all, and one who has partial time and starts but dies before completion.
This branch assumes the view that reflection is the first duty.
2
Understand the obligation of reflection as a means
The commentary explains that reflection is not obligatory per se but as a means to knowledge. The obligation of reflection is conditioned by doubt. Iji also discusses the meaning of 'doubt' as hesitation.
Hesitation includes both wahm (illusion) and zann (assumption).
3
Analyze the case of delay
The passage discusses someone who delays reflection without excuse and then dies. The apparent ruling is disobedience, even if later it becomes clear that time was insufficient. The analogy of a woman who breaks her fast and then menstruates illustrates this.
The intention to reflect is part of reflection; treating it separately would lead to infinite regress.

فاسداً يفيد الجهل، فلا يرد ما قيل: إنه يلزم من ذلك أن لا تجب المعرفة عند الظن والوهم والتقليد والجهل المركب مع ظهور بطلانه، بقي أنه يلزم من ذلك أن لا تجب على العاقل الجاهل لعدم تحقق المقدمة أعني التردد، لكن القائلين بوجوب المعرفة عقلاً يدعون الضرورة في حصول الخوف لكل عاقل بعد سماعه الاختلاف ورؤية آثار النعم . وإن كان مقدمة فليس من الأسباب ليكون إيجاب النظر إيجاباً له، بمعنى تعلق خطاب الشرع، إن قلت مراد أبي هاشم هو الوجوب العقلي كالنظر قلت : معنى الوجوب العقلي عندهم أن يحكم العقل بأن تركه سبب للعقاب في حكم الله تعالى سواء ورد الشرع أم لا على ما سيعلم . قوله : ( وأنت خبير إلخ) اعتراض إن كان قول الآمدي والحق توجيهاً لقول أبي هاشم وتحقيق إن كان قوله : اعتراضا على أبي هاشم، وقد يقال : كون أول الشك مقدمة غير لازم بل غير معقول إذ لا بد من مدة بعد أدلة يقع فيها طلب المبادئ وترتيبها حتى يحصل تمام النظر. وأما إذا لم يشرع فيه بل أخره بلا عذر ومات ( ففيه احتمال والأظهر عصيانه ) لتقصيره ،بالتأخير وإن تبين عدم اتساع الزمان لتحصيل الواجب ( كالمرأة تصبح طاهرة فتفطر ثم تحيض) في ذلك اليوم ) فإنها عاصية وإن ظهر أنها لم يمكنها إتمام الصوم وإنما خص الفرع بالنظر لاقتضائه زماناً يتأتى فيه التفصيل الذي ذكره بخلاف القصد، وأما المعرفة فالشروع فيها راجع إلى الشروع في النظر، وقد يقال : في هذا التخصيص إيماء إلى أنه المختار فإن القصد إلى النظر من تتمته كيف ولو جعل واجباً برأسه وجب أن يقصد إلى تحصيله، ولزم أن يكون القصد مسبوقاً بقصد آخر.
The Fifth Observatory - The Seventh Goal: They differed regarding the first obligatory duty upon the mukallaf. Its continuation is within capacity, for he may leave reflection and thus doubt continues, or he may reflect and thus doubt ceases. And you are expert that what he said does not benefit Abu Hashim because what must precede his intention to reflect is the beginning of doubt, not its continuation. The second (and it is correct) in refuting him: or the obligation of knowledge according to him is conditioned by doubt, as required by his principle, because fear which necessitates the obligation of knowledge only arises in his view from the doubt resulting from awareness of people's disagreement about the Creator and from seeing the effects of blessings. And if its obligation is conditioned by the occurrence of doubt in his view, then its requiring does not require it, nor does it necessitate its requirement, like the obligation of zakat, since it is conditioned and restricted by the attainment of the nisab, it is not an obligation to acquire the nisab, nor does it necessitate the obligation of its acquisition unanimously. (Branch: If we say the first obligatory duty is reflection, then one who could have time sufficient for complete reflection and attaining thereby knowledge of God, and did not reflect in that time, and did not attain it without excuse, is disobedient without doubt. And one who could not have time at all, such as one who died upon reaching puberty, is like a child who died in childhood. And one who could have time for some reflection but not its completion: if he started it without delay and death overtook him before the completion of reflection and attainment of knowledge, there is no disobedience. His statement: (Indeed the obligation of knowledge, etc.) in Sharh al-Maqasid: that the obligation of reflection is conditioned by doubt, so it is not a premise for the absolute obligation. And the truth is what is in the text because reflection is not among the obligations primarily and essentially, rather its obligation is because it is a premise for the absolute obligation, and likewise intention and doubt because they are premises. So conditioning and absoluteness must be considered in the obligation primarily and essentially, as understood from the proof mentioned by the commentator. His statement: (by doubt) i.e., by hesitation, because fear only arises from absolute hesitation which includes illusion and assumption as well. And this hesitation is present in the muqallid and the one with compound ignorance initially when considering the two sides and the relation, so knowledge becomes obligatory upon him, then after that he engages in taqlid or defective reflection that yields ignorance, so it is not refutable that which was said: it follows from that that knowledge is not obligatory in the case of assumption, illusion, taqlid, and compound ignorance, despite the apparent falsity of that. It remains that it follows from that that it is not obligatory upon the intelligent ignorant person due to the lack of the premise, i.e., hesitation. But those who affirm the intellectual obligation of knowledge claim necessity in the occurrence of fear for every intelligent person after hearing the disagreement and seeing the effects of blessings. And if it is a premise, it is not among the causes such that requiring reflection would require it, in the sense of the attachment of the address of the Lawgiver. If you say: Abu Hashim's intended meaning is the intellectual obligation like reflection, I say: the meaning of intellectual obligation according to them is that the intellect judges that its abandonment is a cause for punishment in the judgment of God, whether the Law comes or not, as will be known. His statement: (And you are expert, etc.) is an objection if the saying of al-Amidi and the truth is a justification of Abu Hashim's statement, and a verification if his statement is an objection against Abu Hashim. And it may be said: that the beginning of doubt being a premise is not necessary, rather it is unintelligible, for there must be a period after evidence in which the seeking of principles and their arrangement occurs until complete reflection is attained. As for if he does not start it but delays it without excuse and dies, then there is a probability, but the more apparent is his disobedience due to his negligence in delaying, even if it becomes clear that there was insufficient time for fulfilling the obligation, like a woman who becomes pure in the morning and breaks her fast, then menstruates that day: she is disobedient even if it appears that she could not have completed the fast. And this branch was specified for reflection because it requires time in which the mentioned detail applies, unlike intention. As for knowledge, starting it reverts to starting reflection. And it may be said: in this specification there is an indication that it is the chosen view, for intention to reflect is part of its completion; how, if it were made an independent obligation, one would have to intend to acquire it, and intention would have to be preceded by another intention.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses the dispute over the first obligatory duty upon the mukallaf (the legally responsible person). It begins by stating that the continuation of doubt is within the mukallaf's capacity, as he can either abstain from reflection and remain in doubt, or engage in reflection and remove doubt. The author then critiques Abu Hashim's position: Abu Hashim argued that the first duty is knowledge conditioned by doubt, but the author points out that what should precede the intention to reflect is the beginning of doubt, not its continuation. The correct view is that the obligation of knowledge is indeed conditioned by doubt, according to Abu Hashim's own principle, because the fear that makes knowledge obligatory stems from doubt arising from awareness of differing opinions about the Creator and from seeing the effects of blessings. Since the obligation of knowledge is conditional on the occurrence of doubt, requiring knowledge does not entail requiring doubt itself, just as the obligation of zakat, which is conditional on possessing the nisab, does not obligate acquiring the nisab. The passage then presents a subsidiary issue (far'): if the first duty is reflection, then the ruling on one who has sufficient time for complete reflection but fails to reflect is clear disobedience. If one dies upon reaching puberty without time, he is like a child. If one has time for partial reflection but dies before completing it, he is not disobedient. The author then provides a commentary (qala) explaining that the obligation of reflection is conditioned by doubt, so it cannot be a premise for an absolute obligation. The truth is that reflection is not obligatory per se but only as a means. The term 'doubt' is interpreted as hesitation, which includes both wahm (illusion) and zann (assumption). This hesitation exists in the muqallid (blind follower) and in one with compound ignorance initially, so knowledge becomes obligatory upon them; if they then follow a defective path, they are still accountable. The objection that knowledge would not be required in cases of zann, wahm, taqlid, and compound ignorance is refuted because hesitation covers the first two, and the latter two involve defective reasoning that still requires correct reflection. The commentary also addresses the criticism that the intelligent but ignorant person lacks the premise of doubt, but proponents of intellectual obligation claim that fear arises necessarily in every intelligent person after hearing differences and seeing blessings. Finally, the author discusses the case of delaying reflection: if one delays without excuse and dies, the apparent ruling is disobedience, analogized to a woman who breaks her fast after becoming pure and then menstruates—she is culpable even if the fast becomes impossible later. The specification of reflection is due to its temporal nature, unlike intention. The passage concludes that the intention to reflect is part of the reflection process, so if it were a separate obligation, it would require another intention, leading to infinite regress.

النظر
الفرع
العصيان
التردد
المقلد
الجهل المركب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if reflection becomes impossible, there is no sin in delaying it.
Correction: The passage shows that delaying without excuse is disobedience even if it later becomes impossible, because the obligation is to start on time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a person who has time for some reflection, starts it without delay, but dies before completing it?
A) Disobedient
B) Not disobedient
C) Like a child
D) Requires expiation

Key Takeaway
Reflection is a time-sensitive means to knowledge; delaying it without excuse is sinful even if completion becomes impossible, and its obligation is conditioned by hesitational doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed seeking knowledge about God, telling yourself 'I will do it later'? What excuse are you hiding behind right now that prevents you from starting serious reflection today?
?
When doubts about your faith arise, do you quickly dismiss them or turn away, or do you engage in reflection to resolve them? The lesson says hesitation triggers an obligation—what hesitation have you been ignoring in your heart?
?
Are you merely following what your family or culture taught you without personal verification? If so, the lesson declares that knowledge is obligatory upon you from the moment of hesitation. Will you take that first step today and begin reflecting on the proofs of God's existence?

Three Madhahib on Faulty Reasoning and Ignorance
Iji enumerates three madhahib on faulty reasoning and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical positions regarding whether faulty reasoning necessarily leads to ignorance.

Core Idea
Not all faulty reasoning leads to ignorance—scholars differ.

Iji presents three madhahib on the relation between faulty reasoning and ignorance. This lesson introduces each position and its implications for theological epistemology, setting the stage for deeper debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage identifying three schools
Locate the enumeration of the three madhahib. Notice how each is introduced and what specific claims they make about faulty reasoning.
The first school is attributed to Imam al-Razi.
2
Understand the first madhhab (absolute entailment)
The first school holds that faulty reasoning absolutely yields ignorance, whether the fault is in matter or form. The proof uses the example of believing the world is eternal and that the eternal is independent of a cause.
Imam al-Razi is said to have chosen this view, though some interpret his proof differently.
3
Understand the second madhhab (no entailment)
The second school, considered correct by the author, holds that faulty reasoning does not entail ignorance at all. This is stated compactly in the passage.
The passage includes glosses that discuss intention and temporal aspects.
4
Explore the third madhhab (tafsil)
The third school distinguishes between faults in matter (material) and faults in form (logical structure). Only faults in matter are claimed to lead to ignorance.
Some argue that even this is clearly false, as shown by the denial of self-evident truths.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the relationship between reasoning, fault, and ignorance matters for theology. These positions affect whether flawed arguments can still produce knowledge.
This connects to the broader issue of epistemic validity in kalam.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : النظر الصحيح يستلزم العلم ] الذين قالوا : النظر الصحيح يستلزم العلم المنظور فيه (فقد اختلفوا في ) النظر ( الفاسد هل يستلزم الجهل ) أي الاعتقاد الذي لا يطابق المنظور فيه ) على مذاهب ) ثلاثة ( أحدها واختاره الإمام الرازي أنه يفيده مطلقاً) سواء كان فساده من جهة مادته أو من جهة صورته ) لأن من اعتقد أن العالم ،قديم، وكل قديم غني عن العلة امتنع أن لا يعتقد أن العالم غني عن العلة ضرورة) وهو جهل : وقد يقال : إن دليله هذا يرشد إلى أن المختار عنده هو المذهب الثالث، أعني التفصيل كيف والقول بأن الفاسد من جهة الصورة يستلزم الجهل ظاهر البطلان ؟ ( وثانيها ) وهو الصواب قوله : (بخلاف القصد فإنه ليس بزماني ولو سلم كونه زمانياً لا يتأتى فيه التفصيل المذكور. قوله : ( وجب أن يقصد إلى تحصيله لأن الواجب برأسه لا يسقط من ذمة المكلف إلا بالنسبة المتعلقة به بالذات فيحتاج إلى قصد آخر. قوله : (لا يطابق المنظور فيه الظاهر لا يطابق الواقع على ما هو المعتبر في مفهوم الجهل المركب كما سيجيء، إلا أنه أقام المنظور فيه مقامه إشارة إلى اتحادهما عند الناظر بناء على أن العاقل لا يطلب خلاف الواقع، وإن كان نظره يؤدي إليه لفساده . قوله : ( امتنع أن لا يعتقد إلخ ولا شك أن هذا الامتناع ناشئ عن الاعتقاد بالمقدمتين على الهيئة المخصوصة لا دخل لخصوصيتهما في ذلك، ففي كل نظر فاسد يعتقد الناظر قوله : (مقيد بالشك) قيل : فيلزم أن لا تجب المعرفة عند الظن والوهم والتقليد والجهل المركب مع ظهور بطلانه أجيب بأن مراده بالشك ما يتناول الأولين على ما أشرنا إليه، والواجب الأخيرين هو النظر في الدليل ووجه دلالته لأن النظر والمعرفة مع الجزم بأحد النقيضين ممتنع، نعم يلزم عدم وجوبها على الغافل الجاهل مع ظهور بطلانه . قوله : (ظاهر (البطلان ظهور بطلانه يؤيد عدم كونه مختار الإمام ولا يدل على أنه ليس مذهباً لأحد كيف وقد اتخذ جماعة إنكار البديهيات بأثرها مذهباً .
The Fifth Observatory - The Eighth Goal: Correct reasoning entails knowledge. Those who said that correct reasoning entails knowledge of the object of reasoning differed regarding faulty reasoning: does it entail ignorance (i.e., a belief that does not correspond to the object of reasoning) according to three schools. One of them, chosen by Imam al-Razi, is that it yields it absolutely, whether its fault is from its matter or from its form, because one who believes that the world is eternal and everything eternal is independent of a cause, it is impossible that he does not believe that the world is independent of a cause necessarily, and that is ignorance. And it may be said that this proof indicates that the chosen view for him is the third school, i.e., the distinction; how can it be said that the faulty from the aspect of form entails ignorance, which is clearly false? The second (and it is correct) is: His statement: (Unlike intention, for it is not temporal, and even if it were conceded to be temporal, the mentioned detail would not apply to it.) His statement: (One must intend to acquire it because an obligation in itself is not removed from the responsibility of the mukallaf except by an act related to it essentially, so it needs another intention.) His statement: (It does not correspond to the object of reasoning; apparently it does not correspond to reality as is considered in the concept of compound ignorance, as will come, except that he placed the object of reasoning in its place to indicate their unity for the viewer, based on the fact that the rational person does not seek what contradicts reality, even if his reasoning leads to it due to its corruption.) His statement: (It is impossible that he does not believe, etc.) And there is no doubt that this impossibility arises from the belief in the two premises in the specific form; the particularity of the premises has no role in that, so in every faulty reasoning, the viewer believes. His statement: (Conditioned by doubt.) It was said: It follows that knowledge is not obligatory in the case of assumption, illusion, taqlid, and compound ignorance, despite the apparent falsity. The answer is that what he means by doubt includes the first two, as we indicated, and the obligation for the latter two is reflection on the proof and its indication, because reflection and knowledge with certainty of one of the two opposites is impossible. Yes, it follows that it is not obligatory upon the heedless ignorant person, despite the apparent falsity. His statement: (The apparent falsity.) Its apparent falsity supports that it is not the chosen view of the Imam, and it does not indicate that it is not a school for anyone; indeed, a group has taken the denial of self-evident truths in their entirety as a school.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth goal regarding the relation between correct reasoning and knowledge. It begins with the statement: 'Correct reasoning entails knowledge.' Then it notes that those who affirm this differed about faulty reasoning: does it entail ignorance (defined as a belief that does not correspond to the object of reasoning)? Three schools are enumerated. The first school, adopted by Imam al-Razi, holds that faulty reasoning absolutely yields ignorance, whether the fault is in the matter (material) or in the form (logical structure). The proof given is that if someone believes the world is eternal and that everything eternal is independent of a cause, he necessarily believes the world is independent of a cause—that is ignorance. However, the author suggests this proof actually indicates that al-Razi's chosen view is the third school (the distinction), because claiming that faulty form alone yields ignorance is clearly false. The second school is stated as 'the correct one' but its content is not elaborated directly; instead, the text includes several glosses. The first gloss contrasts intention (qasd) with reflection, noting intention is not temporal and even if temporal, the detail does not apply. The second gloss explains that if reflection were an independent obligation, one would need another intention to intend it, leading to infinite regress. The third gloss defines ignorance as lack of correspondence to reality, but notes that the object of reasoning is used as a substitute because the viewer seeks reality. The fourth gloss explains that in faulty reasoning, the belief in the premises leads to a necessary conclusion, regardless of the content. The fifth gloss returns to the condition of doubt, arguing that doubt here includes zann and wahm, and for the latter two (taqlid and compound ignorance), the duty is reflection on the proof. The final gloss discusses the apparent falsehood of the third school but notes that some people deny self-evident truths as a valid school.

النظر الفاسد
الجهل
الجهل المركب
مذاهب ثلاثة
الإمام الرازي
التفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All faulty reasoning leads to ignorance.
Correction: Not all scholars agree; some hold it does not, and others distinguish between material and formal faults.

Checkpoint
According to the first madhhab (Imam al-Razi), what is the effect of faulty reasoning?
A) It always leads to knowledge.
B) It absolutely entails ignorance.
C) It only leads to ignorance if the fault is in the matter.
D) It has no effect on belief.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, there are three distinct positions on whether faulty reasoning produces ignorance: absolute entailment, no entailment, and a distinction based on the type of fault.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn al-Razi's proof shows that from false premises, logical deduction forces a false conclusion. Think of a time when you held a strong opinion based on what you assumed were true premises. Did you ever realize later that a premise was false? How does this lesson make you more careful about the foundations of your beliefs?
?
The third madhhab distinguishes between mistakes in your starting assumptions and mistakes in how you connect them. Which type of error do you more commonly fall into when you argue your religious convictions? What will you do to guard against that specific weakness in your reasoning?

Three Positions on Faulty Reasoning and Ignorance
Al-Jurjani enumerates the three positions on whether faulty reasoning entails ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on whether faulty reasoning (al-nazar al-fāsid) necessarily entails ignorance (al-jahl), along with the key arguments for each view.

Core Idea
Faulty reasoning may or may not cause ignorance—scholars split into three camps.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a key debate in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām: does flawed reasoning automatically produce ignorance? The discussion hinges on two factors: the material (mādda) and form (ṣūra) of reasoning. Three positions emerge, with the majority rejecting absolute entailment, while Imam al-Razi affirms it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the first sentence establishes the context, then three positions are introduced.
The third position is only referenced indirectly.
2
Identify the three positions
Identify the three madhāhib: first (Imam al-Razi: absolutely yes), second (majority: absolutely no), third (tafsīl: detail). Note the arguments given for the first and second.
The third is not fully argued here.
3
Understand the concept of 'matter' and 'form'
Understand that faulty reasoning can be faulty in its material (content/premises) or form (logical structure). The first position says both lead to ignorance; the second says neither does.
This classification is central to the debate.
4
Examine the arguments
The first position's argument uses an example (world eternal, eternal independent of cause → world independent of cause is ignorance). The second position's argument: if faulty reasoning caused ignorance, then a truth-seeker following a false argument would become ignorant—but that doesn't happen.
The text hints that al-Razi's proof might actually support the third view.
5
Reflect on the correct position
The text states the second position is 'correct and chosen by the majority'. Understand that this is the favored view in classical Ashʿarī thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن : النظر الصحيح يستلزم العلم الذين قالوا : النظر الصحيح يستلزم العلم المنظور فيه (فقد اختلفوا في ) النظر ( الفاسد هل يستلزم الجهل ) أي الاعتقاد الذي لا يطابق المنظور فيه ) على مذاهب ) ثلاثة ( أحدها واختاره الإمام الرازي أنه يفيده مطلقاً) سواء كان فساده من جهة مادته أو من جهة صورته ) لأن من اعتقد أن العالم ،قديم، وكل قديم غني عن العلة امتنع أن لا يعتقد أن العالم غني عن العلة ضرورة) وهو جهل : وقد يقال : إن دليله هذا يرشد إلى أن المختار عنده هو المذهب الثالث، أعني التفصيل كيف والقول بأن الفاسد من جهة الصورة يستلزم الجهل ظاهر البطلان ؟ ( وثانيها ) وهو الصواب والمختار عند الجمهور ( أنه لا يفيده مطلقاً سواء كان فاسداً مادة أو صورة ( وقد احتج عليه بأنه لو أفاده واستلزمه لكان نظر المحق في شبهة المبطل يفيده الجهل ) وليس الأمر كذلك
The Eighth Purpose: Sound reasoning entails knowledge. Those who said that sound reasoning entails knowledge of the thing reasoned about differed concerning faulty reasoning: does it entail ignorance, i.e., belief that does not correspond to the thing reasoned about? There are three positions. One: chosen by Imam al-Razi, that it yields it absolutely, whether its fault is in its matter or its form, because one who believes that the world is eternal and that every eternal is independent of a cause must necessarily believe that the world is independent of a cause, which is ignorance. And it is said: this proof of his indicates that his chosen view is the third position, namely the detail; how? The statement that the faulty in form entails ignorance is manifestly false. The second: which is correct and chosen by the majority, that it does not yield it absolutely, whether the fault is in matter or form. They argued for it that if it yielded and entailed it, then the reasoning of the person of truth upon the sophistry of the false one would yield him ignorance, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three positions (madhāhib) regarding whether faulty reasoning (al-nazar al-fāsid) entails ignorance (al-jahl). The first position, attributed to Imam al-Razi, holds that faulty reasoning necessarily yields ignorance regardless of whether the fault is in the material (mādda) or form (sūra) of the reasoning. The example given: one who believes the world is eternal and that every eternal is independent of a cause must believe that the world is independent of a cause—this is ignorance. The text notes that this argument might actually support the third position (tafsīl). The second position, held by the majority, is that faulty reasoning does not yield ignorance at all. Their argument: if faulty reasoning entailed ignorance, then a sound reasoner following a faulty opponent's argument would gain ignorance, which is not the case. The third position (al-tafsīl) is mentioned obliquely as 'the third madhhab' without explicit listing here.

Key Terms
النظر الفاسد
الجهل
المادة
الصورة
الإمام الرازي
التفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three positions are equally argued and equally valid.
Correction: The text explicitly states that the second position is 'the correct and chosen by the majority', indicating it is the favored view.

Checkpoint
How many positions are discussed regarding whether faulty reasoning entails ignorance?
A) Two
B) Three
C) One
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The majority of scholars hold that faulty reasoning does not necessarily cause ignorance; only sound reasoning yields knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a weak argument for a belief you hold, do you feel that examining it might weaken your knowledge? How does this lesson’s claim that faulty reasoning does not necessarily cause ignorance challenge your instinct to avoid counterarguments? Will you now engage more openly with opposing views, trusting that your sound reasoning will protect your knowledge?
?
Think of a time you held a false belief because of a flawed argument you accepted. Did you recognize that belief as ignorance (a positive misbelief), or did you simply think you lacked information? This passage defines ignorance as a mismatch with reality. Can you admit that you actively believed something false? What will you do to ensure your future beliefs are based on sound reasoning, even when the argument feels convincing?
?
The majority of scholars hold that faulty reasoning does not produce ignorance. Yet many people today avoid learning logic for fear it will lead them astray. Are you one of them? This lesson suggests that even if your reasoning is faulty, it does not automatically make you ignorant—so why do you avoid rigorous thinking? Will you commit to studying sound reasoning (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) to build knowledge, rather than avoiding reasoning altogether?

Refuting the Objection: Faulty Reasoning and Ignorance
Al-Jurjani refutes an objection and provides a positive proof for the second position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the objection against the second position by demonstrating that the objection's own logic would lead to absurd consequences, and how the verifiers establish that faulty reasoning has no intrinsic relation to ignorance.

Core Idea
The objection fails symmetrically; faulty reasoning has no inherent connection to ignorance.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani addresses a clever objection against the second position (that faulty reasoning does not entail ignorance). The objection tries to show that if faulty reasoning cannot yield knowledge, then by symmetry sound reasoning might also fail. Al-Jurjani dismantles this objection by turning its reasoning back upon itself, and then strengthens the original position with a positive proof from the verifiers. Pay close attention to the symmetrical argument and the distinction between intrinsic and accidental causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the flow. Note the objection and the response structure.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical moves.
2
Identify the objection and the symmetrical answer
Find where the objection is presented and then the response. Notice how Al-Jurjani uses 'if you say... we say...' to contrast the conditions for knowledge and ignorance.
The answer shows that the objection's condition applies equally to both sides.
3
Understand the verifiers' proof
Read the section starting with 'وأثبته المحققون'. Understand why faulty reasoning lacks an inherent aspect of entailment and how it differs from sound reasoning.
Compare with the example from Imam al-Razi mentioned in the passage.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument solidifies the second position and why the symmetry argument is effective in kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب لو صح هذا الاحتجاج (لم يكن) النظر ( الصحيح مفيداً) ومستلزماً ( للعلم وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن غير مفيد له بل كان مفيداً لكان نظر المبطل في حجة المحق يفيده العلم فإن قلت شرط إفادة العلم اعتقاد المقدمات ) المعتبرة في النظر الصحيح ( والمبطل لا يعتقدها ) فلذلك لم يفده العلم قلنا : هو مشترك إذ شرط إفادته ) أي النظر الفاسد ) للجهل اعتقادها ) أي المقدمات المعتبرة فيه والمحق لا يعتقدها فلذلك لم يفده الجهل ) وأثبته ) أي المذهب الثاني وهو عدم الإفادة ( المحققون بأن النظر الفاسد ليس له وجه استلزام للجهل ) أي ليس له في نفس الأمر ما لأجله يستلزمه وإن كان قد يجلبه) اتفاقاً كما في المثال الذي أورده الإمام الرازي ( بيانه أن النظر الصحيح، إنما هو في مقدمات له في نفس الأمر إلى المطلوب ) بالنظر ( نسبة ( مخصوصة ( بسببها يستلزم العلم بالمطلوب ) عند انتفاء أضداد العلم قال الآمدي : إن الدليل المنظور فيه مع المطلوب على صفتين في ذاتيهما لا يتصور معهما الانفكاك بينهما ( وليس للفاسد ذلك ) فإن الشبهة المنظور مقدمتيه يكون مفيداً للجهل، فثبتت الكلية المطلوبة
The answer: if this argument were sound, then sound reasoning would not be beneficial and entailing knowledge; otherwise, i.e., if it were beneficial, then the reasoning of the false one upon the proof of the sound one would yield him knowledge. If you say: the condition for yielding knowledge is believing the premises considered in sound reasoning, and the false one does not believe them, therefore it does not yield him knowledge. We say: it is common, for the condition for its yielding—i.e., of faulty reasoning—of ignorance is believing them, i.e., the premises considered in it, and the sound reasoner does not believe them, therefore it does not yield him ignorance. And the verifiers established the second position—i.e., non-yielding—by that faulty reasoning has no aspect of entailment for ignorance, i.e., it has no thing in itself due to which it entails it, though it may bring it about incidentally, as in the example given by Imam al-Razi. Its explanation: sound reasoning is only in premises that have in themselves a specific relation to the desired conclusion, due to which it entails knowledge of the desired conclusion when the opposites of knowledge are absent. Al-Amidi said: the proof reasoned about and the desired conclusion have two qualities in their essences such that disjunction between them is inconceivable. And faulty reasoning does not have that, for the sophistry whose premises are reasoned about would be beneficial for ignorance, so the required universality is established.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the response (jawāb) to the objection against the second position (that faulty reasoning does not entail ignorance). The objection is answered symmetrically: if the objector says that the condition for sound reasoning to yield knowledge is belief in the premises, and the false reasoner does not believe them, similarly the condition for faulty reasoning to yield ignorance is belief in its premises, which the sound reasoner does not believe. Therefore, the objection fails. The verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) prove the second position by arguing that faulty reasoning lacks an intrinsic relation of entailment to ignorance—it may cause ignorance accidentally due to belief, but not necessarily. Al-Amidi's remark emphasizes that in sound reasoning, the premises and conclusion have an inseparable connection in reality, unlike faulty reasoning.

الجواب
الاعتقال
المقدمات
الاستلزام
الآمدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection shows that the second position is inconsistent, because it seems to prove that both sound and faulty reasoning have similar conditions.
Correction: The answer shows that the objection's logic actually applies symmetrically, so it cancels out and does not refute the second position. Moreover, the verifiers prove that faulty reasoning lacks intrinsic entailment, so the position stands.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does faulty reasoning not necessarily entail ignorance?
A) Because it lacks a real aspect of entailment in itself.
B) Because the premises are always false.
C) Because the reasoner does not believe the premises.
D) Because it only entails ignorance if the reasoner is a believer.

Key Takeaway
Faulty reasoning does not entail ignorance intrinsically; the objection fails due to symmetry, and the second position is established by the verifiers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you dismiss someone's argument as faulty, do you ever consider that your own reasoning might be just as vulnerable to the same flaw? How might this lesson's 'symmetry' principle change the way you argue with others?
?
The passage says faulty reasoning can still cause ignorance accidentally if the person believes its premises. Is there a belief you hold that you have never fully examined, which might be built on faulty reasoning? What would it cost you to honestly investigate it?
?
Sound reasoning has an intrinsic relation to its conclusion—faulty reasoning does not. In your own life, have you ever relied on reasoning that felt convincing but lacked genuine grounding? How can you ensure your most important conclusions are built on sound premises?

Does Correct Reasoning Necessarily Yield Knowledge?
Majority view on correct reasoning with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that correct reasoning does not necessitate knowledge absolutely, and why belief in premises is a necessary condition for reasoning to produce its result.

Core Idea
Correct reasoning requires belief in premises to yield knowledge.

This passage presents the majority view on the relationship between correct reasoning and knowledge, along with a sophisticated objection-response dialectic. It shows that reasoning alone is not sufficient; the reasoner's belief in the premises is crucial. The discussion refines the condition for reasoning to produce knowledge or ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice the dialectical structure: view, objection, response, further refinement.
2
Identify the majority view
Find where the author states the majority position on correct reasoning. What does it say about its benefit?
The majority says correct reasoning does not benefit at all, whether faulty in matter or form.
3
Trace the objection and response
Identify the objection and the response. The objection argues from a hypothetical scenario. The response counters by symmetry.
Both objection and response rely on the condition of belief in premises.
4
Understand the role of belief
Focus on the final exchange: 'If you say... We say...'. This clarifies that belief in premises is the key condition for reasoning to yield knowledge or ignorance.
The response shows that the same condition applies symmetrically to both truth-seeker and sophist.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : النظر الصحيح يستلزم العلم
والمختار عند الجمهور ( أنه لا يفيده مطلقاً سواء كان فاسداً مادة أو صورة ( وقد احتج عليه بأنه لو أفاده واستلزمه لكان نظر المحق في شبهة المبطل يفيده الجهل ) وليس الأمر كذلك (والجواب لو صح هذا الاحتجاج (لم يكن) النظر ( الصحيح مفيداً) ومستلزماً ( للعلم وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن غير مفيد له بل كان مفيداً لكان نظر المبطل في حجة المحق يفيده العلم فإن قلت شرط إفادة العلم اعتقاد المقدمات ) المعتبرة في النظر الصحيح ( والمبطل لا يعتقدها ) فلذلك لم يفده العلم قلنا : هو مشترك إذ شرط إفادته ) أي النظر الفاسد ) للجهل اعتقادها ) أي المقدمات المعتبرة فيه والمحق لا يعتقدها فلذلك لم يفده الجهل
The Fifth Observatory - The Eighth Objective: Correct Reasoning Necessitates Knowledge. And the chosen position according to the majority is that it does not benefit absolutely, whether it is faulty in matter or form. And it was argued against it that if it benefited and necessitated, then the correct reasoning of the truth-seeker in the sophist's fallacy would necessitate ignorance, and that is not the case. The answer: if this argument were valid, then correct reasoning would not be beneficial and necessitating knowledge. Otherwise, i.e., if it were beneficial, then the faulty reasoning of the sophist in the truth-seeker's proof would necessitate knowledge. If you say: the condition for the benefit of knowledge is belief in the premises considered in correct reasoning, and the sophist does not believe them, therefore it did not benefit him knowledge. We say: it is common, for the condition for its benefit, i.e., faulty reasoning, for ignorance is belief in them, i.e., the premises considered in it, and the truth-seeker does not believe them, therefore it did not benefit him ignorance.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the dialectic around the majority view that correct reasoning does not necessitate knowledge in an absolute sense, even if the reasoning is faulty in matter or form. The objection is that if correct reasoning necessarily yields knowledge, then a truth-seeker using a sophist's fallacy would acquire ignorance (since the fallacy is false), which is absurd. The response counters that if the objection held, then correct reasoning would not be beneficial; otherwise, a sophist using a truth-seeker's valid proof would acquire knowledge (which is not the case). The discussion then refines: the sophist does not believe the premises, so his reasoning does not produce knowledge; similarly, the truth-seeker does not believe the premises of a fallacy, so it does not produce ignorance. The key point is that belief in the premises is a necessary condition for reasoning to yield its result, whether knowledge or ignorance. The author thus shows that the objection fails because it ignores the role of belief.

Key Terms
النظر الصحيح
النظر الفاسد
استلزام العلم
اعتقاد المقدمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That correct reasoning always necessitates knowledge regardless of the reasoner's belief in the premises.
Correction: The passage shows that belief in the premises is a necessary condition. Without belief, correct reasoning does not yield knowledge.

Checkpoint

B) The reasoner must believe the premises
C) The conclusion must be true

Key Takeaway
Correct reasoning does not automatically yield knowledge; the reasoner's belief in the premises is a necessary condition for reasoning to produce its effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Faulty Reasoning Does Not Necessitate Ignorance
Investigators' proof that faulty reasoning lacks intrinsic necessitation for ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why faulty reasoning lacks an intrinsic necessitation for ignorance, and why correct reasoning inherently leads to knowledge.

Core Idea
Faulty reasoning has no intrinsic link to ignorance; any connection is accidental.

This passage from the investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) tackles a subtle point: does faulty reasoning inevitably lead to ignorance? The answer is no, not intrinsically. The key distinction lies in the nature of the relation between reasoning and its outcome. Correct reasoning has an essential relation to its conclusion, while faulty reasoning does not. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the full English translation. Notice the technical terms like 'al-istilzam al-dhati' (intrinsic necessitation).
The passage is dense with commentary; focus on the core argument.
2
Identify the two types of reasoning
The passage contrasts correct reasoning (al-nazar al-sahih) with faulty reasoning (al-nazar al-fasid). Note how each relates to its conclusion.
Correct reasoning has an essential relation; faulty does not.
3
Understand the concept of 'intrinsic necessitation'
The investigators assert that faulty reasoning has 'no aspect of necessitation' in reality. Any ignorance it produces is accidental, due to the reasoner's belief.
This contrasts with the common misunderstanding that faulty reasoning always entails ignorance.
4
Reflect on the role of belief
The passage notes that if belief is the foundation, both correct and faulty reasoning can be necessitating when believed. But the difference is that correct reasoning has an intrinsic relation independent of belief.
This answers the objection that both seem to produce results when believed.
5
Consider the refutation of Imam al-Razi's example
The passage responds to al-Razi's example, clarifying that not everyone who uses faulty reasoning believes in its premises. Hence, faulty reasoning does not necessitate ignorance by itself.
This solidifies the claim that the relation is not intrinsic.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأثبته المحققون بأن النظر الفاسد ليس له وجه استلزام للجهل أي ليس له في نفس الأمر ما لأجله يستلزمه وإن كان قد يجلبه اتفاقاً كما في المثال الذي أورده الإمام الرازي بيانه أن النظر الصحيح إنما هو في مقدمات له في نفس الأمر إلى المطلوب بالنظر نسبة مخصوصة بسببها يستلزم العلم بالمطلوب عند انتفاء أضداد العلم قال الآمدي إن الدليل المنظور فيه مع المطلوب على صفتين في ذاتيهما لا يتصور معهما الانفكاك إلخ لكونها صادقة مناسبة للمطلوب قوله قال الآمدي إلخ تمهيد لما سيجيء من أن ما ذكره من التحرير إنما يتأتى على اصطلاح من جعل المفرد دليلا وتعريض للمصنف بأن المناسب لقوله فالنظر الصحيح يوقف على وجه دلالة الدليل أن يقول بدل قوله في مقدمات في دليل قوله وليس للفاسد ذلك أي الحصول في مقدمات لها في نفس الأمر نسبة بسببها يستلزم الجهل بالمطلوب لأن مقدماته إما كاذبة فهي غير متحققة في نفس الأمر فضلاً يكون لها نسبة إلى المطلوب في نفس الأمر وإما صادقة غير مناسبة عن أن فيها ليس لها في نفس الأمر بحسب ذاتها نسبة مخصوصة وصفة ذاتية لأجلها تكون مستلزمة للمطلوب بل استلزامها إياه راجع إلى أن الناظر اعتقد فيها وجود صفة يلزمها المطلوب لأجلها وهو مخطئ فيه ألا ترى أنه إذا ظهر خطؤه في اعتقاد الدلالة لم تبق الدلالة أصلاً فالنظر الصحيح يوقف على وجه دلالة الدليل على وجه المطلوب لرابطة بينهما في نفس الأمر بحسب ذاتيهما الخلاف أعني الجهل المركب بالمطلوب ولا خفاء به بعد التحرير والتوضيح الذي قدمناه وقول الإمام الرازي في المثال الذي أورده من اعتقد هاتين المقدمتين اعتقد تلك النتيجة الجهلية قلنا ما ذكرته حق ولكن ليس وإفادتها العلم مغاير لنفسه من حيث إنه رابطة بين ذاتي الدليل والمدلول وإليه يشير قوله بحسب ذاتيهما عقلاً قوله بحيث لا ينفك عنه عادة أو عق قوله ذاتية أي رابطة متحققة بالنظر إلى ذاته بل له رابطة اعتقادية قوله وهو قول الإمام إلخ بعدما استدل على ما ادعي من عدم إفادته الجهل أجاب عن استدلال الإمام بأن اللازم مما ذكرته أن الاعتقاد بالمقدمتين يستلزم الاعتقاد بالنتيجة الجهلية وهو حق لكنه لا يثبت المدعى وهو استلزام النظر الفاسد الجهل إلا إذا ثبت أن النظر الفاسد يستلزم الاعتقاد بالمقدمتين وليس كذلك إذ ليس كل من أتى بالنظر الفاسد يعتقد حقية المقدمات وتحقق المناسبة وكونه على هيئة الإنتاج حتى يستلزم النظر الاعتقاد بالمقدمتين المستلزم للجهل وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع البحث الذي ذكره الشارح بقوله ولقائل أن يقول إلخ لأنه ما استدل على عدم استلزامه الجهل بأنه لا يستلزم الاعتقاد المستلزم للجهل حتى يرد عليه أنه يجري في النظر الصحيح أيضاً بل استدل على المدعى بعدم تحقق الرابطة الذاتية في النظر الفاسد وتحققه في الصحيح ورد استدلال الإمام بأنه غير تام لعدم التقريب فتدبر
And the investigators affirmed that faulty reasoning has no aspect of necessitation for ignorance, i.e., it has nothing in reality by virtue of which it would necessitate it, although it may bring it about accidentally, as in the example given by Imam al-Razi. Its explanation is that correct reasoning is only about premises that have in reality a specific relation to the desired conclusion by virtue of which it necessitates knowledge of the desired conclusion when the opposites of knowledge are absent. Al-Amidi said: The proof being considered, along with the desired conclusion, is upon two qualities in their essences such that separation between them is inconceivable. And faulty reasoning does not have that, for the fallacy whose two premises are considered is beneficial for ignorance. So the required universality is established, and by this the weakness of what the commentator transmitted by his saying 'it may be said, etc.' is shown, because if the foundation of necessitation is belief, then in a formally faulty [argument], if he believes it to be productive due to the hiddenness of its faultiness from him, it is necessitating like a materially faulty [argument] if its faultiness is hidden from him and he believes its truth, with no difference between them, as is not hidden. So his saying 'apparently false' is refuted by stating that on the supposition of knowledge of its faultiness in form, it is conceded, and on the supposition of lack of knowledge, it is denied. His saying: 'And the answer, etc.' The summary of the answer after considering the question and answer shows that there is no benefit in both without belief, and after belief, it is realized in both, so the statement that correct reasoning is beneficial without faulty is arbitrary. His saying: 'It has no aspect of necessitation, etc.' means that what is meant is necessitation in reality, and faulty reasoning has no aspect of necessitation in it, so there is no necessitation, unlike correct. His saying: 'Although it may bring it about accidentally' due to belief in the aspect of necessitation. His saying: 'It is only about premises, etc.' because they are true and suitable for the desired conclusion. His saying: 'Al-Amidi said, etc.' is a prelude to what will come, that what he mentioned of the exposition is only feasible according to the terminology of one who makes the single [term] a proof, and a criticism of the author that the appropriate [expression] for his saying: 'So correct reasoning stops at the aspect of the indication of the proof' is to say instead of his saying 'in premises' 'in a proof'. His saying: 'And faulty does not have that' i.e., obtaining premises that have in reality a relation by virtue of which it necessitates ignorance of the desired conclusion, because its premises are either false, so they are not realized in reality, let alone having a relation to the desired conclusion in reality, or true but unsuitable. However, in them there is not in reality according to their essence a specific relation and essential quality by virtue of which they are necessitating of the desired conclusion, but rather their necessitation of it goes back to the fact that the reasoner believed in them the existence of a quality that necessitates the desired conclusion, and he is mistaken in that. Do you not see that when his error in belief of indication appears, the indication does not remain at all. So correct reasoning stops at the aspect of indication of the proof upon the aspect of the desired conclusion due to a relation between them in reality according to their essences, so it necessitates knowledge of it and includes it such that it is not separate from it, unlike faulty reasoning with ignorance, because what is subjected to faulty reasoning does not have an essential relation with the opposite of what is reasoned about, so that faulty reasoning would stop at it and necessitate by virtue of it belief in that opposite, namely compound ignorance of the desired conclusion. And there is no hiddenness in it after the exposition and clarification that we have presented. And the saying of Imam al-Razi in the example he gave: Whoever believes these two premises believes that ignorant conclusion. We said: What you mentioned is true, but the matter is not that everyone who comes with faulty reasoning in it, i.e., in that example, believes it likewise, i.e., believes that its premises are true and sound. His saying: 'For the fallacy, etc.' is a proof of the negation of the relation in the manner of al-Amidi. His saying: 'So correct reasoning, etc.' means if the premises in which correct reasoning occurs have a specific relation to the desired conclusion, then correct reasoning stops at the aspect of indication of the proof, which is the single [term], upon the desired conclusion due to inclusion of those premises on that aspect because it is a predicate or subject in them. The relation, as indicated by it, is his saying: 'It stops at the aspect of indication of the proof, etc.' It is not hidden that indication is an aspect, according to the commentator's statement, so that faulty reasoning would stop at it. So either what is meant by 'aspect of indication' is the path of indication of the proof without the conventional meaning, or it is said that the aspect of indication in terms of indication and its benefit of knowledge is different from itself in terms of it being a relation between the essences of the proof and the proven, and to that his saying 'according to their essences' points, rationally. His saying: 'Such that it is not separate from it' customarily or rationally. His saying: 'Essential' i.e., a relation realized in consideration of the essence, rather it has a belief-based relation. His saying: 'And it is the saying of Imam, etc.' After he argued for what is claimed of its not benefitting ignorance, he responded to Imam's argument that the necessary consequence of what you mentioned is that belief in the two premises necessitates belief in the ignorant result, and that is true, but it does not prove the claim, which is the necessitation of faulty reasoning for ignorance, unless it is established that faulty reasoning necessitates belief in the two premises, and that is not the case, for not everyone who comes with faulty reasoning believes the truth of the premises, the realization of suitability, and its being in the form of production, such that the reasoning would necessitate belief in the two premises which necessitate ignorance. And by what we have clarified for you, the refutation of the objection mentioned by the commentator in his saying 'and it may be said, etc.' appears, because he did not argue for its non-necessitation of ignorance by saying that it does not necessitate belief that necessitates ignorance, lest it be objected that it also applies to correct reasoning. Rather, he argued for the claim by the non-realization of the essential relation in faulty reasoning and its realization in correct, and he refuted Imam's argument that it is incomplete due to lack of precision, so reflect.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition by the investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) proving that faulty reasoning does not intrinsically necessitate ignorance, and that correct reasoning does intrinsically necessitate knowledge. The argument begins by asserting that faulty reasoning has no intrinsic aspect of necessitation for ignorance; any ignorance it produces is accidental, due to the reasoner's belief. An example from Imam al-Razi illustrates this. The explanation contrasts correct reasoning, whose premises have an essential relation to the conclusion, with faulty reasoning, whose premises lack such a relation. Al-Amidi's statement reinforces that the proof and conclusion are inseparable in their essences. The commentator then clarifies that if belief is the foundation of necessitation, then both correct and faulty reasoning can necessitate their results when believed, but the difference is that correct reasoning has an intrinsic relation while faulty does not. The passage further discusses the nature of 'aspect of indication' (wajh al-dalala) and the difference between intrinsic and belief-based relations. Finally, it responds to an objection from Imam al-Razi, showing that the claim does not require that all faulty reasoning leads to ignorance, but only that it lacks the intrinsic relation necessary for it. The commentator advises careful consideration of the distinctions made.

النظر الصحيح
الاستلزام الذاتي
الرابطة الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That faulty reasoning always entails ignorance.
Correction: The passage shows that faulty reasoning has no intrinsic relation to ignorance; any connection requires the reasoner's belief in the premises, and not everyone using faulty reasoning believes them.

Checkpoint
According to the investigators, why does faulty reasoning not intrinsically necessitate ignorance?
A) Because it always produces true conclusions
B) Because it has no essential relation to the opposite of the reasoned object
C) Because no one believes in faulty reasoning
D) Because faulty reasoning is identical to correct reasoning

Key Takeaway
Faulty reasoning does not intrinsically necessitate ignorance; it only does so accidentally when the reasoner believes in its faulty premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain about a religious truth, have you ever asked yourself whether your reasoning for it is genuinely correct or just familiar? What if your certainty is built on faulty reasoning you haven't examined?
?
Think of a time you were wrong about an important matter of faith. Was your ignorance because your reasoning was faulty, or because you believed something that seemed logical at the moment? How does this lesson change how you view your own intellectual responsibility?
?
The passage says that ignorance from faulty reasoning only comes when you believe the premises are true. Are there beliefs you hold simply because they feel right, without verifying that they have an essential connection to truth? What would it cost you to admit that your reasoning might be built on sand rather than rock?

Correct vs. Incorrect Consideration in Kalam
Sanusi distinguishes correct and incorrect consideration by their intrinsic relation to the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental distinction between correct and incorrect consideration in kalam, and why only correct consideration necessarily leads to knowledge through an intrinsic relation.

Core Idea
Correct consideration reveals an essential link; incorrect consideration relies on false belief.

This passage from Sanusi establishes a key principle in Islamic epistemology: not every act of reasoning leads to valid knowledge. The author distinguishes between correct consideration (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ), which discloses a real relation between evidence and conclusion, and incorrect consideration, which only appears to do so due to a mistaken belief. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the contrast between the two kinds of consideration.
2
Identify the core distinction
Look for the phrases that describe the intrinsic relation (rābiṭa dhātiyya) in correct consideration and the absence of such a relation in incorrect consideration.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why only correct consideration necessarily leads to knowledge. What does this say about the role of the reasoner's belief?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيها ليس لها في نفس الأمر بحسب ذاتها نسبة مخصوصة وصفة ذاتية لأجلها، تكون مستلزمة للمطلوب بل استلزامها إياه راجع إلى أن الناظر اعتقد فيها وجود صفة يلزمها المطلوب لأجلها، وهو مخطئ فيه ألا ترى أنه إذا ظهر خطؤه في اعتقاد الدلالة لم تبق الدلالة أصلاً فالنظر الصحيح يوقف على وجه دلالة الدليل على وجه المطلوب لرابطة بينهما في نفس الأمر بحسب ذاتيهما فاستلزم العلم به وتضمنه بحيث لا ينفك عنه بخلاف النظر الفاسد مع الجهل إذ ليس لما وقع فيه النظر الفاسد رابطة ذاتية مع خلاف ما عليه المنظور فيه حتى يوقف النظر الفاسد عليها، ويستلزم لأجلها الاعتقاد بذلك الخلاف أعني الجهل المركب بالمطلوب ولا خفاء به بعد التحرير والتوضيح الذي قدمناه
In it [the premises of incorrect consideration] there is not, in reality with respect to its essence, a specific relation and an essential attribute because of which it would be necessarily entailing the sought conclusion. Rather, its entailing it is due to the fact that the considerer believed in it the existence of an attribute that entails the sought conclusion because of it, and he is mistaken in that. Do you not see that when his error in believing the indication becomes apparent, the indication does not remain at all? So correct consideration discloses the aspect of the indication of the evidence (al-dalīl) upon the aspect of the sought conclusion, due to a relation between them in reality with respect to their essences, so it necessitates knowledge of it and comprises it in such a way that it is inseparable from it. In contrast, incorrect consideration [does not necessitate] ignorance, because there is no essential relation between what is considered in incorrect consideration and the opposite of what the object of consideration is actually upon, such that incorrect consideration would disclose it and necessitate because of it belief in that opposite, namely compound ignorance of the sought conclusion. And there is no obscurity in this after the clarification and explanation we presented.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental distinction between correct and incorrect consideration (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ wa al-fāsid) in logic and kalām. It begins by describing the nature of premises that lack an intrinsic link to the conclusion: they do not have an essential relation (nisba makhṣūṣa wa ṣifa dhātiyya) that would make them entail the sought result. When someone falsely believes such a link exists, their belief is erroneous and the apparent indication vanishes once the error is exposed. Correct consideration, by contrast, discloses the aspect of indication from the evidence to the sought conclusion because there is a real, essential relation (rābiṭa) between them in reality (fī nafs al-amr). This relation makes knowledge of the conclusion necessarily follow and is inseparable from it. The passage then contrasts incorrect consideration: it does not necessitate ignorance because there is no essential relation between its premises and the opposite of the truth. If it produced ignorance, it would be via the false belief of the considerer, but the incorrect consideration itself lacks the intrinsic link. The author concludes that this point is clear after the earlier clarification.

Key Terms
النظر الصحيح
النظر الفاسد
الرابطة الذاتية
وجه الدلالة
الجهل المركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any act of reasoning either leads to knowledge or to ignorance.
Correction: Incorrect consideration does not necessarily lead to ignorance—it only appears to due to false belief, but it has no essential relation to either knowledge or ignorance.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between correct and incorrect consideration according to this passage?
A) Correct consideration always uses true premises, while incorrect consideration uses false ones.
B) Correct consideration has an intrinsic relation to its conclusion, while incorrect consideration lacks such a relation.
C) Correct consideration produces certainty, while incorrect consideration produces ignorance.
D) Correct consideration is done by scholars, while incorrect consideration is done by common people.

Key Takeaway
Correct consideration necessitates knowledge because it reveals an intrinsic relation between evidence and conclusion, while incorrect consideration lacks such a relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about your faith that seemed logically sound, only to later realize the reasoning was flawed because there was no real connection between your evidence and conclusion? How did that discovery affect your certainty?
?
Think of a theological conviction you hold strongly. Can you honestly say you have examined the intrinsic relation (رابطة ذاتية) between your evidence and that conclusion, or are you relying on assumptions you've never questioned? What would it take for you to investigate that relation today?
?
This passage shows that when an error in reasoning is exposed, the indication disappears completely. Is there any area of your religious understanding where you fear your confidence might crumble if you scrutinized the reasoning? Are you willing to do that risk now to seek truth?

Why False Premises Don't Always Cause Ignorance
Refutes the claim that false premises necessarily produce ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why incorrect consideration (al-nazar al-fasad) does not necessarily produce ignorance, and how the belief in the premises is the deciding factor.

Core Idea
False premises only cause ignorance if you believe they are true.

Imam al-Razi once gave an example to argue that false premises inevitably lead to ignorance. In this passage, the commentator responds by clarifying the role of belief: the false premises themselves don't cause ignorance unless the thinker actually believes them. This distinction is crucial for understanding how human reasoning can still be sound in form even with false content, and how ignorance is tied to conviction, not just logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify al-Razi's claim and the response
Notice that al-Razi claims that anyone who believes two false premises will believe a false conclusion. The response acknowledges this but makes a key distinction.
The response starts with 'قلنا' (we say).
3
Focus on the key phrase 'ربما لم يعتقد ذلك'
This phrase (perhaps he does not believe that) is the pivot. It shows that the person engaging in incorrect consideration may not actually believe the premises are true.
If the premises are not believed, then no ignorance arises from the consideration itself.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider that incorrect consideration can be a cause of ignorance for some, but it is not a necessary entailment. The decisive factor is belief in the premises.
This protects against the misconception that any flawed argument automatically produces ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الإمام الرازي في المثال الذي أورده من اعتقد هاتين المقدمتين اعتقد تلك النتيجة الجهلية قلنا ما ذكرته حق ولكن ليس الشأن من أتى بالنظر الفاسد فيه أي في ذلك المثال اعتقده كذلك أي اعتقد أن مقدماته حقة صادقة، بل ربما لم يعتقد ذلك، فلا يحصل له الجهل، فلا يكون النظر الفاسد مستلزماً للجهل وإن كان جالباً له لبعضهم بسبب اعتقاده
And the statement of Imam al-Razi regarding the example he gave: 'Whoever believes these two premises believes that ignorant conclusion.' We say: What you mentioned is true, but the matter is that whoever engages in incorrect consideration in it — i.e., in that example — believes it similarly, i.e., he believes that his premises are true and correct. Rather, perhaps he does not believe that, so ignorance does not occur for him. So incorrect consideration is not necessarily entailing ignorance, even if it is a cause of it for some people due to their belief.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Imam al-Razi and its answer. Al-Razi gave an example where someone who believes two false premises will believe a false conclusion — this suggests that incorrect consideration leads to ignorance. The response clarifies: the case al-Razi describes assumes the person believes the premises to be true. But in incorrect consideration, the person might not actually believe the premises are true; they might be merely hypothetical or known to be false. Thus, the incorrect consideration itself does not necessitate ignorance. It may be a cause of ignorance for some who mistakenly believe the premises, but it is not intrinsic to the consideration itself. The key point is that ignorance (al-jahl) arises from the false belief in the premises, not from the formal structure of the consideration.

Key Terms
النظر الفاسد
الجهل
الاعتقاد
المقدمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any invalid argument automatically causes ignorance.
Correction: False premises only cause ignorance if the person actually believes them. Without belief, no ignorance arises from the argument itself.

Checkpoint
Why does incorrect consideration not necessarily entail ignorance according to the response to Imam al-Razi?
A) Because false premises can sometimes lead to true conclusions.
B) Because the person performing the consideration may not believe the premises to be true.
C) Because incorrect consideration is always invalid in form.
D) Because ignorance is a special type of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Incorrect consideration does not automatically produce ignorance; it depends on whether the thinker believes the premises to be true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
