The Role of Belief in Correct Consideration
Objection and answer about the role of belief in consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why correct consideration necessitates actually believing the premises, and why lacking belief renders the act neither correct nor incorrect consideration.

Core Idea
Correct consideration is not just a logical form; it includes the act of affirming premises.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: what if someone goes through the motions of a valid argument but does not actually believe the premises? Does that still count as correct consideration and produce knowledge? The answer clarifies that 'consideration' (naẓar) inherently involves a mental act of affirming the premises, so without belief, there is no consideration at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim: correct consideration does not always lead to knowledge because one might not believe the premises.
Notice the objector uses the term 'correct consideration' (النظر الصحيح).
2
Follow the hypothetical reply
The text imagines a counter-argument: if one does not believe the premises, then there is no correct consideration at all.
This reply already hints that belief is essential to the definition.
3
Analyze the final answer
The answer confirms that without belief, there is neither correct nor incorrect consideration, because both require affirmation (true or false).
The key point: consideration is a mental act, not merely a logical structure.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it shows that the term 'consideration' (naẓar) implies actual psychological engagement with the premises.
This reinforces the link between logic and belief in classical kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : ليس كل من أتى بالنظر الصحيح اعتقد مقدماته حقة، وإذا لم يعتقدها كذلك لم يحصل له بذلك النظر العلم بالمنظور فيه، فلا يكون النظر الصحيح مستلزماً للعلم، فإن قلت: إذا لم يعتقدها لم يكن هناك نظر صحيح لأنه ترتيب علوم تصديقية ولا تصديق علمياً له فيما ذكرته، قلت : إنه إذا لم يعتقد المقدمات لم يكن أيضاً هناك نظر فاسد بحسب مادته لأنه ترتيب تصديقات غير مطابقة وليس له حينئذ تصديق غير مطابق
And someone may say: Not everyone who engages in correct consideration believes his premises to be true. If he does not believe them as such, then he does not obtain through that consideration knowledge of the object of consideration. So correct consideration is not necessarily entailing knowledge. If you say: If he does not believe them, then there is no correct consideration at all, because it is an arrangement of affirmatory sciences (ʿulūm taṣdīqiyya) and he has no affirmatory knowledge (taṣdīq ʿilmī) in what you mentioned. I say: If he does not believe the premises, then there is also no incorrect consideration with respect to its matter, because it would be an arrangement of non-conforming affirmations, and he would have no non-conforming affirmation at that time.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents a new objection and its answer. The objector argues that correct consideration might not produce knowledge if the person does not actually believe the premises; they might be engaging in the argument hypothetically. The answer distinguishes between the formal arrangement and the actual belief. If one does not believe the premises, then it is not truly a case of correct consideration because correct consideration requires affirmatory sciences (ʿulūm taṣdīqiyya) — i.e., affirmations that the premises are true. Likewise, it would not be incorrect consideration either, because that also requires affirmations (though false). Thus, the objection fails: correct consideration as such requires belief, and without belief, there is no act of consideration (naẓar) that can be judged as correct or incorrect. The point is that the term 'consideration' (naẓar) implies a actual mental act of affirming premises.

Key Terms
النظر الصحيح
الاعتقاد
المقدمات
التصديق العلمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correct consideration is purely a logical form; one can perform it without believing the premises and still be engaged in correct consideration.
Correction: Correct consideration includes the mental act of affirming the premises; without belief, the act is not consideration at all.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what happens if someone does not believe the premises of a correct-looking argument?
A) The argument still counts as correct consideration and produces knowledge.
B) The argument is still correct but does not produce knowledge.
C) There is no correct consideration at all because belief is essential.
D) It becomes incorrect consideration.

Key Takeaway
Correct consideration is not merely a logical form; it requires the mental act of affirmatively believing the premises, and without that belief, no consideration (correct or incorrect) takes place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear or recite a proof for the existence of Allah, do you merely follow the logical steps, or does your heart truly affirm each premise? In light of this lesson, reflect: without that inner affirmation, are you truly engaging in correct consideration? What does that mean for the state of your īmān?
?
Are there areas of your 'aqīdah where you have learned the arguments but never genuinely believed the premises in your heart? How can you move from mere intellectual acceptance to heartfelt affirmation? What concrete step will you take today to strengthen that inner belief?
?
This lesson teaches that without belief, your 'nazar' is not even considered correct or incorrect—it is nothing. Does this challenge your understanding of learning? Have you been satisfied with just understanding arguments without letting them penetrate your heart? Repent and resolve to engage with knowledge as true affirmation.
